==PAGE:2==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
The use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in clinical decision-making has the potential to aggravate
current health inequities. Within Machine Learning, numerous recent publications frame this topic as fairness in
Machine Learning, and several studies have attempted to clarify and characterize what constitutes fair and ethical
algorithmic classification in healthcare. However, the usefulness of these fairness definitions is uncertain due to
ethical and technological concerns. In this master’s thesis these fairness definitions or metrics, are investigated to
cover the topic of fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare.
Through the use of literature and domain experts, I identify distinct types of bias and discriminatory practices, that
can harm machine learning applications. By using relevant literature, three overarching concepts of fair machine
learning are defined, described, and discussed. Not only do such mathematical definitions of (un-)fairness promise
to detect fairness, but they also give a machine-readable objective function to which decision-making systems can
adhere. As machine learning involves the distribution of goods, the established fairness metrics are first justified
and proven to conform to distributive justice theories, and then linked principles for allocating scarce medical
intervention. Combined it provides ethical principles and norms that can be used to guide the design of fairness
criteria for algorithmic classification for healthcare. However, by analyzing findings from literature and interviews,
it is clear that fairness metrics do not guarantee fairness. Each fairness metric, that relies upon the target variable
Y , has the potential to exacerbate existing disparities in health. As true labels do not always accurately reflect or
summarize accountability variables and may be tainted by historical injustices, there is no guarantee that fairness
metrics guarantee fairness. To avoid returning to the status quo, continuing existing discrimination, society must
accept the task and risks of adopting a coherent multi-principle framework, to ensure equitable distribution of goods.
To do so, this master’s thesis concludes by establishing a cohesive multi-principle framework for ensuring fair and
responsible AI and Machine Learning for healthcare, which encompasses every relevant moral concept, derived
throughout the project.
I would like to emphasize that algorithmic fairness metrics for healthcare, lack the contextual and causal aware-
ness necessary to reason about the mechanisms underlying health disparities outside of a model, which requires
additional attention. Future research should require developers and stakeholders involved in the development of
predictive models for clinical application, to think critically about the broader socio-technical context surrounding
the use of machine learning in healthcare.

Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
1
Introduction
In healthcare Artificial Intelligence (AI) and Machine Learning are increasingly being deployed, as it holds signifi-
cant potential in terms of detecting, predicting, and diagnosing diseases with greater accuracy compared to human
doctors (Mehta, Pandit, and Shukla, 2019; Chouldechova, 2016). In a Danish context, initiatives are being taken to
integrate more AI-based solutions into the Danish healthcare system, as it is a major component of the Danish Life
Science Strategy 2021, which intends to exploit Danish healthcare data (Regeringen, April 2021).
The inspiration for this project started in October 2020, when I interviewed Leo A. Celi (clinical research director
and principal research scientist director the MIT Laboratory of Computational Physiology). Leo A. Celi, like
many other scientists and academicians, is concerned about the expanding use of artificial intelligence and machine
learning in healthcare due to biases (Chubb and Reed, 2021; Crawford, 2017).
According to Oxford Reference, 2022, biases can be described as the systematic obscuring of outcomes or findings
from the true condition of circumstances, or any of various processes that culminate in systematic obfuscation.
And models and algorithms, like humans, are susceptible to biases that render their conclusions "unfair." A biased
model is one that favors a particular set of people and misrepresents distinct demographic populations, which can
have a severe impact on the conclusions of healthcare algorithms (Hardt, Price, and Srebro, 2016; Crawford, 2017).
Additionally, current discriminatory behaviors can result in biased datasets, which can perpetuate algorithms that
end up duplicating existing social prejudices, creating a negative feedback loop that perpetuates or, worse, amplifies
existing discriminatory practices (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Over the last five years, unfairness in machine learning has gone from virtually unknown to often making headlines.
Significant instances of undesirable bias induced in automated processes are frequently identified, evidenced from
literature research of the topic in Section 2. The concept of algorithm fairness has not only gained traction by
researchers but worldwide in the fields of artificial intelligence and software engineering, but also institutions as
the European Union (Floridi and Cowls, 2019).
However, as of 2022, a number of healthcare algorithms have been shown to be biased, favoring or disfavoring
some patient groups unfairly (Celi, 2021). Consequently, the presence of biases and discriminatory in machine
learning models used in healthcare, fosters the risk of misdiagnosis for certain demographic population groups,
which ultimately could lead to loss of life. Most notably was in 2019, when it was revealed that millions of black
patients had been misdiagnosed as a result of racial imbalances in a health algorithm used to triage patients (Ledford,
2019).
Thus, if algorithms and models are used properly, they have the potential to be beneficial. If misused, the impli-
cations for those affected could be severe. As French cultural theorist Paul Virilio puts it: “When you invent the
ship, you also invent the shipwreck; when you invent the plane you also invent the plane crash; and when you invent
electricity, you invent electrocution... Every technology carries its own negativity, which is invented at the same
time as technical progress.” (Jacobson, 2019).
Instead of reverting to status quo with self-perpetuating biased algorithms, society must face the challenge and
risks associated with developing a cohesive multi-principal framework for fair and responsible AI, and research
should not abandon its data-driven approach, as risk score/assessment (risk scores are the predicted probability of
some target value, e.g. a disease.) instruments are generally more accurate than professional human judgments
(Chouldechova, 2016).
Before (un)fairness in machine learning can be detected it must first be defined. Numerous recent machine learning
studies have sought to articulate and define fairness in an automated decision-making system statistically. Not only
do such mathematical definitions of (un-)fairness promise to detect fairness, but they also give a machine-readable
objective function to which decision-making systems can adhere, to avoid unfair treatment in the first place (Barocas,
Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018). But fairness is an ambiguous concept that can be articulated in a variety of ways to
reflect the unique conditions of the use case or the stakeholders’ ethical perspectives. As a result, there is no clear
consensus in studies about what constitutes true fairness in machine learning (Verma and Rubin, 2018).
As a considerable number of algorithms with reported biases are based on supervised learning techniques, more
specifically classification problems based on thresholding risk scores, this will be the primary focus of this thesis.
(Deo, 2015, Pierson et al., 2021). This is not to imply that AI in healthcare will not be considered; rather, machine
learning will receive the most of the attention. Later chapters will use the terms machine learning and artificial
intelligence interchangeably as the discussion expands.
Page 4 of 136
==PAGE:6==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
This master’s thesis will examine the types of biases that appear in machine learning-based algorithms for health-
care, how fairness in machine learning classification can be defined, which principles should guide machine learning
systems, and how we can ensure fair and responsible AI and machine learning in healthcare in practice.
• Research Question 1: What are the types of biases that appear in machine learning-based algorithms, in
healthcare, what are their sources, and what are their consequences to patients?
• Research Question 2: What is fair and ethical algorithmic classification, and what concepts of fairness in
machine learning exist?
• Research Question 3: How may we elicit people’s perceptions of algorithmic fairness?
• Research Question 4: Which ethical principles and norms can be used to both guide and justify the design
of fairness criteria for algorithmic classification for healthcare?
• Research Question 5: How do to ensure fair and responsible AI and Machine learning for healthcare in
practice?
Figure 1: Thesis architecture (reading guide)
The project is organized into four stages, as illustrated in Figure 1, which serves as the project’s framework. Stage
Page 5 of 136
==PAGE:7==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
1 will elucidate the first and second research questions and provide some context for the fourth and fifth research
questions. These findings will be used in stage 2, where the interviews and questionnaire will provide more infor-
mation about the first research question and serve as a foundation for the third research question. Additionally, it
provides some preliminary insights on the fifth research topic, which will be used in stages 3 and 4. In stage 3, the
findings are analyzed to determine the answers to the fourth research question and to provide guidance for the fifth
research question. These findings are then used to answer the fifth research question in stage 4.
Stage 1:
To answer the first research question, Chapter 3 will elaborate on artificial intelligence and machine learning as well
as their use. The chapter will magnify not only the potential benefits, but also the negative implications associated
with the usage of erroneous and biased healthcare algorithms, and the resulting consequences, across the machine
learning life cycle, from data collection to deployment. This will be exemplified Section 3.4. Additionally, this
chapter will discuss the types of discrimination that might occur as a result of erroneous and biased algorithms in
Section 3.5. Moreover, the chapter will provide quantitative metrics for determining models’ performance across
groups in Section 3.3. The metrics will later be used, when exploring the fairness metrics of Chapter 5. More-
over, Section 3.3 will provide an example of a classification model, which will be used later in Chapter 5 and the
questionnaire in Chapter 6.
As machine learning is the distribution of goods, notions of fairness are vital. Section 4.1 will elaborate on the
theories of distributive justice, and how it can be linked to notions of machine learning. This will provide prelimi-
nary answers to the fourth research question of which ethical principles and norms can be used to guide the design
of fairness criteria for algorithmic classification. Next, the chapter will demonstrate assuring fairness is just one
pillar when ensuring fair and responsible AI and machine learning. Section 4.7 will elaborate on the complicated
ethical principles for the adoption of socially beneficial AI, as proposed by Floridi and Cowls, 2019. Their findings
indicate, that a framework fair and responsible AI and Machine learning for healthcare in practice entails more than
just ensuring notions of algorithmic fairness, which will be used later when addressing the fifth research question
in stage 4.
When knowledge about what can constitute fairness, mathematical fairness criteria for machine learning will be
explored. Mathematical fairness criteria are often grouped into different categories, depending on how they each
formalize algorithmic fairness, where the most commonly used are individual fairness, group fairness, and causality-
based fairness. Section 5 will elaborate on the different proposed sets of fairness criteria, available from literature,
with a strong focus on group fairness. To a large extent, group fairness criteria often fall into one of three distinct cat-
egories Independence, Separation, and Sufficiency. Not only do such mathematical definitions of fairness promise
to detect fairness, but they also give a machine-readable objective function to which decision-making systems can
adhere. This will then provide answers to the second research question.
Each of the fairness metrics has distinct advantages and disadvantages that vary according to the context in which
they are utilized. What could be done, though, was to deploy them all in order to reap the full benefits of the
metrics. However, achieving them simultaneously is impossible, which will be demonstrated through the Impossible
Theorem (not to be confused with Arrow’s Impossibility Theorem), in Section 5.3.6. Moreover, by Unfortunately,
as Section 5.3.5 will demonstrate, not only can adhering to fairness metrics reduce accuracy, but there is growing
concern that, even when fairness metrics are used, there will be difficulties in practice, as true labels do not always
accurately reflect/summarize accountability variables. They are, at best, a representation of the current state of
circumstances — which may be tainted by historical injustices and discrimination. Care must be taken to ensure
that a model is is not susceptible to prejudice and injustice (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021).
As there is no universally accepted definition of fairness in machine learning, and since multiple fairness metrics
can not be attained simultaneously, there is frequently a trade-off between which principle to implement.
Stage 2:
As humans have different sets of moral principles, and diverse cultural preferences and outlooks support varying
viewpoints on fairness, a single quantitative definition or metric that is acceptable to everyone in a given situation,
is highly complex, which underscores the difficulty of resolving this issue (Suresh and Guttag, 2021). As fairness
metrics are very dependent on individuals’ ethical views of fairness, it begs to question of how may we elicit people’s
perceptions of algorithmic fairness?
To address this a questionnaire is conducted in Section 6.1 to uncover individuals’ beliefs towards what principles
of fairness for machine learning from Chapter 5 algorithms should adhere to. This section will thus provide answers
to the third research question.
Page 6 of 136
==PAGE:8==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
The questionnaire is then supplemented with interviews with five domain experts which will provide detailed in-
sights on why injustice and biases occur in the machine learning life cycle, thus expanding the answers to the first
research questions. Moreover, the interviews will provide context for a discussion about which fairness indicators
are most important in specific situations, how disparities may be desirable and if not, then how they can be resolved
and mitigated. Lastly, the interviews will give insights on how to establish fair and responsible AI in healthcare,
providing preliminary answers to the fifth research question. The interviews can be found in Section 6.2.
Stage 3:
Stage 3 will analyze the findings from Stages 1 and 2 to undertake a more in-depth investigation of the fourth
research question, and considerations needed to address the fifth research questions.
As there is no universally accepted moral for fairness in machine learning, the Section 7.1 will firstly analyze how
metrics for fairness machine learning in Chapter 5 can be justified and conform to moral notions of distributive
justice in Chapter 4. This justifies the use of the metrics to assist in the design of fairness criteria for algorithmic
classification for healthcare.
As fairness in machine learning often is concerned with ensuring equal treatment across groups, it is also vital to
determine who should receive treatment. Is it treating people equally, favoring the worst-off, maximizing total ben-
efits, or promoting and rewarding social usefulness? (Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009). These are factors
that can be reflected in an algorithm, and combined with the fairness metrics of Chapter 5 and are therefore crucial
to consider when determining which ethical principles and standards should be used to guide the development of
algorithmic categorization fairness criteria. This will be the subject of Section 7.2.
However, as it is particularly difficult to anticipate the consequences of policies in the distant future, any policy
that imposes a clear burden on the present in exchange for a hypothetical future advantage should be held to a
high standard, which does not only include fairness. (Card and Smith, 2020), By focusing exclusively on fairness
measurements that quantify the objective outcome redistribution, a decision-maker may neglect critical factors
regarding the ethical principles for the adoption of socially beneficial AI in Section 4.7 (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh,
2021). Consequently, 7.3 will examine the consequences of imposing fairness constraints on beneficence and non-
maleficence, as well as the necessary considerations of fairness when imposing a possible framework.
Thus the analysis will analyze how the sources described and reviewed in previous chapters (literature and inter-
views), as well as additional literature address the arising challenges in the distribution of benefits and burdens,
relating results to principles and metrics of fairness for algorithmic classification.
Stage 4:
To avoid reverting to the status quo with biased self-perpetuating algorithms fueling negative feedback loops, society
must face the challenge and risks associated with developing a cohesive multi-principal framework for responsible
AI. To do so, the project concludes with a discussion, which will answer the rising concerns within the subject of
fair machine learning while keeping a focal point on the research question of how do to ensure fair and responsible
AI and Machine learning for healthcare.
The discussion will combine the work of Hond et al., 2022 and M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021, as well findings
from interviews, and analysis from stage 3 to formalize a framework for the responsible use of AI and machine
learning in healthcare, that integrates the notions of socially beneficial AI; beneficence, non-maleficence, autonomy,
justice, and explainability from Section 4.7. Due to the wide recognition and industry-proven use to guide data-
mining efforts, the CRISP-DM model will be adopted in the framework to leverage the benefits of the CRISP-DM
model. The established framework will provide answer to the fifth research question.
1.1
The project’s affiliation with UN Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs)
This project helps identify how AI may contribute towards some of the UN Sustainability Goals, but also how
biases and unfairness in machine learning algorithms for healthcare can potentially hinder the achievement of the
following UN Sustainability Goals (Economic and Development, 2015):
• SDG 3: Ensure healthy lives and promote well-being for all at all ages
• SDG 5: Achieve gender equality and empower all women and girls
• SDG 10: Reduce inequality within and among countries

Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
SDG 3: Ensure healthy lives and promote well-being for all at all ages
AI can assist in achieving this goal, as various advancements in AI present tremendous prospects for providing
better, as well as faster, treatments than have been possible so far.
SDG 5 & 10: Achieve gender equality and empower all women and girls - Reduce inequality within and among
countries
With the advancement of AI and fairness in machine learning, there is a belief that pre-existing biases will be
eliminated, as it will no longer be solely up to the doctors or others to select who should receive care, but they
should be assisted, through an algorithmic decision-making system. Yet, as this project will demonstrate, existing
prejudices frequently persist in algorithms and can be magnified to some extent. Nevertheless, there are a variety
of measures available to address these imbalances and help mitigate any potential discrimination for example based
on gender.
This also relates to SDG 10, which strives to minimize inequality within and between countries by empowering and
supporting social, economic, and political inclusion for all, regardless of age, gender, handicap, race, ethnic origin,
religion, or economic or another status. To comply with this requires the absence of any discrimination within this,
through an algorithmic decision-making system, that adheres to fairness, as algorithms have the ability to be more
accurate than doctors (Mehta, Pandit, and Shukla, 2019; Chouldechova, 2016). The issues raised above with SDG
5 are also relevant, and considerable caution must be exercised while building algorithms, as erroneous algorithms,
for example in the health sector, can result in potential discrimination.
2
Methodology
The chapter presents the theoretical basis of science in fairness in machine learning. Then the methods used are
described, the interviews, and questionnaire, and how they contribute to answering the research questions.
2.1
Literature Review
A review of the current literature was made to provide an overview of artificial intelligence and machine learning
and inherent biases or discriminatory practices, theories of ethics and fairness, fairness in machine learning, and
analysis of how views of theories of fairness are reflected in algorithmic classification. A lot of recent machine
learning research has attempted to mathematically quantify fairness in an automatic decision-making system.
Multiple PupMed searches were constructed in combination with supervisor Henning Boje. A first search aimed to
map the terrain of fairness and biases in machine learning for healthcare, limited to 2010-2022, gave 1773 articles
of relevance (06-02-2022). Another search was constructed aiming at providing relevant literature for fairness
in machine learning for healthcare. This search gave 54 articles of relevance (06-02-2022), and can be found in
Appendix 10.1. Besides this project, I am working with Leo A. Celi on other projects for Fairness in Machine
Learning for Healthcare. From there a more detailed and in-depth PupMed search was constructed together with
librarian Paul Bain, PhD, MLIS from Harvard Medical School’s Countway Library. Together with him, 780 records
were initially found, however, as of (06-02-2022) 889 articles are found from a PubMed Preliminary Search. The
most relevant articles for this project were chosen, in combination and both Henning Boje, and personal selection
which consisted of 20 articles. The articles were chosen for their relevance to this project based on their abstracts
and their potential to bring value to the various sections. This is not to say, that the excluded articles are not
relevant. Rather than that, the articles chosen offered greater value to the project and were therefore chosen. The
exclusion of articles raises foreseen limitations, as it is necessary to acknowledge that in a highly dynamic field such
as algorithmic fairness, articles, that are not included in this master’s thesis, can be just as significant as those that
are.
Additional articles were found adhering to pearl-growing of PupMed search.

The PupMed search also revealed that in the last couple of years the field of Fairness in Machine learning have been

For Google Scholar several search strings were used, however, that was done alone, without a librarian, and not as
systematic as the PupMed searches. The google scholar search aimed at providing knowledge to fairness in machine
learning in general and not directly with a scope on healthcare. This was essential since important discussions on
fairness in machine learning are infrequently contained within healthcare. An example is the Barocas, Hardt, and
Narayanan, 2018. The google scholar strings can be found in Appendix 10.1. Moreover, before his departure,
Martin Mose Bentzen provided relevant literature and books, which were used as well. Also, literature from Leo
A. Celi was provided, together with literature from Henning Boje.
Most of the relevant literature was used are either books, or published articles from different journals, however, the
project is also based on grey literature, and blogs post from different Machine Learning Sites.
Based on literature (mostly peer-reviewed) of experts such as Solon Barocas, Moritz Hardt, Arvind Narayanan, Kate
Crawford, Luciano Floridi, Cynthia Dwork, Nathan Srebro, Emanuel, Ezekiel, Roemer, Dworkin, and Meredith
Whittaker contributed to a thorough mapping of the research questions.
A deliberate choice has been made to include in the body of literature reviewed and used also so-called “grey”
literature, encompassing papers from arXiv, technical reports, and, in a few cases, home pages and blogs. The
arXiv papers have typically been presented at conferences and are working papers, but are rarely peer-reviewed.
The inclusion of non/peer-reviewed sources was deemed necessary since important discussions are not infrequently
contained in these papers. A good example is an influential work in progress “Fairness and Machine Learning” by
Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018.
This paper is heavily influenced by the work of Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018, where the authors are experts
within the field of fairness in machine learning, and they have gathered most of the available literature within their
book. Combined with their work, and pearl growing from their work, helped to map the terrain of the available
literature within the field. The work Chouldechova, 2016; Dwork et al., 2011; Hardt, Price, and Srebro, 2016 also
provided mathematical definitions of fairness which do not only aim at detecting unfairness in automatic decision-
making systems, but also to give a machine-readable objective function to which decision-making systems can
adhere to.
However, the field of research is changing, and researchers have become increasingly focused and interested in the
topic of group fairness criteria, due to its ease of computation and ease of implementation, for example, by altering
a classifier’s threshold. Consequently, the use of group fairness criteria was examined to a greater extent. Group
fairness criteria often fall into one of three distinct categories Independence, Separation, and Sufficiency, which are
inspired by the work of Hardt, Price, and Srebro, 2016, Chouldechova, 2016, and Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan,
2018. Their work addresses methods for defining and eliminating discrimination through the use of machine learn-
ing systems, however many other methods exist as demonstrated However, many other notions of group fairness
exist as demonstrated in Table 1. Most work is related to Hardt, Price, and Srebro, 2016), who proposed the idea
of Separation, and who is also one of the key researchers within the use of Independence, sometimes often refereed
to as Demographic Parity, together with Dwork et al., 2011. Sufficiency or Predictive Parity is based on the work
of Chouldechova, 2016.
However, defining fairness in an algorithm is difficult due to the lack of a universal law of fairness, and recent
research has revealed that many of these notions of fairness are flawed, either because they contradict the system’s
original objective - i.e. they reduce predictive accuracy - because they do not conform to legal or moral notions of
fairness, or because they contradict one another (Yang, Cissé, and Koyejo, 2020; Liu et al., 2019; V. Carey, 2020).
The relevant literature to map the terrain for Fairness in Machine Learning can be seen in the table below.

As machine learning is the distribution of some goods, either treatment, loans, etc., notions of distributive justice
were linked to these concepts of fairness machine learning. To outline the notions of distributive justice, the project
made make use of the Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy, as well as relevant literature from Rawls, 1971; An-
derson, 1999; Mack, 2018; Iserson, 1999; Beauchamp and Childress, 2013; Heidari et al., 2018; Dworkin, 1981;
Ross, 1994.
As there is no universally accepted definition of fairness in machine learning, there is frequently a trade-off between
which statistic to implement, which is dependent on which moral principles to adhere to. To address this matter
a questionnaire was developed aiming to provide insights, inspired by the work of Amalie Broggaard Pauli, 2021.
The questionnaire, together with the work of Leben, 2020 and Binns, 2017 provide insights to the necessary basis
for a discussion of which definition of fairness metrics to adhere to. Moreover Binns, 2018, Leben, 2020 as well as
Heidari et al., 2018 were used to derive justification as to how to fairness metrics conform to moral notions of fair-
ness. However, as the fairness principles were justified, there was still missing an important ethical consideration,
which is the determination of who should receive treatment. Thus Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009 were to
provide additional insights to the analysis.
To cover the topic of an overall framework of AI, the findings Floridi and Cowls, 2019 were used. They studied
a number of the most notable ethical standards for artificial intelligence produced by entities such as IEEE among
others, to identify an overarching framework comprised of five basic ethical AI principles. Four of these are fun-
damental bioethical principles: beneficence, nonmaleficence, autonomy, and justice.
For the discussion, additional litterateur were used, to help address different viewpoints, which were discovered
through a combination of earlier pearl-growing, as well as literature provided by Henning Boje. It builds on earlier
work from Floridi and Cowls, 2019, who enlarge their findings to link political philosophy with notions of fairness.
From Floridi and Cowls, 2019 combined with the work of Hond et al., 2022, and M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021 a
framework was constructed to address how do to ensure fair and responsible AI and Machine learning for healthcare
in practice.
2.2
Questionnaire -Fairness in healthcare algorithms
The questionnaire was titled "Fairness in healthcare algorithms," and sought to uncover respondents’ attitudes to the
fairness of different metrics of algorithms for fair machine learning. The questionnaire can be found in Section 10.2.
The questionnaire was inspired by the work of Amalie Broggaard Pauli, 2021 and Meredith Whittaker, 2021. The
initial idea and the hypothesis for the questionnaire were based on the work of Awad et al., 2018, which demonstrated
that moral choices are not universal, and the attitudes and decisions differ depending on societal and cultural values.
Page 10 of 136
==PAGE:12==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Besides the initial goal of the questionnaire, there was also an underlying hypothesis, that respondents’ attitudes
to the fairness of different metrics of algorithms for fair machine learning, differed across work domain or other
features.
The questionnaire was created in an iterative process, with supervisor Henning Boje and tested in pilot trials by
respondents. Once an initial design was constructed, it was tested by having the respondent go over the question-
naire, without interruption, which allowed for thinking out aloud. Their thoughts and critiques were then recorded
and used to prepare for a new edition of the questionnaire. Four test subjects were used in total. A student from the
University in Copenhagen in the seventh semester of psychology, an employee at the Ministry of Foreign Affairs,
a DTU student in the tenth semester of mechanical engineering, and a consultant at AMCS Group. The test group
was to some sort diversified, in terms of their knowledge of machine learning. The initial testing led to several
findings. Firstly, there was a clear distinction between, was most fair and was the best. The test subjects differed in
what they believed, should be implemented at a hospital, and what they believed was most fair. This initial finding
was then integrated into the questionnaire, which would allow respondents to differ between the two.
Numerous considerations were considered in the development, design, and framing of the questionnaire, mostly
inspired by the work of Spiegelhalter, 2021 and Heard and Rakow, 2021.
Also, the matter of priming is of importance and was taken into consideration when constructing the questionnaire.
For the questionnaire, partial scenarios were used, combined with Likert scales, to uncover respondents’ attitudes
to the fairness of different metrics of algorithms for fair machine learning. The questions were presented as neutral
as possible, without overwhelming positive or negative framing, as positive or negative framing of proportions can
change the emotional impact of respondents, as changing the frame of the question can change the perspective of the
perceived risk. Spiegelhalter, 2021 However, the phenomenon was examined in more depth to investigate whether
there could be other factors that would influence the answers.
Heard and Rakow, 2021 also investigated this phenomenon. The research echoed the findings of Spiegelhalter,
2021 but furthermore extended how framing can affect responses. They found that participants spent time acquir-
ing outcome and probability information equally in an (affect-poor) monetary loss scenario; yet acquired outcome
information more frequently than probability information when examining an (affect-rich) like medical side-effects
scenario. Moreover, they demonstrate that affective responses can influence perceived riskiness like Spiegelhalter,
2021 did, and that manipulating effect influences people’s use of probability information, as participants showed
less sensitivity to probability information for a cancer scenario (affect-rich) compared to that of a car crash scenario
(affect-poor). So in the case of affect-rich scenarios, respondents are less likely to take into account the probabil-
ities, if the consequences are too dire. These findings were essential when constructing the questionnaire, which
is why the questionnaire was changed from initially addressing lung cancer therapy, to addressing organ donation,
where to aim was to reduce the emotional influence of framing on responses. Moreover, Heard and Rakow, 2021
demonstrated that the presentation of the questions also plays a vital part in the interpretation of the questions.
Formatted either graphically or tabular, allows for people to see the patterns associated with the statistical data
more clearly, which can help people grasp “gist” knowledge, which would increase sensitivity to probabilities, thus
counterweight any affect rich scenarios, influence on sensitivity to probabilities. The scenario offered in the ques-
tionnaire was thus presented to some extent in an affect-rich scenario with accompanying graphics, to include the
findings of Heard and Rakow, 2021; Spiegelhalter, 2021. Affect-rich scenarios were chosen, as it was of impor-
tance, that the respondents were focused on the outcomes, as the outcomes reflected the different fairness criteria.
This would help uncover, which of the criteria respondents deemed most fair. However, to remain focused on the
probabilities offered, the scenarios were presented graphically, as Heard and Rakow, 2021 demonstrated that visual
presentation increases sensitivity to probabilities.
The questionnaire was disseminated through an online link. This was accomplished by a process known as snow-
ball sampling, in which the questionnaire was circulated to an existing network of respondents, who subsequently
distributed it to their networks. The questionnaire, on the other hand, remained somewhat focused, emphasizing
that it should be mostly distributed only to individuals with at least some prior expertise in machine learning, to
avoid inadequate answers, and avoid information overload for non-technical respondents. This was done based on
the questionnaire’s early tests, which demonstrated that those without prior knowledge of machine learning had
a harder time absorbing the concepts, which could result in incorrect responses. To address this matter further,
respondents were asked how well they understood the fairness criteria, to see if there was a link between those who
struggled to comprehend the questionnaire and their responses, as opposed to those who did, as this was a tendency
found by Amalie Broggaard Pauli, 2021.
Considerations of Spiegelhalter, 2021 and Heard and Rakow, 2021, was thoroughly considered in the development
Page 11 of 136
==PAGE:13==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
of the questionnaire. Their recommendations provide a basis for a suitable and useful questionnaire, which can be
used to uncover respondents’ attitudes to the fairness of different metrics of algorithms for fair machine learning.
However, concerns regarding the validness are still present, due to the framing of the questions. This raises concerns
if answers are biased on emotional responses. However, the combination of de-emphasizing the emotional influence
of framing on responses, combined with the knowledge of graphical presentation, the hope is, that it would increase
the validness of the questionnaire.
From the questionnaire a total of 13 responses were gathered, which also highlights another limitation of the project,
however, it was useful to provide preliminary insights, which could then be used to guide the interviews.
2.3
Qualitative Interviews
To further expand the sources of the report, a short selection of expert views and perceptions were gathered in terms
of qualitative data collected through interviews. The selection of experts could not be made in a systematic and
rigorous way due to time pressure and pandemic restrictions, and may therefore be characterized as a convenience
sample, which is one of the foreseen constraints of this project.
The interviews were conducted using my network within the healthcare system in Denmark and the United States of
America, the private sector, and from researchers within this field, to provide a comprehensive overview of fairness
in the full machine learning life cycle, and to concretize and elevate the report from the purely theoretical.
The interview questions were developed in response to the literature review. The literature review revealed some
of the most common types of biases, how to detect biases, and the various fairness metrics used in the literature.
In the interview guide, questions regarding findings from previous studies, as well the significance of key findings
from the questionnaire were included. The aim of the interviews was to provide insights and give an impression of
why biases arise, and how they can be mitigated. The interviews were consequently structured in a semi-structured
manner. However interviews were conducted with an openness that ensured the possibility of new discoveries and
for the exploration of areas, which were uncovered during interviews (Adams, 2015). I used my already-developed
sub-question as an interviewer to deepen the question and thereby assisting and guiding participants. As a result,
my responses became short and concise. The method’s free format was both an advantage and a disadvantage, as
it did not guarantee that all questions are adequately addressed. However, it should be noted that the components
that are not answered discussed by the interviewees can be just as significant as those that are. (Adams, 2015).
The interviews were recorded and transcribed. Participants were told before the start of the interview, that it would
be recorded, and it was only for personal use. The interviews can be found in the Appendix 10.3. The interviews
were done through either Microsoft Teams or Zoom which were then recorded and transcribed. These are available
in the Appendix 10.3. The benefits of a semi-structured interview were that it allowed for freedom of expression,
allowing the debate to diverge and focus on what the participants feel relevant in regard to the topic, while also
eliciting new perspectives that the interviewer (I) may not have considered. Thus, the format allowed (Adams,
2015).
Interviews with Leo A. Celi AI research at MIT, Louis Lind Plesner and Christian Hedeager of Radiologic Artifi-
cial Intelligence Testcenter of Herlev Hospital, Kristoffer Gordon, Senior Data Scientist at 2021.AI, and lastly an
interview with Sune Hannibal Holm, was conducted to get an insight of his with Fair Treatment with AI.
The interviews significantly enhanced the report’s complexity and made it appear less theoretical, while also pro-
viding insight into the research questions. The interviews can be found in Appendix 10.3
The literature review, interviews, and questionnaire were then merged and worked as the foundation for the project,
which allowed for addressing the research questions. The literature review combined with interviews was used to
unfold to first two research questions and to some extent the fourth and fifth research questions, whereas the ques-
tionnaire served as a basis for the third. Lastly, a combination of the findings, from both litterateur and interviews
was used to establish needed answers for addressing the fourth and fifth research questions.

3
Artificial intelligence in health care: risks and remedies
The field of Artificial Intelligence (AI) and Machine Learning is vast and is not an easy concept to define, and they
hold immense promise, but also associated risks which cannot be neglected.
Even though AI systems, are far from reaching the levels of human intelligence and behavior, there is an increased
interest in the impact of intelligent systems, particularly in the healthcare sector, and some scholars have suggested
that when it comes to minorities, an ’impartial’ machine may be fairer than a person in their judgments (cho;
Mehta, Pandit, and Shukla, 2019). One widely used definition of AI given by McCarthy, Minsky, Rochester, and
Shannon in the seminal ’Proposal for the Dartmouth Summer Research Project on Artificial Intelligence’, the 1955
document that launched the field of artificial intelligence defines AI as: “For the present purpose, the artificial
intelligence problem is taken to be that of making a machine behave in ways that would be called intelligent if a
human were so behaving" (Dignum, 2019). The idea is under the expectation that AI possesses intelligence and
behavior comparable to that of humans.
Often domain experts distinguish between strong and weak AI. The AI implemented in games such as chess is cat-
egorized as weak AI, hence its limited functionality. Other examples of weak AI include Siri, Alexa, and Google
search (Dignum, 2019). Opposite to weak AI is strong AI or general AI, which is when computers achieve a level
of consciousness and decision-making that is comparable to (or superior to) that of humans, however at this point
is only theoretical (Dignum, 2019). Human intelligence is complex, with cognitive, emotional, and social com-
ponents, and current AI systems are only capable of matching human intelligence in extremely confined domains,
such as chess or Go. Even though AI systems, are far from reaching the levels of human intelligence and behavior,
there is an increased interest in the impact of intelligent robots on human dignity, particularly in the healthcare and
legal sectors (Dignum, 2019).
The underlying issue is whether machines are capable of fully respecting human dignity in their judgments and
displays of empathy (Dignum, 2019). If machines take the place of humans in situations requiring empathy, people
may feel alienated, undervalued, and frustrated. Joseph Weizenbaum suggested in 1976 that artificial intelligence
should not be utilized to replace people in roles requiring respect and care. (Dignum, 2019).
Building intelligent systems entail a variety of tasks, including language comprehension, problem-solving, plan-
ning, pattern recognition, communication, and learning. Different fields of research are defined by the methods
they use to accomplish these objectives, and Figure 2 attempts to provide an overview of the different applications
of AI.
Figure 2: Applications of AI by Dignum, 2019
However, before going any further the notion of bias is addressed. According to Oxford Reference, 2022, bias can
be described as the systematic obscuring of outcomes or findings from the true condition of circumstances, or any
of various processes that culminate in systematic obfuscation. While these prejudices may emerge in public health
sciences, for example, epidemiology, as a result of a conflict of interest, they are frequently the result of one of several
other circumstances. Although epidemiologists have identified several distinct types of bias, numerous of these are
Page 13 of 136
==PAGE:15==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
variants of fundamental errors in design, methodology, procedures, or logical reasoning, with the most prevalent
types being selection bias, observer bias, among others. Investigators exercise extreme caution when developing
methodologies, designing experiments, and performing analyses, to avoid the presence of biases (Oxford Reference,
2022).
The following sections will describe AI from a computer science perspective. The chapter will elaborate on the
development of AI and Machine Learning, with a focal point on healthcare but also other sectors. In addition, the
chapter will elaborate on how binary classification models can be utilized to assist in medical care, but also the
luring dangers due to implicit bias and discriminatory practices in the Machine Learning Life Cycle, which will be
supported by numerous examples. The chapter will provide a comprehensive view of the typical forms of biases,
discrimination, and the layers of inequalities, with a focus on the healthcare sector.
3.1
AI and Machine Learning
It is important to distinguish the difference between AI and machine learning as they are not the same thing. The
difference is depicted in Figure 3.
This data-driven approach for machine learning contrasts with the expert system approach for classical AI, in which
programmers consult with human domain experts to understand the decision-making criteria and then translate
those rules into software code. These models, on the other hand, are extremely difficult to develop, as they need
subject expertise and can be difficult to adjust to changing situations. Machine Learning takes a different approach
to system development: rather than developing a model or set of instructions for performing a given task, engineers
provide the system with (a massive amount of) data about the task and the expected results. After analyzing all of
this data, the computer is intended to derive a model (Dignum, 2019).
There are numerous methods for Machine Learning, the most well-known of which are Supervised Learning, Unsu-
pervised Learning, Reinforcement Learning, and Deep Learning. The methods differ in their design of an algorithm,
but the common goal is to develop a trained model capable of making generalizations without human intervention.
It should be accurate not only with respect to the instances in the training set but also with respect to future scenarios
that it has never encountered before (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Figure 3: The difference between AI and Machine Learning by Dignum, 2019
For this project the focal point is aimed at machine learning and risk scores, typically based on supervised learning as
the major algorithms used in healthcare, and also in which there have been reported biases, are based on supervised
machine learning techniques, as the Framingham Risk Score for coronary heart disease (CHD), which is one of the
most commonly used instances of supervised learning in medicine (Deo, 2015; Pierson et al., 2021). This is not to
say that AI in healthcare is not discussed, rather, it is that majority of the focus is going to be machine learning. In
the later chapters, machine learning and AI will be used interchangeably as the discussion moves to a broader scale.
3.1.1
The machine learning life cycle
This section will briefly go over the steps for generating a model, that can be used in healthcare. All of the steps are
deciphered in Figure 4. It involves three topics, data gathering, model building, and model deployment/implemen-
tation. The following process is oversimplified and does take into account all the needed steps when developing
Page 14 of 136
==PAGE:16==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
algorithms, as the focal point of the project is not model development. When developing algorithms, and applying
machine learning techniques, begins with data gathering, which is often denoted as preprocessing. From that, a
mathematical framework encapsulating the variety of different decision-making rules with adjustable parameters
is selected, which can be described as the algorithm. An algorithm is a finite sequence of well-defined instructions
that are often used to solve a specific class of problems or conduct a computation. An algorithm may have millions
of parameters, which can be adjusted, the sum of which is difficult to comprehend by observation alone. Hence,
the use of the expression ‘black box’ refers to the ambiguity of algorithms (Dignum, 2019).
When training the model, the developers adjust the parameters with respect to the given objective function. The
objective function evaluates the desirability of the outcome of a particular set of parameters. This is accomplished
automatically by the model, which continuously adjusts its parameters in response to actual and predicted results.
The objective function is very dependent on the specific, case and the inherent data of the model. Often model
accuracy is the objective, which a model tries to optimize, however, Section 3.3, will elaborate, on how this is not
always the case. After training a system, the model is tested on the test set to determine the model’s accuracy and
effectiveness, to ensure robustness, which can be denoted in-processing. If the model’s accuracy and effectiveness
are sufficient, the model can then be used to make predictions or take action in response to new data, and from that,
the model can be deployed and implemented in the desired setting, during post-processing (IBM, 2020).
Figure 4: An illustration of a Machine Learning Life cycle. Adopted from Suresh and Guttag, 2021
3.1.2
CRISP-DM
The Cross-Industry Standard Process for Data Mining (CRISP-DM) can be used to show the stages involved in
analytics projects for model development. CRISP-DM, or Cross-Industry Standard Process for Data Mining, is the
most extensively used analytics process standard by far. It is an open standard that anybody may adopt. It was
developed with financing from the European Union by a partnership of over 200 interested organizations (Forbes,
2015) This standard splits the process of generating and deploying machine learning classification models into six
distinct stages/tasks that are iteratively connected (Chapman et al., 1999).
The CRISP-DM process model has six major phases:
• Business Understanding: The establishment of a clear understanding of the problem that is being tried to
solve, how it impacts the organization, and the goals for addressing it.
• Data Understanding: Examination, description, and assessment of the data
• Data Preparation: Curation of initial raw data into final data set used for modeling.
• Modeling This stage entails selecting and using various modeling techniques, as well as optimizing their
parameters to their optimal levels. Due to the fact that multiple methods may be applicable to the same data
Page 15 of 136
==PAGE:17==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
mining problem, it is frequently required to revisit the data preparation step and ascertain the unique needs
for the data form.
• Evaluation: In order to deploy the model, it must first be re-evaluated, including its ability to achieve the
business objectives from phase 1. The basic purpose is to assess if any major issues have been appropriately
resolved. If not, previous phases have to be revisited.
• Deployment: Integration of the model into the desired application domain (Chapman et al., 1999)
The CRISP-DM model can be very valuable for this work because it incorporates all of the procedures taken in
constructing the classifier presented in Figure 4, and. Moreover, the CRISP-DM industry-proven way to guide
data-mining efforts. Thus it will be used later to serve as inspiration for a framework for Responsible AI.
Figure 5: Process diagram for the CRISP-DM model. From Chapman et al., 1999
3.2
Machine learning and classification models
A classification model’s objective is the mapping from given observed variables X to the space of values that the
target variable Y can assume. The Target Variable Y is the variable that is to be predicted, given some input data
X. For instance, an algorithm might attempt to estimate whether a patient should receive a specific treatment, given
his or her medical records (X). However, a model can not learn how those features relate to treatment, without first
being given an example of which features of X, would result in the patient needing treatment (Barocas, Hardt, and
Narayanan, 2018).
Supervised learning is the prevalent method of developing a model, that reflects the relationships between input and
output, given the data sample and expected outputs. The fundamental concept is quite straightforward. Assume
the labeled data, often known as training examples is known, (x1, y1) , . . . , (xn, yn), where each example is a pair
(xi, yi) of an instance xi and a label yi. Typically, labels in a classification issue come from a discrete set, such as
1, 1 in the case of binary classification. These labels can be interpreted as s partitioning the set of instances as either
acceptance or rejection based on their label. By equating a positive label with acceptance and a negative label with
rejection, such a classifier can be interpreted as a decision rule.
Typically, in a regression problem, the label y is a real number. The objective is no longer to accurately anticipate
the value of y, but to come close. In practice, the tools used to address classification and regression problems are
extremely similar (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
3.2.1
Score Function for a binary classification model
A classifier is often denoted as ˆY , which is the predicted value, whereas the actual true condition is denoted as
Y . A model’s performance can be defined through P{Y = ˆY }, which is denoted as the True Positive Rate(TPR).
Notions of TPR are elaborated on later in Section 3.3.
Classification is frequently approached by first regressing data into a single continuous value, a score function. (e.g.
predicted probability of having a disease). A common way of deriving score functions is the expectation of the target
Page 16 of 136
==PAGE:18==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
variable Y conditional on the features X, from historical data. From that the score can be expressed as R = r(X)
where r(x) = E[Y | X = x], or more shortly, R = E[Y | X]. The score function, in some aspects, delivers the
most accurate estimate for the target variable given the available data. A common example is a credit score, which
is widely utilized in some countries, to determine the approval or refusal of loan applications depending on their
credit score value (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
A score function can be turned into a classifier by thresholding the score function to certain values. For a binary
classifier by thresholding the score a threshold t, so that when the score function is above the threshold t, the
classifier outputs ˆY = 1(accept), and when the score function is below t then ˆY = 0(reject). The values 1 and 0
are arbitrary. To determine what the threshold should be is depending on the given scenario, which is determined
based on the notion of utility for the resulting classifier (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Other methods of machine learning exist, however, as stated earlier in Section 3.1 this project will examine the use
of supervised learning, and its use to build binary classifiers, due to its relevance for healthcare algorithms (Barocas,
Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
3.3
Quantitative Metrics for Binary Classification Models
What characteristics make a classifier suitable for a particular application, and how can it be determined which one
to choose from a large number of alternative classifiers? While this question frequently lacks a satisfactory solution,
some formal criteria can assist which highlights the various characteristics of a classifier that can influence a choice.
To access a model’s performance requires an investigation of a model’s predicted value and its true values. The
measures are best explained through the use of a confusion matrix, which is a useful tool, often used in machine
learning to describe how the expected values and true values are distributed. It describes the accuracy of a classifi-
cation model. Rows and columns of the matrix represent instances of the predicted and actual classes, respectively.
For a binary classifier, both predicted and actual classes have two values: positive and negative (see Table 2). They
correspond to whether the prediction is correct or not, which is elaborated on with the following definitions:
Actual positive (P)
Actual negative (N)
Predicted positive
True positive (TP)
False positive (FP)
Predicted negative
False Negative (FN)
True negative (TN)
Table 2: Confusion matrix for a binary classification Fawcett, 2006
• P: Condition Positive is the amount of real positive cases from the data
• N condition negative is the amount of real negatives cases from the data
• TN: True Negative: when a model predicted ’no’ and the actual value was ‘no’ (i.e., correct prediction).
• TP: True Positive,: when a model predicted ’yes’ and the actual value was ‘yes’ (i.e., correct prediction)
• FN: False Negative: when a model predicted ’no’ and the actual value was ‘yes’ (i.e., wrong prediction)
• FP: False Positive: when a model predicted ’yes’ and the actual value was ‘no’(i.e., wrong prediction)
By using the confusion metrics it allows for calculating different performance metrics, which are utilized to deter-
mine model applicability, with the most commonly used metric being accuracy. The accuracy of a machine learning
model is used to assess, how effective a model is in identifying relationships and patterns between variables in a
dataset using the input, or training data. The more generalizable a model is to ’unseen’ data, the more accurate
forecasts and insights it may generate, which results in increased value.
From the confusion matrix of Tabel 2, the accuracy of a model, can be calculated through the following equa-
tion(Fawcett, 2006):
accuracy, % =
TN + TP
TP + TN + FP + FN
and the error rate, which states how often the model is making a wrong prediction.
Classification error = (FP + FN)/(TP + TN + FP + FN)

==PAGE:19==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Improved accuracy is vital for machine learning models, e.g. in hospitals. For instance, a false negative cancer
diagnosis, is highly unwanted, as earlier detection of cancer increases survival rates. On the other hand, too many
false positive for instance, a false positive cancer diagnosis is costly to both the hospital and the patient, due to the
psychological stress involved, in having cancer. However, as accuracy is the most commonly used metric to compare
models, model evaluation is not always concerned with accuracy only (Raj, 2020). More importantly, knowing what
metric is important to use, for a given dataset is vital, however, case-specific. When the data is balanced, accuracy
is a good indicator of a model’s performance. On the other hand, if data is skewed and unbalanced, accuracy is
not a valid metric of evaluation (Raj, 2020; Rawat, 2019). For instance, an algorithm constructed on a dataset of
breast cancer patients comprised of men and women in which 99% of men were negative for breast cancer, and
using accuracy to measure performance, should raise concern. Even when the model fails to predict any cases of
breast cancer among men its accuracy is still 99%, as data contain 99% non-breast cancer among men. So, accuracy
does not hold good for imbalanced data (Raj, 2020; Rawat, 2019). For the majority of scenarios, datasets will be
unbalanced, and thus accuracy will be a suboptimal metric for evaluating classification models. This highlights the
importance of both a thorough understanding of the data and also begs the question of what other metrics exist for
evaluating the performance of machine learning models and which one should be used (Raj, 2020; Rawat, 2019).
Below are other statistical metrics used for evaluating a binary classifier. The metrics use an event and condition,
reflecting the prediction and the ’ground truth’.

3.3.1
From scores to classifiers - example with kidney transplant waiting list
The following example will explain how to go from scores to classifiers and how statistical measures can be used
to evaluate a binary classifier. The example build on the Superhero Dataset from Kaggle Kaggle, 2018, and have
been used to construct the questionnaire in Section 6.1. The resulting questionnaire can be found in Appendix
10.2. The case and figures are based on the work of Meredith Whittake from Google and Amalie Pauli from the
Alexander Institute and adjusted to fit the framework of healthcare, making the fictional, and only for illustrative
purposes (Meredith Whittaker, 2021; Amalie Broggaard Pauli, 2021). Nonetheless, the figures demonstrate how
the Fairness metric Independence or Demographic Parity can be achieved for a dataset, and what metrics need to
align in order to do so.
A hospital has developed an algorithm to aid the experts in truly identifying eligible patients for a kidney transplant
waiting list. The algorithm works by using the "estimated glomerular filtration rates (eGFR)" which is computed by
combining demographic information, like sex, gender, and age, with patients’ health records, together with a blood
sample (X) to estimate patients’ glomerular filtration rates (R). Based on the eGFR values the algorithm predicts
whether or not patients, should be granted a place on the kidney transplant waiting list ( ˆY ). (eGFR) readings near
0 suggest that individuals are in severe need of a kidney transplant due to their impaired kidney function, whilst
values near 1 show that the kidney is functioning normally. Due to the scarcity of available kidneys, a threshold
is set, determining the cut-off point between patients, where all patients with eGFR values below the threshold are
added to the waitlist. Values above the eGFR threshold indicate, that the patient’s kidney function is estimated
as stable, and for the moment being, the patient does not need a kidney transplant, thus not added to the waitlist
(Health (NIH), n.d.; Bjornstad, Karger, and Maahs, 2018).

was positive. However, it is possible that our model predicted all values as positive, indicating that the model is
inaccurate.

in need of a kidney transplant.

extremely poor due to the huge amount of false negatives (Koehrsen, 2021).

predicted negative cases in the negative class divided by the total number of anticipated negative cases. In the
context of the kidney transplantation example, NPV refers to the probability that a patient denied a waiting list spot
genuinely was not in need of a kidney transplant.

As stated with precision and recall, there is a tradeoff. Increasing one, lowers the other, as the tradeoff can be
described as: While recall refers to the capability of a model to locate all relevant occurrences within a dataset,
precision refers to the proportion of data points our model claims are significant that actually are (Koehrsen, 2021).
By balancing both of them, gives a more ‘combined’ expression for the precision-recall called the F1-score. The
F1-score is a statistic that takes the harmonic mean of recall and accuracy (Raj, 2020; Rawat, 2019).
F1 Score = 2 · ( Recall · Precision)
Recall + Precision
(9)
Instead of a simple average, the harmonic mean is utilized since it penalizes extreme values. With a precision of
1.0 and a recall of 0.0, a classifier will have an average of 0.5, however, the F1 score will be 0. The F1 score assigns
equal weight to both metrics. Thus the F1 score can be an adequate measure, if the goal is to strike a balance
between Precision and Recall, and if there is an unequal balance in the data. If it is desired to prioritize either recall
or accuracy, the Fβ score can be used, which is a measure that incorporates recall and accuracy as well but weights
them differently.
Fβ =
 1 + β2
· ( precision · recall )
(β2 · precision + recall )
(10)
A common utility notion is to strike a balance between false positive rate and true positive rate, as higher true
positive rates results in more false positives, and vice versa. The selection of a threshold and the consequent trade-
off between true positive and false positive rates may be graphically represented using a ROC curve, which stands
for receiver operating characteristic. Such a figure is depicted in Figure 7. The true positive rate can be calculated
as 1 - the false positive rate.
Figure 7: An illustration of a ROC curve. Each point on the solid green curve is represented by a scoring function
that is thresholded at some value. The dashed line illustrates the trade-offs that result from randomly accepting an
instance regardless of its characteristics with a probability p ∈[0, 1]. From Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018
The ROC curve is widely used, for the graphical representation of thresholds. Moreover, the ROC curve also
provides the score’s prediction power of the target variables, where the area under the curve (AUC) is a commonly
used indicator of predictiveness; it represents the probability that a random positive instance would receive a higher
score than a random negative occurrence. The dotted line in 12 represents an area of 1/2 indicating random guessing,
whereas each point on the solid green curve is represented by a scoring function that is thresholded at some value,
Page 21 of 136
==PAGE:23==
