February 13, 2022
whereas a are of 1 indicates flawless categorization, or more precisely, the score equals the target. Thus greater
the AUC, the more accurate the model is at discriminating between positive and negative classifications. If, on the
other hand, the AUC is zero, the classifier would forecast all Negatives as Positives and all Positives as Negatives
(Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Above are just some of the metrics, that can be used to determine model performance. However, the metrics chosen
are depending on the notion of utility. Later sections will demonstrate, how these metrics, by group fairness metrics
to check for any potential bias within datasets and models (Bellamy et al., 2019). However, why would a model
not ensure equal performance across demographic groups, and why do disparities exist? Numerous factors may
play a role, including a history of explicit discrimination, implicit gender attitudes and stereotypes, and disparities
in the distribution of particular features by gender (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018). The next section will
demonstrate that stereotypes can be self-fulfilling and saturate an algorithm even if the algorithm is perceived to
have no discrimination. As machine learning is integrated into decision-making, it is vital to exercise caution to
avoid machine learning becoming a part of a negative feedback loop, which be elaborated on in the following
sections.
3.4
The dangers of Machine Learning due to implicit bias and discriminatory practices
in the Machine Learning Life Cycle
Fairness or the negative consequences of flawed and biased machine learning can be argued to be irrelevant in ma-
chine learning systems used for filtering email spam, recommender systems for restaurants, movies, and shopping,
as these are low-stakes situations; a forecast is simply true or incorrect, and incorrect prediction is typically negli-
gible - at worst, little discomfort, or displeasure. However, when it comes to machine learning decision algorithms,
such as those used in hospitals, the cost of errors is enormous, as they could have a significant influence on people’s
life (Jacobson, 2019).
While training machine learning algorithms to optimize prediction accuracy on training data appears to be objective,
it usually yields algorithms that, while technically correct, produce biased and unfair outcomes. The biased and
unfair algorithms have partly to do with algorithms only being the product of the inherent data and thus only as
objective as the data. If the underlying data is skewed in any way, the algorithm may reproduce, if not exacerbate,
its systemic imbalances based on race, gender, religion, or other traits (Jacobson, 2019).
Even more confounding is the reality that social problems such as racism, prejudice, and discrimination are deeply
ingrained in the society — and thus in the data collected. For example, despite having the same incidence of drug
use as whites, blacks are arrested four times more frequently for drug-related offenses. (Jacobson, 2019). Even if
engineers successfully obtained and trained a predictive machine learning model on this data, the resulting algorithm
would be contaminated by the bias inherited by the environment that provided the data. Predictive services based on
powerful machine learning systems have become increasingly popular in the criminal justice system, which was also
the case of COMPAS, a risk-assessment algorithm that evaluated an accused criminal’s probability of recidivism,
which was utilized to inform judges’ and help them with their decisions at every stage of the process. The tool was
created based on assisting individual judges in dispensing justice more evenly, thus eliminating discrepancies in
sentencing across groups, thus increasing fairness. However, workers at ProPublica Jeff Larson and Angwin, 2016,
a nonprofit organization, discovered that rather than increasing fairness, the tool tended to reinforce racial biases
found in the law enforcement data, that had been used to train it — even though race was not one of the system’s
inputs. Additionally, ProPublica discovered that judges who relied on these risk assessments frequently lacked
an understanding of how the scores were calculated, due to the black box, rendering them unable to successfully
challenge them or identify and correct for the inherent biases (Jeff Larson and Angwin, 2016).
3.4.1
Types of harms
When decision-making processes in, health care, or any sector are biased or contains discriminatory practices, it
can create allocative harms result, which occurs when a system or a model denies some groups an opportunity or
resource, and the result of poor allocative decisions may be loss of life in terms of healthcare. Allocative harms can
occur for a number of reasons where one of the most challenging aspects is determining the utility to be optimized.
’True’ utility is frequently an unquantifiable, imprecise goal (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018; Crawford,
2017).
Additionally, even with the most rigorous and cross-functional training and testing, it is a challenge to ensure that
a system will be fair across all situations, as algorithms tailor their decisions to existing data patterns, necessitating
Page 22 of 136
==PAGE:24==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
the collection of data on the population they affect. Moreover, it is not always obvious how decisions will be
implemented. A judgment made by an algorithm is frequently provided as "one piece of information" to be used
by a human decision-maker, who may use the algorithm’s output to discriminate in unanticipated ways. (Barocas,
Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018; Crawford, 2017).
Allocative harms are those caused by algorithmic inequity that can be reduced through resource reallocation,
whereas representational harms are those caused by inequity that cannot be mitigated through resource reallocation.
By contrast, representational harms are instances of stereotype persistence and cultural denigration that occur when
systems reinforce some groups’ subordination along identity lines such as race, class, gender, as well as under-
representation in datasets, which often are over-represented, typically by white men (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan,
2018; Crawford, 2017).
Thus an algorithm or model can accurate, as it reflects the current state of circumstance, however, it can be unfair,
as it reinforces the current unfairness in the social system, which opens up the question of what an algorithm should
return? If the unfairness was reinforced from the unfairness in the social structures under which the model was fit,
the question then becomes, what should the algorithm return, as both harms have the potential to contribute to a
negative feedback loop (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018; Crawford, 2017).
3.4.2
Negative Feedback Loop
Nobel prize winner Kenneth J. Arrow’s work Theory of Discrimination, exemplifies the impact of negative feed-
back loops, his theory of discrimination (Arrow, 1971). In the example, there are two sorts of workers and jobs:
skilled and unskilled, with skilled professions requiring specific skills to perform effectively. Under appropriate
assumptions (e.g., employers cannot accurately assess worker qualifications before hiring, but can only do so after
offering costly on-the-job skills training), an economic equilibrium emerges in which the following feedback loop
persists:
• The employer discriminates on the basis of wage between the two groups.
• As a result, the disadvantaged group earns poorer returns on qualifications investment.
• Assuming workers are reasonable, each group respond to their given treatment, by investing differently in
acquiring qualifications e.g. jobs, with one group acquiring higher qualifications due to their treatment
• Again, under specific rationality assumptions, the employer discriminates on the basis of perceived differ-
ences in acquired qualifications for groups.
The relevance of this concept is that it can account for the persistence of inequality and discriminatory practices,
without assuming underlying disparities between groups or employers discriminating against similarly competent
individuals. It is to be interpreted as illustrating the possibilities of such feedback loops, and the impact they can
have. This concept can be translated into how negative feedback occurs in the machine learning pipeline, as a
consequence of biases or discriminatory practices. The next section will reiterate some of the most significant and
often occurring sources of bias, based on the work of numerous studies, and will illustrate the various challenges
occurring through the stages of the machine learning life-cycle and provide appropriate terminology.
3.4.3
How biases and discriminatory practices emerge in Machine Learning Systems
When biases exist in data, it can hinder the purpose of any algorithm, and continue reinforcing a negative feedback
loop, even if was made with the best of intentions. To mitigate and intervene in any potential bias or discriminatory
outcomes, and to comprehend the many diverse conceptions of fairness, it is crucial to have an understanding of
why biases arise (Mehrabi et al., 2019).
Biases and discriminatory practices occur in any process along the machine learning life cycle. Several articles
have been used to highlight how biases arise during the machine learning life cycle, however Suresh and Guttag,
2021; Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018; Mehrabi et al., 2019, findings constitute some of the main sources of
biases, that occurs, from data gathering to model building and roll-out:
Page 23 of 136
==PAGE:25==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Figure 8: Biases arising in the machine learning life cycle (Mehrabi et al., 2019
As Figure 8 depicts, biases arise in every step from data generation to deployment. The arrows demonstrate the
cyclic nature of how biases arise and manifest into society, which creates a negative feedback loop, like the one
highlighted in Section 3.4.2. Decisions affected by generated models have an effect on the condition of the world
when new data is acquired or new decisions are made. A model containing bias would then reinforce that existing
bias into society, if not mitigated. The highlighted red text in Figure 8, indicates, the common pitfalls for biases when
building and deploying models. Biases occurring in the preprocessing, or data generation, are denoted biases in data
gathering, whereas in processing a denoted as biases in modeling. Once developed the model is deployed, which
is where the last sort of biases can arise, referred to as biases in deployment (Suresh and Guttag, 2021; Barocas,
Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018; Mehrabi et al., 2019). Below are examples of biases occurring in the machine learning
life cycle, as well as other biases, which can influence machine learning models.
3.4.4
Data gathering bias
Representation Bias: Representation Bias occurs, when the datasets, consist of an incorrect sample, one that is
too small, contains too many erroneous data points or does not reflect the full data pool. Common examples are
the lack of geographical, ethnic, etc. diversity in the datasets, which results will be inaccurate for data points that
differ from a sample.
A team from Stanford performed a systematic review of Type 2 diabetes models and discovered that just 25% of the
publications reviewed included population demographics, similar to that of the target population for black and white
patients. However, no study reported on the type 2 diabetes population, among Hispanics in their training, which
raises concerns, as Hispanics have a greater frequency of Type 2 diabetes complications than whites (Hernandez-
Boussard, 2022). Types of Representation Bias include:
• If the population data used, is not reflected in the target population If data from 20 years ago is used to
predict an outcome for today, e.g. likelihood of credit default.
• If minority groups (or any group) are under-represented in the target population A common example of
this could be if a target population from a medical dataset is set to be females, however, the Asian population,
is under-represented, and is only 5% of the target population, which will result in the model begin less robust
and precise for Asian group due to under-representing.
• Sampling bias occurs when subgroups are limited or uneven sample As a result of sampling bias, trends
identified for one group may not generalize to different population data a. If medical datasets consist of
only patients who were severely ill of a certain infectious disease, and the target population is all adults, the
development sample will be a skewed representation of the target population (Mehrabi et al., 2019).
Measurement Bias: Measurement bias can occur when specific features and labels are chosen and utilized for a
predictive problem. Such a feature typically works as a proxy to estimate some construct, which may not be directly
encoded or observable. However, when said proxies are poor reflections, or when the target constructs and/or are
generated differently among groups, measurement biases can occur:
Page 24 of 136
==PAGE:26==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
• The proxy is a simplified representation of a more complicated construct: In a model in the US, health
costs were used as a proxy for health needs. Due to the fact that less money was spent on Black patients
with comparable levels of need, the algorithm incorrectly determined that Black patients were healthier than
equally ill White patients (Suresh and Guttag, 2021, Ledford, 2019).
• The method of measurement differs considerably between groups: If a hospital monitors its physicians
and clinicians across departments and counts errors as a proxy for quality, it is critical to do so uniformly
across departments. Otherwise, if an apartment is inspected more closely and frequently, one department may
observe more faults than others. This establishes the possibility of a feedback loop in which the department
is subsequently monitored even more frequently as a result of its apparent increased rate of errors (Suresh
and Guttag, 2021).
• The precision of measurement varies by group: In medical contexts, "diagnosed with condition X" is
interchangeably used as a proxy for "has condition X." However, systematic over-or under-diagnosis in spe-
cific groups can occur due to structural discrimination, like gender and racial disparities in diagnoses of
pain-related illnesses, which have been reported to vary across groups, thus leading to erroneous conclusions
(Pierson et al., 2021).
Historical Bias: Preexisting biases are often referred to as historical bias, which occurs if the world as it is, leads
to a model that produces harmful outcomes. Preexisting biases, due to socio-technical issues, can, even if data is
generated from perfect sampling and feature selection, influence a model, creating a positive bias towards groups
that have been historically disadvantaged or excluded. An instance of this involves cardiovascular studies, which
have a strong male bias, as male-specific symptoms were employed to train the models, as previously there was
unawareness of variations in symptoms between men and women (Sangild, 2021).
Tainted bias: Closely related is tainted bias, which is when a model retains existing bias, which initially was caused
by human bias. An example of this could be if a company uses a model to find suitable applicants for a job, based
on features made by a manager’s decision, rather than the applicant’s capabilities. Such a system would replicate
existing biases created by the manager (Mehrabi et al., 2019).
Temporal bias: Temporal bias arises, from the differences over time observed in behaviors populations, and is the
mismatch between the data used in the study and the data available when making e.g., a diagnostic decision. An
example of this is the first discovery of Lyme disease, which was when the initial treatment contained Temporal bias.
Even though the case of Lyme disease is a historical case, temporal bias is not a historical issue. There are various
examples of temporal bias in the medical sector, including clinical care and machine learning, where temporal bias
arises, primarily when cases are sampled unevenly in time across patients’ health trajectories (Yuan et al., 2021).
3.4.5
Biases in modeling
Evaluation Bias: The evaluation of a model is contingent on two critical choices: the testing data and the metrics
utilized. When a model is optimized using training data, its quality is frequently determined by comparison to spec-
ified disproportionately large benchmarks for application evaluation. Thus, biases can occur when these standards
do not accurately represent the target population or are inappropriate for the intended application of the model,
because developers typically train on a benchmark dataset or a synthetic dataset, and so their evaluations frequently
do not correspond to real-world situations. Due to skewness of the benchmark in terms of history, representation,
or measurement, this is worrisome (Mehrabi et al., 2019, Suresh and Guttag, 2021). Similarly, if the wrong perfor-
mance metrics are utilized, evaluation bias might occur. For instance, if the dataset has a significant class imbalance
and solely the area under the curve is analyzed, there may be evaluation bias as a result of the failure to consider
other critical metrics such as positive and negative predicted probability on subgroups. As a result, evaluation bias
also refers to the inefficient use of evaluation metrics. To avoid this, it is proposed that granular and comprehensive
evaluation measures be used (Mehrabi et al., 2019, Suresh and Guttag, 2021, Hernandez-Boussard, 2022).
Aggregation bias: Aggregation bias occurs when a model consists of a diverse population that has been incorrectly
combined, thus leading to erroneous conclusions of a subgroup, as they are drawn based on observations of other
subgroups. More broadly, it occurs on the basis of incorrect assumptions about a population, which have an effect on
the model’s outcome and definition. Numerous cases of aggregation biases have been observed, especially within
healthcare, when AI models have been applied to a heterogeneous population, where a single model is unlikely to
be a match for all groups. AI models have frequently been used in the diagnosis and monitoring of diabetes, where
models have predicted diabetes using Hemoglobin AIc (HbAIc) values. However, a 2019 article demonstrated that
these values vary significantly across ethnic groups, therefore a single model for all people is inevitably biased. As
Page 25 of 136
==PAGE:27==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
a result of these characteristics and their varying meanings and significance among subgroups and populations, a
single model is unlikely to be optimal for all groups within a population. This holds true even if they are equally
represented in the training data. Any sweeping generalizations about distinct populations can result in aggregation
bias (Ford et al., 2019,Mehrabi et al., 2019), Hernandez-Boussard, 2022).
Aggregation bias: Simpson’s Paradox Simpson’s paradox states that a trend, association, or characteristic identi-
fied in underlying subgroups may be completely dissimilar to the association or characteristic detected when these
subgroups are aggregated (Mehrabi et al., 2019).
Several examples of Simpson’s paradox exist, one of them being the gender bias lawsuit in university admissions
against UC Berkeley. One of the more well-known instances of this type of paradox occurred during the gender
prejudice litigation against UC Berkeley in the area of university admissions. After studying graduate school ad-
missions statistics, it appeared as though there was a prejudice against women, with a lesser proportion admitted
to graduate schools than their male counterparts. When admissions data was split and studied by department, it
was discovered that women applicants had equal standing and, in some cases, a little advantage over men. The
contradiction occurred when women applied to departments with lower acceptance rates for both men and women.
This is an illustration of Simpson’s paradox. This effect manifests itself when the trends in subgroups of the dataset
diverge from the general trend. If a model is fit blindly to data, the model could risk providing inaccurate answers.
Developers need to consider the data’s meaning and then select a model that reflects the trend for their demographic.
However, there is no way to solve this problem with only mathematics, and developers of machine learning models
must consider the significance of the data (Mehrabi et al., 2019)
Learning bias: When modeling choices magnify performance discrepancies across different examples in the data,
learning bias occurs. For instance, a critical modeling decision is an objective function that a machine learning sys-
tem optimizes during training. Typically, these functions encode some degree of task precision, and complications
can arise, when the target, e.g., accuracy is prioritized over another e.g., disparate impact, leading to learning bias
(Suresh and Guttag, 2021).
Algorithmic bias: Algorithmic bias is an error that happens when an algorithm at the heart of the machine learning
process is flawed or inapplicable to the current task. Algorithmic bias can be detected when an application begins
to produce incorrect results for a certain set of users. If an algorithm approach produces inconsistent outcomes for
almost identical circumstances, the model should be analyzed to a greater extent and evaluated whether it is a good
fit for the problem at hand. Algorithmic bias might be deliberate or accidental. This could be the result of technical
flaws within the algorithm’s core or an incorrect algorithm selection in the first place (Mehrabi et al., 2019).
Implicit Bias Implicit bias occurs when generalizations are made based on one’s own mental models and personal
experiences. In 2015, a group of academics undertook a review of the literature to determine the prevalence and
consequences of implicit bias. The review concluded that the majority of medical providers exhibit at least moderate
unconscious bias. The Implicit Association Test, which assesses implicit bias, identified approximately identical
levels of bias in patients who were Black, Latinx, or dark-skinned (Hall et al., 2015). A common form of Implicit
Bias is Confirmation Bias, which is defined as the human inclination to seek for, understand, focus on, and remember
information that confirms one’s assumptions, thus inextricably linked to above mentioned behavioral bias.
Confounder: Confounding is another type of bias, and confounding effects (or biases) are some of the most com-
mon obstacles when developing and deploying deep learning to advance medical imaging discovery. The process of
confounding is when an extraneous, third variable termed a confounder distorts the association between an exposure
and an outcome. Confounding is a frequent occurrence in etiologic investigations since the exposure of interest is
rarely the sole factor that differs between exposed and unexposed groups effects on the link between input data (for
example, brain MRIs) and output variables (e.g., diagnosis). Incorrect modeling of these interactions frequently
produces erroneous and biased associations (Lorraine et al., 2015)
3.4.6
Deployment Bias:
Deployment Bias: Deployment biases occur when there is a misalignment between the way a model solves a
problem, and the way it was intended to solve the problem. (Hernandez-Boussard, 2022) A common example is an
AI model in the US, developed to predict healthcare needs. However, the model was trained to forecast healthcare
expenditures, which model was highly accurate and capable of discriminating between patients with high and low
costs. However, as the model was used to predict healthcare needs, with healthcare cost as a proxy, the deployment
of the model was biased. The deployment bias of this AI Solution resulted in a black patient needing to be almost
twice as sick as a white patient in order to qualify for the same beneficial care program (Ledford, 2019).
Page 26 of 136
==PAGE:28==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
3.4.7
Other types of biases
Cognitive bias: Cognitive biases arise when critical thinking is flawed, which can occur during the processing or
interpretation of available information. Cognitive bias can impair decisions and judgments, resulting in diagnostic
errors, patient harm, and financial implications. This occurs due to the complex structure of the human mind which
is wired to seek out the simplest solution and seeking for the simplest solution may imply to cease looking for
alternatives (R. M. Piryani, S. Piryani, and R. Piryani, 2019). A study of Cognitive biases in hospitals revealed that
as diagnostic errors are considered to account for 6–17% of adverse events in hospitals, cognitive errors account for
28 % of these. Moreover, cognitive bias accounts for 70% of diagnostic errors, while knowledge shortage accounts
for a negligible percentage (R. M. Piryani, S. Piryani, and R. Piryani, 2019).
Presentation Bias Presentation bias results from the manner in which information is presented. An example of this
could be if a healthcare professional is searching through potential best practices for its patients, the users are only
presented with the content they see, which means that only viewable information receives clicks, and additionally
the user might not be presented with all of the information available (Mehrabi et al., 2019).
3.5
Discrimination
Discrimination, like bias, is a source of injustice. Discrimination is a source of unfairness that results from human
prejudice and stereotyping based on sensitive characteristics, which may occur intentionally or accidentally, whereas
bias is a source of unfairness that results from data collection, sampling, and measurement. (mostly) (Mehrabi et al.,
2019).
Often when developing algorithms, there are a set of anti-discriminatory protected attributes, which serve to pro-
tect individuals and instill fairness in machine learning. Thus developers of algorithms are not allowed to use
those attributes when constructing algorithms. When biases occur in algorithms, they often breach those anti-
discriminatory protected attributes, which lead to non-intended discriminatory consequences, which brings forth
the notions of fairness in machine learning, which are attempts to mitigate any potential biases (Barocas, Hardt, and
Narayanan, 2018). Below demonstrates the different interpretations of discrimination, and how a potential biases
algorithm, can lead to an algorithm inflicting discriminatory practice, which can be divided into to subcategories:
Explainable, Unexplainable Discrimination, and Sources of Discrimination. In some circumstances, differences
in treatment and outcomes between groups can be justified and explained by certain characteristics. When these
distinctions are reasonable and explicable, they are not deemed illegal discrimination and are thus referred to as
explainable (Mehrabi et al., 2019). Unexplainable discrimination occurs when a group is subjected to unjustified
prejudice, which is deemed illegal and consists of direct and indirect discrimination. Sources of Discrimination
consist of Systematic Discrimination and Statistical Discrimination (Mehrabi et al., 2019, and Barocas, Hardt, and
Narayanan, 2018).
• Direct Discrimination (Disparate Treatment): Direct discrimination occurs when persons’ protected char-
acteristics explicitly result in unfavorable outcomes for them. Typically, there are some characteristics defined
by law as being illegal to discriminate against, and it is these characteristics that are frequently referred to in
computer science literature as "protected" or "sensitive" features. Sensitive characteristics may include race,
color, sex, religion, and country of origin, among others (Mehrabi et al., 2019).
• Indirect Discrimination (Disparate Impact) Indirect discrimination occurs when individuals are treated
unfairly based on ostensibly neutral and non-protected traits; nonetheless, protected groups or individuals
continue to be treated unfairly as a result of the implicit impacts of their protected attributes. (For example, if
a patient’s residential zip is used to determine if the patient should be given treatment). This, however, may
result in racial prejudice since, despite the fact that zip code appears to be a non-sensitive property, it may
correlate with race (Mehrabi et al., 2019).
•
Discrimination on a Systemic Scale. Systemic discrimination refers to rules, practices, and behaviors
that are embedded in an organization’s culture or structure and may perpetuate prejudice against specific
subgroups of the population (Pierson et al., 2021) demonstrated this point, as their research discovered that
a widely used algorithm in US hospitals for allocating health care to individuals has been systematic in its
discrimination against black people. Their findings are elaborated on in Section 3.6.1.
• Statistical Discrimination Statistical discrimination is a phenomenon in which decision-makers evaluate
an individual’s membership in a group based on average group statistics. It typically arises when decision-
makers use an individual’s readily apparent, identifiable features as a proxy for either hidden or more difficult-
to-determine characteristics that may be significant to the conclusion. An example in healthcare if clinicians
Page 27 of 136
==PAGE:29==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
use race as a predictor of a patient’s underlying risk of having a condition, and the clinician is of that belief,
that the disease’s prevalence varies by racial/ethnic group. Another example could be if clinicians interpret
diagnostic signals from members of minority groups with increased noise for patients of different ethnic/racial
groups. If doctors are unable to communicate with specific patients due to differences in language, culture, or
communication patterns, they may be more likely to miss symptoms that are present, as will be demonstrated
in Section 3.6.4 (Balsa, McGuire, and Meredith, 2005).
3.6
Examples of Biases and Discriminatory practices in healthcare
To comprehend the many diverse conceptions of fairness, it is also necessary to comprehend the various forms of
discrimination that may occur, as a consequence of biases. The following section will elaborate on some examples
of biased algorithms.
3.6.1
Millions of black people affected by racial bias in health-care algorithms
A review of medical records of Americans discovered that the algorithm used by hospitals to help manage care for
around 200 million people in the United States each year was systematically discriminatory toward black patients.
The study, which was conducted by Pierson et al., 2021 concluded that the algorithm was less likely to refer black
people than white people who were equally sick, to programs that aim to improve care for patients with complex
medical needs. When the researchers ran standard statistical checks, they noticed that patients who identified as
black were frequently assigned lower risk scores than similarly ill white people. As a result, black patients were
less likely to be sent to highly personal treatment. The researchers discovered that the algorithm gave risk scores
to patients based on their cumulative healthcare costs over a one-year period. When developing the algorithm, this
was justified, as from the data, the average black patient, had similar overall healthcare costs to that of an average
white patient. However, a closer examination of the data found that the average black patient was also far sicker than
the average white patient, with a higher rate of diabetes, anemia, renal failure, and hypertension. When the data
were combined, it was shown that care supplied to black patients cost an average of US$ 1,800 less per year than
care provided to a white individual with the same number of chronic health conditions, showing a skewed resource
allocation. The authors hypothesize that this decreased access to care is a result of systemic discrimination, from
distrust of the healthcare system to direct discrimination by healthcare practitioners (Pierson et al., 2021).
The devastating consequences found by the study are vital to mitigate, however, this type of study is rare because
researchers often cannot gain access to proprietary algorithms and the reams of sensitive health data needed to fully
test them. The rareness of these studies gives the team reasons to believe, that many algorithms, could contain sim-
ilar biases (Pierson et al., 2021). The example demonstrates how existing discriminatory practices, lead to a biased
dataset when then perpetuates an algorithm, copying the preexisting biases, that exist within society. Moreover,
it shows the consequences of biases if existing within a dataset, and how it can lead to severe disparities between
demographic groups. Lastly, it illustrates, that biases can be hard to avoid, if not paying attention to preexisting
discriminatory practices (Pierson et al., 2021).
3.6.2
A sick difference - Difference in life expectancy in Aalborg
In a Danish context, differences in healthcare has also been demonstrated, when a study gathered health data from
10,000 residents of Aalborg’s rich Hasseris neighborhood and the city’s poor Aalborg East neighborhood. People
of the Aalborg East have an average life expectancy of 67 years, which is comparable to the average life expectancy
in Pakistan, whereas citizens of Hasseris have an average life expectancy of 80 years (Vedsted, 2016). The project
was led by Peter Vedsted, who also highlighted the differences between Social Economics Classes (SES). Moreover,
research from Peter highlighted the differences in SES in terms of cancer treatment, where social inequalities have
an immense impact on the treatment given, as lower SES led to lower participation in screening, lower awareness
of risk factors and symptoms, different notion of "what is normal", negative perceptions about cancer and health
care system, harder to get an appointment with the doctor, several consultations before the diagnosis is made and
less appropriate investigation processes (Vedsted, 2018). Moreover, lower SES is known to be strongly associated
with risk factors such as smoking, a sedentary lifestyle, obesity, social isolation, unsanitary water supplies, and
malnutrition, which means lower SES need for care is indistinguishable higher, as also demonstrated in Aalborg
East (DR, 2016). Thus developers need to be cognizant of the differences in SES can have on patients’ health, as if
a zip code is used as a proxy for determining care, it could have fatal consequences.
Page 28 of 136
==PAGE:30==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
3.6.3
Medical trials suffer from a strong male bias
Stroke claims the lives of 13 million people each year. In Denmark, women suffer from strokes at a higher rate
than males, even though elderly men are at a higher risk. This has to do with the fact that men die earlier. This
concerns Hanne Christensen, who is a professor and head physician, and she is essentially concerned about how
medical trials suffer from a substantial masculine bias. "If we look at where our usual data comes from, it comes
from male medical students," she explains. There is nothing wrong with them; they are simply not the most av-
erage members of society." (Sangild, 2021). This skewed distribution of subjects is just not limited in terms of
gender. This also holds for both age and ethnic origin. The skewed gender distribution is problematic since men
and women react differently to disease and women’s symptoms must be reflected equally to men’s symptoms in
disease descriptions. Women are in danger of being misdiagnosed if this is not done. Additionally, when designing
medication, it is critical to document women’s response to medicines (both the effects and adverse effects) as well
as men’s, Hanne underlines. However, Hanne Christensen explains, that there is considerable evidence that the
symptoms that women, particularly younger women, experience after strokes and heart attacks are slightly different
than those experienced by men. However, what is stated in the textbooks always reflects the symptoms experienced
by men, whereas women’s symptoms are sometimes referred to be ’non-specific’ by physicians, Women experience
significantly more pain, often headaches, as a result of a stroke. Women are more likely to have dizziness, lethargy,
and other generalized symptoms. This may certainly mislead and result in an underdiagnosis if one is unaware that
it is an indication of a stroke. The differences in symptoms are related, in part, to the fact that women’s blood clots
often occur in a different location in the brain than men’s. It could be related to biological differences, but it may
not be the only cause. Additionally, lifestyle factors such as smoking and alcohol consumption can have a role,
which males are statistically more likely to do. However, other distinctions are entirely biological, such as those
linked with menopause, the stage of life during which menstruation ceases. The earlier a woman discontinues men-
struation, the greater her risk is of stroke. Men also have a gender-specific risk factor, which is sperm quality: the
lower the sperm quality, the higher the risk of stroke. Thus, there appears to be some relationship between fertility
and gender that manifests itself rather differently in the two sexes, which if not taken into account could have dire
consequences (Sangild, 2021). Hanne explains that these biases and potential misdiagnoses occur because there
is little knowledge and too few experiments with women’s unique symptoms. There is an insufficient amount of
data and unequal gender distribution in all experiments and investigations (Sangild, 2021). As these trials and data
gatherings are the basis for algorithmic development, there is an imminent danger of developing biased algorithms,
which will continue to demonstrate similar discriminatory practices.
3.6.4
Discrimination against refugees and immigrants in Danish hospitals and job centers
The clinic for immigrant medicine at Odense University Hospital has been operating for five years. Over 700
patients with refugee/immigrant backgrounds have been evaluated and assessed. The study summarizes five years
of experience at the Immigrant Medicine Clinic, abundantly documented with patient cases, regarding the several
reasons why refugees and immigrants face differential treatment, particularly in the Danish medical system. Until
now, that aspect of discrimination has been largely ignored. However, it is clear from this report that there is a
severe lack of basic knowledge and experience regarding refugees and immigrants in the Danish hospital system,
to the point where it jeopardizes patient safety and the quality of examinations and treatment, as well as delays
diagnoses, increases the frequency of medication errors and results in more severe side effects and complications.
The conclusion is that there is a critical need for a significant increase in doctors’ and nurses’ knowledge, as well
as an improvement in the working environment that prohibits them from exerting the additional effort necessary to
avoid discrimination. This analysis demonstrates a compelling necessity for each region to develop an immigrant
medical clinic to handle the most complex patient cases (Sodemann, 2013).
The examples above are just a selection of some of the many cases of algorithms tainted with biases and discrim-
inatory practices. The examples demonstrate how existing imbalances in society can have a significant impact on
the algorithms being developed. Fortunately, few incidents have been documented in the Danish context, although,
this could also be though to the fact that, there have been conducted a small number of studies, as access to data is
difficult, as stated in Ledford, 2019. Without the ability to review the data, it is impossible to determine whether
the algorithms behave fairly.
Nevertheless, in 2015, job centers across Denmark implemented a new digital clarification and conversation tool,
which used artificial intelligence to determine whether or not an unemployed citizen is at risk of becoming long-
term unemployed. However, the tool was heavily criticized, as it lead to a substantial risk of discrimination, as the
profiling models used information as gender or ethnicity, which was then be used as either a positive or negative
factor in its assessment (IT-Branchen, 2021).
Page 29 of 136
==PAGE:31==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
In the next three years, a new Danish research initiative led by Aasa Feragen will explore bias and fairness in artificial
intelligence used in medical applications. The objective is to build fair algorithms that will aid in providing equitable
treatment to everyone in the health care system, as there is an underlying concept that there are imbalances in the
treatment provided in some health areas. This must be addressed by the implementation of a fair health algorithm
(DTU, January 2021).
However, as the examples demonstrate biases and discriminatory practices for algorithms used in healthcare, dis-
tinguished from other applications for machine learning, as the consequences of erroneous algorithms can affect
people’s lives and can lead to loss of life, as seen with Ledford, 2019 and Sangild, 2021 as well as other examples.
3.7
Concluding Remarks on the dangers of Machine Learning Algorithms
The aim of his chapter was to provide insights into the first research question of what are the types of biases that
appear in machine learning-based algorithms, what are their sources, and what are their consequences to patients.
Unbiased and fair algorithmic decision-making can be as challenging to achieve as it is in human-led decision-
making systems. Human systems are prejudiced in the same ways that algorithmic systems are skewed — both
were created by humans — and human decision-makers are biased in extra ways that machines are not. However,
algorithmic systems pose additional hurdles in some ways, as their implementation frequently renders them less
accessible and transparent than human procedures.
As Cathy O’Neil, author of Weapons of Math Destruction and an advocate for algorithmic responsibility, recently
stated in an interview:
“When you have something that’s important and secret, it’s almost always going to be destructive” (Jacobson, 2019).
As demonstrated throughout this chapter, biases can creep in in a number of different ways, as demonstrated in
Section 3.4.3, which can cause a number of biased outcomes, as both demonstrated in Section 3.4.3, and further
elaborated in Section 3.6. Existing discriminatory practices, as demonstrated by Ledford, 2019, and Sangild, 2021,
can perpetuate an algorithm, copying existing societal biases constructing a negative feedback loop, as demon-
strated in Figure 8, that maintains existing discriminatory practices, or even worse, amplify them. These biased or
discriminatory practices results in either allocative harms or representational harms as demonstrated Section 3.4.1
Additionally, people are frequently unaware that an algorithm is being used to make a decision that affects them
— and even if they are, the algorithm is presented as a sophisticated, inexplicable "black box" that is impossible to
see, let alone understand.
From, the chapter does also provide a plausible framework, the CRISP-DM in 5, which can be used to address how
do to ensure fair and responsible AI and Machine learning for healthcare in practice. Thus, the framework will be
used in later sections.
As machine learning is the distribution of goods, notions of fairness are vital. However firstly, a notion of what is
fair needs to be established, as fairness in classification does not only entail examining machine-learning algorithms
from a statistical standpoint, but also a ethical standpoint. To comprehend and describe the ethical behavior required
of such complex systems, as well as how to determine what is fair and just, a philosophical view of machine learning
and AI is needed to help explain discrepancies in individual interpretations, thus providing ethical theories upon
which artificial systems can base their actions, which will be explained in Chapter 4.
Page 30 of 136
==PAGE:32==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
4
Ethical and Moral Decision-Making
As machine learning is the distribution of some goods, either treatment, loans, etc., it makes it relevant for a fairness
discussion. Moreover, distributive justice can be linked to these concepts of fairness in machine learning, and be
used as justification (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021). However, how can fairness be constituted?
During the peak of the COVID-19 pandemic, several hospitals were overcrowded and faced a ventilator shortage,
and were forced to deal with ethical dilemmas, such as which patients should have access to a ventilator? Should
ventilators be reserved for people with the best chance of recovery (Savulescu, Cameron, and Wilkinson, 2020)?
Given the rising autonomy of AI and machine learning systems and their deployment in real-world contexts, these
systems will also face ethical challenges sooner or later, and bias, fairness, and discrimination have moved from
being a topic in philosophy and psychology to being included in machine learning and algorithms (Dignum, 2019).
In modern society, and especially during COVID-19, dealing and with resources, often scares, leads to people to
evaluate what is fair, just, and equitable, differently due to their own different experiences, beliefs, and values.
Moreover, particular morality also plays a vital part. Cultural and societal values have significant moral decisions,
which is evident from a recent study published in Nature (Awad et al., 2018). "Self-driving car dilemmas reveal
that moral choices are not universal. The study, as the title suggests, revealed the morals differ deepened on where
societal and cultural values. The study presents three clusters, that vary significantly in how much weight they
assign to certain preferences. For example, in the Eastern cluster, the preference for sparing younger characters
over older characters is considerably less evident, compared to the Southern cluster, where the inclination is much
stronger. The same tendency is shown in regards to the preferences for higher-status characters, as well as women
compared to the Eastern and Western clusters (Awad et al., 2018). As the study demonstrates, ethical disagree-
ments are frequently the consequence of conflicting individual interpretations of the issue at hand, which might be
cultural, political, or religious in nature. While the public’s ethical preferences should not be the sole determinant
of ethical policy in health care algorithms, it implies that politicians, programmers, designers, etc. should be, if
not responsive, at least cognizant, of the patterns of similarity and difference in nation’s moral preferences, when
designing artificially intelligent systems (Dignum, 2019; Awad et al., 2018).
Justice and fairness are often used interchangeably, as they are often perceived to be related or similar. From Plato’s
Republic through the late Harvard philosopher John Rawls’ A Theory of Justice, every significant work on ethics
has emphasized the centrality of justice to morality. Often, the terms fairness and justice are used interchangeably;
nevertheless, they are not synonymous, as not all fairness is just, and not all that is just is fair. However, distinct
interpretations of the two terms exist, and for the purpose of this paper, justice is referred to as a standard of rightness,
which society is held to, whereas fairness is a subjective principle of the judgment of whether a decision is morally
right or wrong (Miller, 2021; A. Carey and Wu, 2022).
Distributive justice deals with the subject of the distribution of goods in society, such as freedom or welfare, and
opportunity (Julian Lamont, 2013). The issue has centered on whether ’opportunity’ as a social benefit extends
to healthcare, however by using the work of Norman Daniels, it can be argued that healthcare can be linked to
opportunity, which is considered a primary good (Daniels, 1982).
Thus this chapter will elaborate on the theories of distribute justice and how they can be linked to notions of machine
learning, thus providing preliminary insights to the fourth research question of which ethical principles and norms
can be used to guide the design of fairness criteria for algorithmic classification.
4.1
Theories of Justice
To establish how material and non-material goods should be allocated in society, political philosophers have offered
a variety of normative theories over the years in an attempt to comprehend what constitutes fairness and how to
attain it. In circumstances such as healthcare allocation, defining what constitutes fairness and how to quantify it
remains a point of contention, with no universally accepted definition (Savulescu, Cameron, and Wilkinson, 2020)
Distributive justice refers to the fairness of decision-making outcomes (ends; equitable share). In other words, it
refers to the degree to which one believes that the allocation of goods is equitable (Julian Lamont, 2013)
As stated earlier goods can be extended to opportunities in healthcare, (Chapter 4), and thus goods can be defined
as the probability of receiving treatment, like ventilators, or a kidney transplant in Section 3.3.1 When machine
learning algorithms are used to provide treatment to applicants, the probability of receiving a treatment can be
quantified, as the predictive models assign probabilities to occurrences from the dataset, as mentioned in Section
Page 31 of 136
==PAGE:33==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
3.2. If opportunities are viewed as goods, the algorithm’s fairness can be broadly characterized as offering equal
opportunity to treatment. While this wide definition is not a conclusion but rather a value judgment, it is extremely
beneficial to this work because it focuses on individual approval/rejection decisions.
Equal opportunity is a commonly held philosophical ideal of justice. In contrast to outcome equality, equality of
opportunity distinguishes between morally acceptable and morally objectionable inequalities. At its core, equality
of opportunity seeks to stress the significant reasons for which individuals can be held ethically accountable while
downplaying the significance of circumstances or arbitrary causes (Arneson, 2015). Arbitrary conditions are those
for which individuals are not accountable, whereas voluntary circumstances, or effort, justify unequal distribution
of rewards or harms (Arneson, 2015). Three prominent interpretations of equality of opportunity can be applied
to algorithmic healthcare decision-making processes: libertarian, formal, and substantive equality of opportunity,
from which theories of justice can be conceptualized (Arneson, 2015).
4.2
Libertarian equality of opportunity
According to the libertarian perspective, it is morally permissible for individuals to perform whatever activities or
make whatever decisions with their property as long as they do not infringe on the rights of others. The rights of
others are listed as follows: no theft, fraud, breach of contract, extortion, coercion or force, assault, or inflicting
of physical damage on persons or their property (Arneson, 2015). The two theories of justice that can be used to
construct libertarian equality of opportunity are utilitarianism and libertarianism (Khaleghi, 2020,page 15).
4.2.1
The Libertarian Critique: Robert Nozick’s Version
Robert Nozick seeks inspiration from John Locke and most notably Kant’s second formulation of the categorical
imperative. He argues, that if a society is ought to treat its citizens never merely as a means to an end, but always at
the same time as an end, society must respect citizens complete ownership of themselves, and hence citizens should
have unconditional ownership of themselves, and their gains earned from labor, thus their contribution (Andersen,
2020b).
As a result of our unconditional ownership of ourselves, taxes is equal to forced labor, and taxation should be
tolerated only for the sake of upholding "Justice," i.e. to support the costs of police and courts, which can be argued
in include health to avoid "catastrophic moral horror" (Mack, 2018).
Unlike the theories of justice as egalitarianism or Rawls, libertarian theories do not place a premium on the maxi-
mization of utility or on attaining an equitable distribution of goods; rather, libertarian conceptions of justice place
a premium on "the unrestrained operation of fair procedures" (Iserson, 1999). In his book Anarchy, State, and
Utopia, Robert Nozick argues that the justice of any particular distribution of goods among a number of individuals
is contingent on the manner in which the distribution occurred (Iserson, 1999). Thus According to Nozick:
Libertarianism: A distribution is fair if it is the result of an initial fair distribution and based on voluntary
transactions
Equitable distribution of tangible goods occurs as a result of consensual exchanges between persons, and pro-
cedures take precedence over any assessment of substantive outcomes, paying only limited attention to issues of
public utility or to adequately addressing the health needs of the population (Iserson, 1999). Thus for libertarianism
distributions should be based on the resources that each person contributed towards production, or at least the distri-
bution of goods to individuals should be at least as equitable as their initial contribution, thus leaving no individual
worse (Leben, 2020; Andersen, 2020b). Even though an initial gap could exist due to historical discrimination or
social determinants, it is out of the scope in Libertarianism, and in classic Libertarianism, any variance from the
proportions of original contributions is judged to be unfair (Leben, 2020).
Moreover, libertarianism does not oppose utilitarian or egalitarian patterns of distribution if they are freely decided
by all affected parties. Any distribution of goods, including public health measures and health care, is just and
justified if and only if the relevant community’s residents freely adopt it as public policy (Beauchamp and Childress,
2013).
One way of translating this into the context of binary classifiers is to think of the prevalence of a trait in some
populations as that group’s “investment" in the classification (Leben, 2020). For the example discussed in Section
3.3.1, the proportion of truly sick lower SES patients to their group is 1.4 times larger than the proportion of
patients from the higher SES group (86%:63%). Libertarians are of that believe that each group deserves success
Page 32 of 136
==PAGE:34==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
rates that are at least as equitable as their initial contributions. For example, if the disparity gap in FP or FN rates
between lower SES and higher SES surpasses the amount of the initial disparity gap in the target attribute, then
the Libertarians may call that classifier unfair, as they are worse off than they initially were (Leben, 2020). In
the libertarian concept, the role of ‘brute luck’ with regard to allocation of care becomes prevalent. There will be
patients who have greater possibilities than others due to luck or other circumstances, and libertarians would argue
that regardless of patients’ (un)fortune, there is no moral obligation on social structures to compensate for their
position (Leben, 2020).
However, inequality within that range is not unjust because it is proportional to the trait’s initial inequality in popu-
lations. Even if the original inequity was caused by bad luck or historical oppression, Libertarians do not hold our
classifier accountable for mitigating these problems (Leben, 2020).
4.2.2
Utilitarianism
The most commonly known and most widespread consequentialist theory is utilitarianism. With utilitarianism,
it allows for the clarification of what the value of the consequences consists of, namely utility or welfare, and
also how this should be distributed, namely to ensure the maximum possible sum of welfare, for everyone who can
experience welfare. This notion can be later used, when distribution e.g. ventilators, or other healthcare distributions
(Andersen, 2020a; Larry Alexander, Michael Moore, 2020).
Utilitarianism: An action is right if and only if there among those available options for action there is
no other action that leads to a higher sum of utility. (Andersen, 2020a; Larry Alexander, Michael Moore,
2020).
Prioritanism, another consequentialist and distributive justice theory, states that when goods, such as welfare are
distributed, the most vulnerable individuals must be prioritized first (Andersen, 2020b).
According to Prioritarianism, the ethical value of an individual receiving more welfare diminishes as welfare grows.
The greater the individual’s welfare, the less ethical value is added for that individual. Thus, the ratio of ethical
value to welfare is not one to one but appears concave. The ethical value of welfare decreases slightly concisely with
getting more welfare. This principle explains, why the theory of prioritarianism argues that the most disadvantaged
should be prioritized (Andersen, 2020b).
For a algorithm utilizing notions of Consequentialism, the goal would be to distribute some amount of total goods,
S, among a population of two people, Adam and Ben. Consequentialist approaches will produce a function that
accurately represents each possible distribution and its effects, in other words, a utility function: u (di) →(vi),
where (d1 . . . di) are vectors that represent possible allocations such that each vector sums to S, and (v1 . . . vi) are
measurements of well-being (Leben, 2020).
Then a utility function can thus be a measure of Adam and Ben’s utility, for the given distribution. This utility
function can be discounted by a coefficient, wi, that adjusts the utility of individuals suitably for their need or
relative value. With a suitably discounted utility function in hand, the goods can be distributed depending on if
either a utilitarianism or prioritarianism doctrine is implemented:
Utilitarian Principle (Maxisum):
UT (di) = arg max

Prioritarian Principle (Maximin):

A consequentialist will then use a model that optimizes the utility discounted by the weights. However how should
these weights be determined, and how can utility be measured? In ventilator systems, should only those with the
highest chance at recovery receive a ventilator, or should the model aim at saving the youngest or sickest first? Thus
fairness in consequentialist approaches is demonstrated by the way weights are assigned to each group outcome
according to its proportionate social cost, which will be discussed in Section 7.1 and 7.2.
However, a clear limitation of utilitarianism can be highlighted by going back to a previous example. For instance,
an algorithm constructed on a dataset of breast cancer patients comprised of men and women in which 99% of men
were negative for breast cancer, and using accuracy to measure performance, should raise concern. Even when
Page 33 of 136

the model fails to predict any cases of breast cancer among men its accuracy is still 99%, as data contain 99%
non-breast cancer among men. From a Utilitarian viewpoint, can be deemed fair, as it places a premium to the
maximum possible sum of welfare, for everyone who can experience welfare. Thus as only 1 % of patients did not
get their treatment, it is arbitrary (Leben, 2020).
4.3
Formal equality of opportunity
To be considered equal in opportunity (EO), a benefit must theoretically be available to everybody, regardless of
their background. For instance, a treatment, or a ventilator Formal EO requires that it should available for patients,
and must not discriminate on gender and ethnicity (Arneson, 2015). The distribution of ventilators is an example
of where formal equality of opportunity is not breached. Indeed, all patients admitted to the hospital are considered
for ventilator treatment; those who receive one are chosen solely on the basis of ’merit’ (Arneson, 2015). How-
ever, do all individuals truly have a chance to compete for the ventilators? This is the flaw of formal equality of
opportunity: it is purely formal, ignoring policies and practices that have an indirect effect on persons as a result of
their differences in circumstances. Assume a hospital evaluates applications depending on whether they live in a
certain neighborhood, as in Section 3.6.2. Such a decision could have a great impact on the residents of that neigh-
borhood, and for a machine learning system practicing this, it could lead to the exclusion of unprivileged groups.
Consequently, Indirect discrimination is not regarded for Formal EO. However, this effect is morally justified in the
context of Formal EO (Arneson, 2015).
4.4
Substantive equality of opportunity
Substantive EOP requires not only formal equality of opportunity for access to healthcare but also fair equality of
opportunity (Arneson, 2015).
According to substantive theory, access to qualifications should not be contingent on irrelevant or involuntary vari-
ables, such as gender or socioeconomic status. This concept is inextricably linked to that of indirect discrimination:
A decision-making process that discriminates indirectly against members of a protected class (e.g., ethnicity (im-
migrants), women) is frequently seen to violate substantive equality of opportunity (Heidari et al., 2018).
As such, a critical step toward achieving meaningful equality of opportunity in machine learning is to analyze the
correlations between the sensitive attributes (gender, ethnicity, etc.) and other factors in the dataset, so avoid the
use of proxies to determine sensitive attributes. Two commonly accepted ideals pertain to substantive opportunity
equality: Rawlsian and Luck Egalitarian (Arneson, 2015).
4.4.1
John Rawls’s egalitarian liberalism
John Rawl is inspired by Kant’s second statement of the categorical imperative: People must be viewed as ends
in themselves. According to Rawls, this is grounds to reject utilitarianism, as it allows for the sacrifice of humans
if the benefits to others are sufficiently substantial. Conversely, Rawls lacks Nozick’s absolute trust in voluntary
interactions. The distribution of resources within a society is largely determined by the lots drawn by individual
members, which he refers to as the natural and social lottery: individuals’ wealth or education their parents, their
intelligence, and so on. According to Rawl they are, to some degree, bestowed upon us through a social and natural
lottery (Julian Lamont, 2013; Rodrigues, 2014. Rawls applies contract theory to this intuitive reasoning:
According to Rawls, society should be organized in such a way, in which self-interested individuals would agree,
in what Rawls refers to as the original position, under the veil of ignorance. According to Rawl, these are the
circumstances in which individuals are aware of their presence within society but unaware of how society is con-
structed, and their role owns within that society. The veil of ignorance deprives all information about citizens that
are irrelevant to the selection of justice principles: not just about their race, class, and gender, but also facts about
age, natural endowments, and so forth (Andersen, 2020b; Julian Lamont, 2013). One is obscured by ignorance and
hence unaware of role within society, as these characteristics are bestowed upon us by the social and natural lottery.
As such, they are ethically arbitrary, and their distribution should not dictate the distribution of resources. Thus,
the contract, i.e. a just society and legislation, is the consequence of the deliberations of free and self-interested
individuals who are unaware of their lot is neither the natural nor social lottery (Andersen, 2020b; Julian Lamont,
2013). Thus the contract, or just society, is the result of free and self-interested individuals’ The contract, ie. the
just society is the result of the deliberations of free and self-interested individuals who do not know their lot in
the natural or social lottery (Andersen, 2020b; Julian Lamont, 2013). Rawls’ understanding of the original posi-
tion is that free and self-interested persons will pursue a maximal strategy, that is, they will maximize, or strive
Page 34 of 136
==PAGE:36==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
to maximize, the outcome in which they are worst off in society. These guiding concepts of justice as fairness are
institutionalized through the institution’s two justice principles, following the lexical priority.
1. Liberty: The most fundamental concept necessitates that all individuals have an equal entitlement to funda-
mental liberty. Individuals have the right to own property, speak freely, congregate, worship, and exchange
ideas. These rights, however, do not guarantee unrestricted practice. For instance, an absolute right to unlim-
ited private property is not a form of fundamental liberty, whereas possessing private property is. (Andersen,
2020b; Julian Lamont, 2013)
2. Equality: Rawls establishes the equality principle for evaluating how social and economic inequalities must
satisfy two conditions:
(a) Equal Opportunity: This means that every individual should have equal access to offices and jobs, or
other opportunities under the conditions of fair equality of opportunity; Those with comparable levels of
talent and ability, as well as a willingness to employ them, should have a comparable prospect of success
regardless of arbitrary factors such as the socio-economic background. There shall be no discrimination
or differential treatment of opportunities on the basis of race, religion, ethnic origin, sexual orientation,
or any other feature. Rawls felt that everyone should not only have an equal right to opportunities but
also an equal chance at those opportunities compared to other similar individuals.
(b) The Difference Principle: The difference principle is a strategy for resolving inequities by benefiting
the least-advantaged members of society (the difference principle) In so doing. Rawls believed that the
benefit felt by those who would receive it would also filter upward, thus eradicating inequality.
Thus According to Rawls’s Equality of Opportunity (R-EOP), inherent ability and ambition can justify social in-
equality, but circumstances of birth and upbringing such as sex, race, and social background can not. Thus Rawlsian
EOP presupposes that effort is equivalent amongst individuals and, more specifically, that level of effort is unaf-
fected by circumstance and policy implementation. It requires that individuals exerting comparable effort must
have the same distribution of utility regardless of circumstance (Heidari et al., 2018).
Thus, from Heidari et al., 2018 Rawls Equality of Opportunity (R-EOP) can be mathematically formulated as: A
policy ϕ satisfies Rawlsian EOP if for all circumstances c, c′ and all effort levels e,
F ϕ(. | c, e) = F ϕ (. | c′, e) .
As mentioned the conception of R-EOP takes an absolutist view of effort: it assumes e is a scalar whose absolute
value is meaningful and can be compared across individuals. This view requires effort e to be inherent to individuals
and not itself impacted by the circumstance c or the policy ϕ. Substantive EOP is sometimes compared with Rawls’
fair EOP, which asserts that all persons, regardless of their birth circumstances, should have equal opportunity to
develop their capabilities to allow those with similar abilities and motivation to have equal possibilities (A. Carey
and Wu, 2022). Using the notion of Heidari et al., 2018 it allows for Rawlsian EOP to be translated into a case of
supervised learning.
Before doing so the utility that an algorithmic decision imparts may be described as follows, as described by Heidari
et al., 2018, by combining the notions from Section 3.2. X are relevant attributes (e.g. those reasons for making
decisions), A are irrelevant attributes (e.g. race), Y is the target variable or true label.
• d ∈[0, 1] is an individual’s effort-based utility. This is the utility they receive based on relevant factors made
by the individual. This utility is not directly observable.
• a ∈[0, 1] is the actual utility the individual receives subsequent to receiving allocation ˆy.
• u is the utility or advantage the individual earns as a result of being subject to the decision C, which assumes
u = a −d
• ˆY = C(X, A) is an allocation by a classifier. (From earlier sections R is used, however, they are the same)
For Rawlsian EOP it is then required that the effort d is not affected by irrelevant features A and the specific
decision C. Let F C(.) be the distribution of utility u across individuals under the allocative decisions made by C.
The Rawlsian EOP translates to:
F C(. | A = a, D = d) = F C(. | A = b, D = d)
Page 35 of 136
==PAGE:37==

February 13, 2022
That is, for fixed effort d, the distribution of utility does not depend on circumstance A (Heidari et al., 2018). The
fairness metrics as described in the coming sections can be naturally described using Rawlsian Fair Equality of
Opportunity, for different choices of effort.
Following the lexical priority of Rawls’s principle, the last part of Rawls’s EOP is the Difference Principle, which
allows for social and economic inequality to be acceptable only if the most vulnerable are placed in the best possible
situation (4.4.1).
The Difference Principle mandates that any inequalities in a resource distribution benefit the persons who are least
well-off (maximally beneficial) in terms of their resources, which according to some egalitarian philosophers, would
still be cast as adhering to the spirit (but not the letter) of EOP. To do this, the rule maximizes (over all conceiv-
able resource distributions) and minimizes (over all individuals) each individual’s resource allocation. Since it
"maximizes the minimum," the Difference Principle is frequently referred to as the maximin rule (Heidari et al.,
2018). When operationalized in a machine learning model, the model is optimized to allocate a "fairer" quantity of
accuracy it desired output. This is accomplished by e.g. allocating less accuracy to the majority group and more
accuracy to the minority group, subject to the Difference Principle constraints if accuracy was the goods being
distributed (Hashimoto et al., 2018).
4.5
Luck Egalitarianism
Luck egalitarianism is a position on distributive justice held by several egalitarian and other political theorists. It
has its offsprings from John Rawls, however, Luck egalitarianism differs on a principle that is opposed to Rawls’
theory of justice, which is that according to luck egalitarianism, it is unjust if some people are worse off than
others through no fault of their own. (brute unluck) (Lippert-Rasmussen, 2001). Rawls does not go far enough in
addressing variables that unfairly advantage or disadvantage individuals, and according to Dworkin, 1981, a person’s
outcomes should be determined solely by effort or "chosen luck" (responsible choices). There are no consequences
of situation or "brute luck, from having rich parents to getting born with a disability — should influence the outcome
of fair competition (Dworkin, 1981). However, the difficult question is how to disentangle the consequences of
brute luck from those of responsible choices. Roemer, 2002 provides an operationalized version of EOP, which
has been codified for supervised learning by Heidari et al., 2018. Roemer acknowledges that an individual’s effort
is influenced by their environment. Instead of attempting to disentangle the effects of brute and choice luck on
a candidate’s qualifications, Roemer’s EOP doctrine asserts that for certain aspects of brute luck — such as race,
gender, and disability — that is known to affect a person’s access to opportunities to develop qualifications M.
Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021, should be controlled. Thus for Roemer’s EOP, a fair competition would be by
classifying candidates according to their effort-circumstance distribution—by comparing individuals to others in
similar situations (Roemer, 2002). Thus luck Egalitarianism maintains that unintended consequences cannot justify
inequities. As a result, society is responsible for compensating individuals for features of their disadvantaged
condition that they did not choose (Binns, 2018).
Heidari et al., 2018 translate the Luck Egalitarian Equality of Opportunity into an instance of supervised learning:
A policy ϕ satisfies Luck Egalitarian EOP if for all π ∈[0, 1] and any two circumstances c, c′ :
F ϕ(. | c, π) = F ϕ (. | c′, π)
Notice the difference between Rawls Equality of Opportunity and Luck Egalitarian Equality of Opportunity, which
the Rawls EOP assumes e is a scalar whose absolute value is meaningful and can be compared across individuals,
whereas compared to Luck Egalitarian Equality of Opportunity, which considers individuals environment (circum-
stance) when comparing effort levels.
The difference between luck egalitarian and Rawlsian equality of opportunity.
Assume that we treat years of schooling as effort and gender as a circumstance when making career selections. As-
sume Sofie and Ben both have a five-year education, while Anna and Adam have three and seven years, respectively.
Rawlsian EOP requires that Sofie and Ben have equal employment opportunities, ensuring that factors such as gen-
der do not adversely affect Sofie’s work opportunities (compared to Bob). However, for Luck egalitarian EOP, there
is more to it, luck egalitarian goes a step further and equates prospects based on luck and rank (in terms of years of
education) among all applicants of their gender. This is done by first ranking individuals within their gender group,
then equalizing prospects based on ranks. Sofie is ranked first in our case, whereas Anna is placed second. Ben is
ranked second, while Adam is ranked first. A luck egalitarian EOP policy would ensure that Sofie and Adam have
comparable work prospects and opportunities. As it can be seen in this scenario, the luck egalitarian assigns person
Page 36 of 136
==PAGE:38==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Ben into a less desirable position compared to Sofie even though he has the same effort level (years of education)
as individual Sofie (Andersen, 2020b).
Anderson, 1999 makes the most well-known case against Luck Egalitarianism’s harshness, in her work What is the
point of equality? She asserts that Luck Egalitarians oppose help to individuals who voluntarily place themselves
in unequal positions, as this is a result of personal action. If a resident chooses to live in a neighborhood with
mortality, to make a positive difference to the community, should that individual not be compensated? Another
criticism of Luck Egalitarianism is that it allows discrimination for individuals who are held accountable for their
impairment, compared to those who have the same disability but who are not held accountable. Treating two people
with the same disability differently based on their prior choices does not appear to be consistent with justice and
fairness and may be referred to as absolute discrimination (Anderson, 1999). Thus often highlighted critique of
Luck Egalitarianism is the difficulty to disentangle the consequences of brute luck from those of responsible choices.
The notions mentioned above demonstrate that there is no clear definition of what constitutes fairness, by choosing
either notion of Rawls, Luck Egalitarianism, Utilitarianism, libertarianism, or prioritarianism. However, as the
next section will demonstrate, ensuring fairness, is not the only critical step when designing artificially intelligent
systems.
4.6
Level of Inequalities
When algorithms make decisions, they frequently differ between groups and people based on characteristics related
to the desired outcome.
For instance, a patient who maintains a more healthy lifestyle may be more likely to obtain treatment as a result
of the patient’s link with a larger potential for survival. In this scenario, when it comes to health distribution,
disparities in lifestyle are accepted as inequity. Previously, scholars distinguished between "acceptable" and "un-
acceptable" inequalities based on legal precedents between "explainable" and "non-explainable" discrimination, as
detailed in Section 3.5, and on Rawlsian philosophy’s distinction between "relevant" and "irrelevant" features. For
instance, a healthy lifestyle may be deemed "relevant," but gender or ethnicity may be deemed "irrelevant." While
the former should have an impact on algorithmic decisions, the latter should not. Scholars attempting to codify
these requirements into a mathematical definition of fairness have had to address the question of what constitutes
a fair level of equality. Certain individuals believe that any disparity in a given outcome metric is undesirable; for
instance, treatments administered to men and women should be the same. Others presuppose a fair playing field:
for example, no gender or racial discrimination exists in the actual world that could alter the statistics. Whatever
notion of belief, 5 emphasizes the different levels of inequalities, (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021).
