Article 21 of the European Union’s Chapter of Fundamental Rights prohibits discrimination based on dimensions
such as sex, race, color, ethnic or social origin, genetic characteristics, language, religion or belief, political, mem-
bership in a national minority, property, birth, disability, age, or sexual orientation (Council of Europe, Human
Rights, and Fundamental Rights, 2019). Thus, protected groups would include people of color, women, as well as
intersections along these axes, such as female people of color (Council of Europe, Human Rights, and Fundamental
Rights, 2019)
4.7
Ethical principles for the adoption of socially beneficial AI
AI is already having a significant impact on society. As a response, numerous organizations have launched a variety
of projects aimed at establishing ethical guidelines for the use of socially beneficial artificial intelligence. Regret-
tably, the sheer volume of proposed ideas risks overwhelming and perplexing. Floridi and Cowls, 2019, examined
many of the most prominent principles of ethical standards for artificial intelligence, established by entities such
Page 37 of 136
==PAGE:39==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
AI4People (EU initiative), IEEE among others. In total there are 47 principles contained in the six high profile doc-
uments, driven by experts, established in the interest of socially beneficial AI, which has been analyzed. The team
wanted to investigate whether there was a convergence of ideas, towards a set set of universally accepted principles
(Floridi and Cowls, 2019). Their study revealed a substantial degree of convergence between the set of principles,
which led the authors to encapsulate the set of principles, to an overarching framework for ethical AI, comprised of
five basic concepts. Four of those are fundamental bioethical principles, composed by Beauchamp and. Childress:
beneficence, non-maleficence, autonomy, and fairness, with the addition of explicability. The Four Principles of
Beauchamp and Childress are a widely used paradigm that provides a comprehensive examination of medical ethics
issues in general (Beauchamp and Childress, 2013). The principles have over the passage of time gained acceptance
as important principles of ethics. Tom L. Beauchamp and James F. Childress argue that the principles are anchored
in common morality, that they represent fundamental moral values shared by all humans devoted to morality, and
hence are rooted in human moral psychology, thus making them applicable for broad-scale of ethics, (Beauchamp
and Childress, 2013), and thus applicable for social beneficial AI, as bioethics is the one that most closely resembles
digital ethics (Floridi and Cowls, 2019).
Below are the five ethical principles highlighted by Floridi and Cowls, 2019, for the adoption of socially beneficial
A
• Beneficence: When it comes to beneficence, the documents have a lot in common. The majority of them
argue that it is vital to use AI to increase human well-being and should do so for all sentient animals. Addi-
tionally, others contend that AI should secure the fundamental prerequisites for life on our planet, humanity’s
sustained prosperity, and the preservation of a healthy environment for future generations. Within benefi-
cence, various schools of thought exist, but they all believe that AI should "prioritize human well-being as
an outcome in all system designs." (Floridi and Cowls, 2019).
• Non-maleficence: Non-maleficence is the principle of doing no harm, however different from beneficence,
of doing only good. A particular concern is the prevention of invasions of personal privacy is a primary
priority, which is a principle highlighted in five of the six high-profile initiatives. Moreover, some of the
initiatives raise concerns of the misuse in terms of the AI arms race, while others stress the fact that AI
should be contained “within secure constraints”. Others raise concern against the risk of those developing AI
and warn that developers “should assume their responsibility by working against the risks arising from their
technological innovations”. At the heart of this problem is the issue of autonomy (Floridi and Cowls, 2019).
• Autonomy: By embracing AI and its intelligence agency, it voluntarily relinquishes some of the decision-
making authority to technical artifacts. Thus, affirming autonomy in the context of AI requires finding a
balance between the decision-making authority maintained for ourselves and that delegated to artificial agents.
This introduces concepts of ‘meta-autonomy’ or a ’decide-to-delegate’ model: humans should maintain the
ability to decide which decisions AI should delegate, or be in control over, while also having the ability to
ceding the decisions when necessary. Thus any delegation should be reversible in principle (i.e., deciding to
decide again) (Floridi and Cowls, 2019).
• Justice: The importance of ‘justice’ is highlighted in several of the initiatives while stressing different factors
of justice, or the lack of fairness. Some of the documents argue that AI should seek to eliminate any type of
discrimination, while others argue that AI should promote prosperity while contributing to global justice and
equal access to the benefits. Elsewhere, the term ’justice’ has other connotations (particularly in the sense
of fairness), all of which relate to the use of AI to right past wrongs such as removing unfair discrimination,
fostering diversity, and preventing the emergence of new threats to justice. Additionally, some of the doc-
uments caution against bias in datasets used to train AI systems and argue for the importance of defending
against threats to "solidarity," such as "systems of mutual help such as social insurance and healthcare." The
variety of ways in which justice is defined, from the documents, poses a greater lack of clarity about AI and
raises the question of whether ’we’ are AI’s patients or its doctors. This unclarity can be overcome only by
introducing a fifth principle derived from their investigation (Floridi and Cowls, 2019).
• Explicability: Defined as incorporating both the epistemological concept of intelligibility (as an answer to
the question ’how does it work?’) and the ethical concept of accountability (as an answer to the question
’who is accountable for the way it works?’). The last principle then complements the other four principles:
To achieve beneficent and non-maleficent AI, an understanding of the consequences AI is forcing society and
how is changing everyday life is necessary. In order to achieve autonomy, and “decision about who should
decide”, it is necessary to have an understanding of how AI would act instead of humans, in given scenarios.
To ensure that AI is just in the event of a major negative outcome, the accountable must be identifiable, which
Page 38 of 136
==PAGE:40==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
requires an understanding of why this outcome occurred (Floridi and Cowls, 2019).
Figure 9 illustrates this framework of principles.
Figure 9: an ethical framework of the five principles of AI. From Floridi and Cowls, 2019.
By using this framework, presented in the article, it can provide a coherent and sufficiently comprehensive overview
of the central ethical principles governing AI, and serve as a framework which within laws, rules, technical stan-
dards, and best practices can be developed. Both as an enabler in e.g., AI assisting in reaching United Nations
Sustainable Development Goals, but also constrainer e.g., use of AI to the aid of online crime and cyberwarfare
(Floridi and Cowls, 2019).
The framework is already being utilized and is instrumental in the work of AI4People, Europe’s first worldwide
forum on the social effect of AI, which recently adopted it in order to submit to the European Commission 20
concrete suggestions for a ’Good AI Society. Also, European Commission’s High-Level Expert Group on Artificial
Intelligence’s Ethics Guidelines for Trustworthy AI (HLEGAI 2018 and 2019), which has influenced the OECD’s
Recommendation of the Council on Artificial Intelligence (OECD 2019), was based on the same framework (Floridi
and Cowls, 2019).
The framework has immense potential for how AI should be used today. However, the guidelines are still normative,
it does not provide concrete answers to, what criteria and principles should be employed in determining what is
owed to individuals? Especially in regards to Justice What is a reasonable distribution of resources, and how can
it be implemented into algorithms? What is the notion of treating patients similarly? Equally good, equally bad,
the same level of treatments? These questions are mostly related to the principle of justice and fairness within AI,
which is also the focal point of the project. In order to mitigate fairness within AI, the notion of algorithmic fairness
is often used, which will be the topic of the next chapter.
4.8
Concluding remarks on chapter
The goal of this chapter was to elaborate on the theories of distribute justice and how they can be linked to notions of
machine learning, thus providing preliminary insights to the fourth research question of which ethical principles and
norms can be used to guide the design of fairness criteria for algorithmic classification. Moreover, the framework
presented in 4.7, provides initial insights to the research questions of how to ensure fair and responsible AI and
Machine learning for healthcare in practice.
In Section 4.1 notions of distributive justice are elaborated, and their link to machine learning. Each principle has
its advantages and disadvantages, and the different notions demonstrate that there is no clear definition of what
constitutes fairness, however there is a link between said principles and machine learning.
Scholars distinguished between "acceptable" and "unacceptable" inequalities based on legal precedents between
"explainable" and "non-explainable" discrimination, and on e.g. Rawlsian philosophy’s distinction between "rele-
vant" and "irrelevant" features, as detailed in Section 3.5. These notions are critical to consider when constructing
an algortihm, and will be used later in 7.3.
However, as demonstrated in 4.7, adhering to fairness is just one of the needed critical principles for the adoption
of socially beneficial AI. The framework is based on the work of Beauchamp and Childress bioethical principles,
and constitutes what developers are ought to ensure, when developing AI systems. The framework holds enormous
promise for how AI should be applied. Nonetheless, the rules remain normative; they do not provide precise answers
to the question of what ethical criteria and principles should be used to guide algorithms. Moreover, concrete notions
of fairness in machine learning are still unanswered. What is an appropriate resource distribution, and how can it
Page 39 of 136

be included in algorithms? To further elaborate on fair and ethical algorithmic classification, this will be the focal
point of the next chapter.

5
Fairness in Machine Learning
Fairness in machine learning is a relatively new topic of machine learning that focuses on ensuring that data biases
and model flaws do not result in models that discriminate against individuals based on their race, gender, disability,
sexual or political orientation, or any other sensitive attribute when allocating resources. Thus, fairness in machine
learning can be described as the to apply principles and concepts that attempt at correcting any algorithmic bias.
Consequently, when discussing the term fairness in machine learning, it is how a model allocates resources to
groups and individuals (Mehrabi et al., 2019).
However, it’s critical to remember that algorithms are a mirror of the data used to train them, which means that
biases propagate across the fairness pipeline: Biased data collection practices, as mentioned in Section 3.4.3, result
in biased datasets, which generate biased algorithms. Thus, it is important to manage bias, in both detecting it,
and mitigating it as early in the fairness pipeline as possible. However, before bias can be managed, it must be
quantified. As a result, several methods and measures have been developed to evaluate fairness and facilitate the
management of biases.
However, because humans have varying sets of moral principles and diverse cultural preferences and outlooks sup-
port disparate views on fairness, developing a single quantitative definition or metric that is acceptable to everyone
in a given situation is extremely difficult, highlighting the difficulty of resolving this issue (Suresh and Guttag,
2021). For instance, notions of what constitutes fairness, and what should be equal for patients can be argued to
differ for healthcare. Going back to 3.3.1, for the decision-maker, it could be that of those that have been labeled
eligible for a kidney transplant, how many did truly need it? For the patient, it could be the probability of being
correctly classified? For society, it could be if the selected set is demographically balanced? (Barocas, Hardt, and
Narayanan, 2018)? (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018). In computer science, the majority of work on proposing
new fairness constraints for algorithms comes from the western literature, and many of these papers demonstrate the
effectiveness of their constraints using the same datasets and problems, making it difficult to reach a clear consensus
on which constraints are the most appropriate for particular problems (Mehrabi et al., 2019).
Mathematical fairness criteria are often grouped into different categories, depending on how they each formalize
fairness, with the most commonly used as individual fairness, group fairness, and causality-bases fairness (Dwork
et al., 2011; Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018). Thus, following section will elaborate on the metrics for both
checking biases in fairness, as well as how these metrics can be used to mitigate any potential biases, throughout
the machine learning life cycle of Figure 4, thus providing answers to the second research question of what is fair
and ethical algorithmic classification, and what concepts of fairness in machine learning exist.
However, as will be demonstrated through this chapter, as well as the coming chapters, fairness metrics are often
not useful as direct guides to fairness mitigation (Simoiu, Corbett-Davies, and Goel, 2017).
5.1
Fairness Definitions
While developing fairness and non-discriminatory practices within AI is a natural aspiration, formal definitions
of fairness criteria are on the contrary more difficult. Fairness criteria can be linked with political philosophies,
utilitarian, prioritarian, or deontological theories, and several criteria for fairness have been proposed over the
years (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021). Mathematical fairness criteria are often grouped into different categories,
depending on how they each formalize algorithmic fairness. The most commonly used, and how they formalize
algorithmic fairness are described below:
• Individual Fairness: Individual fairness is encapsulated in the principle of similar individuals should face
similar decisions. This idea focuses on individual comparisons rather than groups of individuals who share
certain features (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
• Group Fairness: Distinguish from individual fairness, by focusing on treating different groups equally,
instead of individuals. Group fairness is the premise that groups of people are susceptible to bias and unfair
judgments, hence group fairness seeks to achieve equality of treatment for groups rather than individuals
(Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
• Causality-based fairness: Individuals who are members of a protected group should obtain the same out-
come as if their membership in the group were reversed in a counterfactual scenario, allowing for the resolu-
tion of problems such as "what would have been the decision if that individual had a different race?" (Barocas,
Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Page 41 of 136
==PAGE:43==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
The terms are elaborated on further below.
5.1.1
Causality-based fairness
Unlike individual fairness and observational fairness criteria of group fairness, counterfactual fairness is a subtype
of Causality-based fairness, enabling the potential to evaluate the impact of replacing solely the sensitive. Through
a causal graph, it provides a way of explaining the impact of bias (Makhlouf, Zhioua, and Palamidessi, 2020). This
necessitates the development of a (causal) model that learns the relationships between attributes and features to
understand how features will change as a result of the e.g., "gender swap." For instance, male-specific property
parameters such as weight and height should be adjusted.
One of the main flaws of counterfactual fairness is the difficulty of constructing an appropriate causal model or
adequately identifying the counterfactual context. The concept of counterfactual fairness is idealistic, as, in practice,
it is difficult to establish common ground on the shape of the causal graph and even more difficult to decide which
features to use once a graph is constructed. (A model may suffer a large loss of accuracy if all the correlated features
are eliminated) (Makhlouf, Zhioua, and Palamidessi, 2020). Finally, counterfactual reasoning can result in several
forms of biases, such as an incorrect assessment of the model’s conclusion when the outcome is already known
(Makhlouf, Zhioua, and Palamidessi, 2020).
5.1.2
Individual Fairness
The objective of individual fairness is to emphasize individuals rather than aggregate statistical measurements. Ac-
cording to Dwork et al., 2011, this means that "similar individuals are treated similarly". By this, they emphasize the
fact, that members of similar groups should be receiving the same outcome regardless of their group membership.
This is the notion of fairness through awareness.
However, the primary limitation of these techniques is the non-trivial task of defining a measure of individual
similarity. Consider three job candidates, A, B, and C. A holds a bachelor’s degree and has worked in a related
field for one year. B holds a master’s degree and has worked in a comparable field for one year. C holds a master’s
degree but has no experience in the field. Is A more closely related to B than C? If so, how much more? This is
a difficult question to answer because the performance of A, B, and C is not known. Numerous studies have been
conducted to attempt to soften the requirement, for example, by examining subpopulations or by ensuring that a
randomly chosen pair of individuals receive the same outcome if they are comparable (Caton and Haas, 2020).
5.1.3
Group Fairness
To a large extent, group fairness criteria often fall into one of three distinct categories Independence, Separation
and Sufficiency, which are inspired by the work of Hardt, Price, and Srebro, 2016, Verma and Rubin, 2018, and
Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018, and bear some resembles to the principles of distributive justice as presented
in Chapter 4. The interlink between fairness in machine learning and group fairness will be discussed in Section
7.1.
Their work addresses methods for defining and eliminating discrimination through the use of machine learning
measures. To more precisely define these measures, they are divided into three broad categories based on a pre-
dicted outcome Independence and anticipated and actual outcomes Separation and Sufficiency (Barocas, Hardt, and
Narayanan, 2018). Most work is related to Moritz Hardt and his colleagues, who proposed the idea of Separation,
and who is one of the key researchers within the use of Demographic Parity (Dwork et al., 2011, Hardt, Price, and
Srebro, 2016). Sufficiency or Predictive Parity is based on the work of Chouldechova, 2016. It could be argued that
other fairness criteria or metrics should be included, but from the work of Verma and Rubin, 2018, and Barocas,
Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018, it can be demonstrated that by using Independence, Separation, and Sufficiency, other
used metrics can be achieved with based on these, as illustrated in Table 6. While Independence is intrinsically
linked to what is referred to as Demographic or Statistical Parity, Separation is linked to Equality of Odds and its
loosened variant Equal Opportunity. Sufficiency is connected to the concept of calibration and Predictive Parity, or
its more strict variants, Predictive Positive (or negative) Parity (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018)
The majority of fairness criteria build on the notions defined in Section 3.2, as distributional features of a joint
distribution of the sensitive attribute A, the target variable Y , and the classifier or score function R. The sensitive
attributes differ depending on which attributes are defined as sensitive. However, legislation and rules govern,
which attributes must be protected by law are elaborated in Section 4.6.
The criteria Independence, Separation, Sufficiency are defined below from Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018.
Page 42 of 136
==PAGE:44==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Independence
Separation
Sufficiency
R ⊥A
R ⊥A | Y
Y ⊥A | R
Table 6: Non-discrimination criteria. From Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018
It is important to notice that these above-stated metrics can be used for defining, detecting, and eliminating discrim-
ination and biases in datasets and models.
While Independence relies exclusively on the distribution of characteristics and decisions, Separation and Suffi-
ciency criteria incorporate the target variable Y . This is critical to remember when navigating the menagerie of
fairness standards in a particular case study: When applying separation- or sufficiency-based criteria, care must be
taken to ensure that the target variable Y is not susceptible to prejudice and injustice.
To attain Demographic Parity, Equalized Opportunity, and Predictive Parity simultaneously is to some extent im-
possibly. As Section 5.3.6, will demonstrate, it has been established that the three fairness criteria are incompatible
with one another and, additionally, conflict with optimizing accuracy.
Despite its limitations, the strength of statistical measures outweighs its limitations, due to its ease of computation
and ease of implementation, for example, by altering the classifier’s threshold. In contrast to individual and counter-
factual fairness, neither verification nor adjustment requires data assumptions. However, selecting and interpreting
any of the metrics involves an understanding of the sources of biases, as well as an assessment and discussion of
whether adjusting the model to achieve parity is the desired goal (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
As the statistical approach focuses on groups’ averages, critics highlight that the approach potentially only ensures
fairness on a group average, and not for individuals or subgroups. An algorithm can occur independently group
fair, for example, when considering race and gender separately, but may not be intersectionally group fair, for ex-
ample, when considering racial and gender intersections, which were the case for facial recognition software as
it performed particularly poorly on black women, compared to other subgroups (Yang, Cissé, and Koyejo, 2020).
This phenomenon is termed fairness gerrymandering, which occurs when there exists unfairness over a small num-
ber of pre-defined groups. Achieving Intersectional fairness is frequently regarded as ideal, however unrealistic,
as it often is associated with significant statistical and computational difficulties, including data scarcity at the in-
tersections of minority groups and an exponentially large number of subgroups. Indeed, present approaches are
either based on brute force enumeration or on searching via a cost-sensitive classification issue, and intersectional
groups are frequently empty for finite samples (Yang, Cissé, and Koyejo, 2020). However, later in Section 6.2.3, it
is demonstrated how gerrymandering can be used, to detect unfairness.
As the goal of this statistical approach of employing fairness criteria is to increase fairness and equality for all
populations, Liu et al., 2019, discovered that adhering to Demographic Parity or Equal Opportunity could poten-
tially result in the protected group being worse off in the future in terms of their underlying credit scores, if not
careful when setting the threshold. Developing an accurate model to foresee the impact of decisions on population
outcomes, can assist in mitigating any unintended consequences of implementing fairness requirements. Liu et al.,
2019. Lastly, a broader notion of criticism comes from is V. Carey, 2020, and M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021, and
Simoiu, Corbett-Davies, and Goel, 2017 who emphasize that group fairness criteria cannot be utilized to determine
whether a model is stereotyping individuals of a group, and "To the extent that error metrics differ across groups,
that tells us more about the shapes of the risk distributions than about the quality of decisions. In particular, it is hard
to determine whether differences in error rates are due to discrimination or to differences in the risk distributions"
(Simoiu, Corbett-Davies, and Goel, 2017)
5.2
Achieving Fairness
Fairness is a nuanced concept that defies a one-size-fits-all answer, as will be demonstrated in Section 5.3.6. How-
ever, fairness in machine learning is twofold: (a) By using the metrics of Independence, Separation, and Sufficienty
it can be determined if outcomes systematically differ between demographic groups, due to any existing biases in
datasets and models. Is a model penalizing women? and (b) mitigating the potential biases and disparities through
the use of bias mitigation approaches, by adhering to the metrics of Independence, Separation, and Sufficiency
throughout the machine learning pipeline (Figure 4, when building a classifier (Castelnovo et al., 2021). These
three types of algorithms are referred to as pre-, in-, and post-processing. Several auditing tools use these steps,
such as IBM’s AI Fairness 360 toolbox Bellamy et al., 2019, which is a toolkit that can be used to audit models.
Page 43 of 136
==PAGE:45==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Such auditing tools are an example of how developers can ensure fairness throughout their model, in the given
scenario, which is also the tool used by Danish AI company 2021.ai 6.2.3.
The types of bias mitigation algorithms are determined by the point in the machine learning pipeline at which these
algorithms can intervene. Pre-processing can be employed if the algorithm is allowed to modify the training data. If
the learning procedure for a machine learning model can be changed, in-processing can be applied. If the algorithm
is unable to modify the training data, it must treat the learned model as a black box, and only post-processing can
be used. The processes distinguish between three distinct techniques as mentioned:
• Adjust the feature space to be uncorrelated with the sensitive attribute during pre-processing.
• When training the data, incorporate the constraint into the optimization process that generates a classifier
from the training data.
• Lastly is to adjust a learned classifier so that it is uncorrelated with the sensitive attribute during post-
processing.
Pre-processing strategies for bias avoidance are frequently data-driven. Certain aspects of the training data may
directly contribute to the model’s poor performance or unjust outcome. As a result, several pre-processing strategies
rely on changing the training set to address variants of dataset imbalances. Thus pre-processing is a collection of
techniques for the transformation of a feature space into a representation, that improves and is in adherence with the
desired fairness metric. Following the pre-processing transformation, any deterministic training process on the new
space will also satisfy the metric (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018.) This can be accomplished in a variety
of ways, including resampling rows of data, reweighting rows of data, reversing class labels between groups, and
deleting sensitive variables or proxies (Bellamy et al., 2019).
Attaining a group fairness metric during training can result in the highest utility, the classifier can be optimized
using this criterion. A drawback of this method is that access to the raw data and training pipeline is required.
Additionally, this approach, embeds for the forfeiting of some generality, as it is typically limited to particular
model classes or optimization problems (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018). A commonly used technique is
adversarial debiasing, presented by Zhang, Lemoine, and Mitchell, 2018, in which biases are mitigated, by adhering
to the definitions of groups fairness by Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018.
Post-processing is the technique aimed at adjusting a model’s outputs while leaving the underlying classifier and data
intact. The benefit here is straightforward: utilizing post-processing techniques, enables the model development
team to utilize any modeling approach they desire, without having to tweak the algorithm or retrain a new model to
make it fairer. Thus, by solely adhering to fairness metrics, post-processing methods are centered on the concept
of altering the outputs of an unfair model to make the final outputs more equitable. For instance, assume a model
to aid with credit risk assessment. The model is deemed accurate however, it is observed that it favors older loan
applicants over younger loan applicants. In post-processing, that classifier is then retained in its current state, but the
outcomes are tweaked to ensure that the overall acceptance rates are more equitable. If enforcing Independence,
then the model would vary the treatments across the groups to adhere to the desired outcome of Independence.
This means that instead of a single classifier threshold, there are group-specific thresholds (Barocas, Hardt, and
Narayanan, 2018). A commonly known postprocessing technique is by Hardt, Price, and Srebro, 2016, in which
the output labels are changed to optimize Equalized Odds, the relaxed version of Equal Opportunity. A classifier that
minimizes the expected cost of false positives and negatives while adhering to the specified fairness constraint as
Equalized Odds can be derived by specifying a cost for false negatives and positives, and by using the ROC curve,
thus retaining the classifier at its current state, but tweaking the outcome. Numerous post-processing strategies
share this fundamental structure: they leave the classifier and data alone and change the outputs solely in a group-
dependent manner (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
The advantage of post-processing is that it can be applied to any black-box classifier, regardless of how it operates
internally. No retraining is required, which is beneficial in complex training pipelines. When access to a trained
model is required but no control over the training process is available, this is frequently the only option. However,
post-processing advantages are also disadvantages, as they frequently result in a significant loss of utility (Barocas,
Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Whether used to measure and assess fairness, or to mitigate bias in models when necessary, the fairness metrics are
of vital importance, and below the metrics are explored to a deeper end.
Page 44 of 136
==PAGE:46==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
5.3
Fairness of a Score Function
5.3.1
Independence
The first criterium is Independence often denoted Demographic Parity, however other synonyms include statistical
parity, group fairness, and differential impact.
Independence requires that each section of a protected class (e.g., gender) receives the positive outcomes at an equal
rate, such as "gaining admission to a university," "obtaining a loan," or "being given treatment." Thus Independence
requires that sensitive attributes A are statistically independent of the score R.
Independence:
The random variables (A, R) satisfy independence if A⊥R. (Barocas, Hardt, and
Narayanan, 2018).
Under the condition of binary classification, independence can be formally expressed as:
P{R = 1 | A = a} = P{R = 1 | A = b},
As can be deducted from the equation above, the formula requires that for all groups a and b, the acceptance rate is
the same. Thus as it can be seen, from the Section 3.3 Independence is achieved, when the positive rates of Equation
2 are equal among groups a and b (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
By going back to the case of 3.3.1, Independence or Demographic Parity can be achieved. Figure 10 illustrates this
case
Figure 10: An illustration of a classification model for kidney transplant waiting list, which achieves Independence
or Demographic Parity. The positive rates are the same for the two groups. Adopted from Meredith Whittaker,
2021; Amalie Broggaard Pauli, 2021
As can be seen from Figure 10, independence is achieved, as the positive rates from equation 2 are the same across
two groups. As can be seen from Figure 10, the thresholds are different, indicating that the two groups do not have
the same base rates.
An alternative relaxation of independence can be formulated as:
P{R = 1 | A = a}
P{R = 1 | A = b} ≥1 −ϵ
This relaxed version is called the p%-rule (p being a parameter), and was defined by Zafar, Valera, Rodriguez
and Gummardi as a generalization of the 80% rule, disparate impact law, also known as the “Four-Fifths Rule” of
the U.S. Uniform Guidelines for Employee Selection Procedures. The rule prescribes that a selection rate for any
disadvantaged group must not be less than four-fifths of that for the group with the highest rate (Zafar et al., 2017)
Page 45 of 136
==PAGE:47==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
One of the benefits of Independence or Demographic Parity is that the approach can help enhance the minority
class’s image over time. This improvement is the result of the progressive closure of a "positive feedback loop"
and justifies the short- to medium-term deployment of demographic parity programs. Moreover, as independence
is independent of the target variable Y , no access to its data is required to measure and rectify bias. This makes this
strategy particularly well-suited for applications involving difficult-to-qualify targets (employment qualification,
credit default, or justice, for example) Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018.
Moreover, Independence can be linked to the idea of detecting indirect discrimination (See 3.5) defined by Euro-
pean anti-discrimination legislation. Simplified the rule states, that any rule that is openly neutral but significantly
more adversely impacts a protected group rather than another group in a similar scenario, is cast as being indirectly
discriminating (Council of Europe, Human Rights, and Fundamental Rights, 2019). By using the metric of Inde-
pendence or Demographic Parity, indirect discrimination can be detected. Going back to the example of Kidney
Transplants, imagine if higher eGFR scores are assigned to people living in certain regions of a city. If more non-
whites live in specific regions compared to whites, and non-white individuals received, on average, higher eGFR
scores compared to white people. This would be detected by using the metric of Independence (Caton and Haas,
2020).
Limitations of independence
One of the limitations of Independence is that if the protected group does differ from other groups, such that dif-
ferential risk is justifiable, under the notion of Independence this would be allowable. For the Kidney transplant,
imagine if a protected group, in general, possessed lower qualifications for a kidney transplant. The subpopulations
are inherently different, meaning different outcomes, which could be justifiable. (Caton and Haas, 2020). Although
the training data may be entirely representational of reality, it may not reach "demographic parity" for a variety
of socioeconomic reasons unrelated to the machine learning setup (some groups may be less qualified because of
poverty, poor healthcare, poor education, poorly funded schools, etc.). Thus, social inequality can be a contributing
factor to the absence of "demographic parity" at the machine learning system level (Caton and Haas, 2020).
Secondly, imagine a corporation that hires applicants from each group a and b. Group a is diligently vetted appli-
cants are selected at a rate of p >0, whereas in group b, the corporation hires carelessly selected applicants at the
same p rate. Even though both groups have identical acceptance rates, it is far more likely that unqualified appli-
cants will be chosen in one group than in the other. As a result, individuals of group b will appear to have performed
worse than members of group a in retrospect, generating a poor track record for group b, leading to reoccurring
criticism (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Lastly, the most vital critique is that as Demographic Parity is independent of the target variable, in the majority
of cases, where there are different base rates, Demographic Parity will end up rejecting optimal classifier R = Y
(Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018). From a healthcare perspective this would hardly make any sense, since
rejecting an optimal classifier, and letting in patients independent of a target variable, would hardly be desirable.
This could be an argument that Independence works as a metric to determine bias in datasets and models, rather
than a measure to adhere to.
5.3.2
Separation
Separation allows for correlation with a target variable, and thus addresses some of the limitations of Independence.
Correlation with the target variable, is not inconceivable, as e.g. hospitals might differ by groups when administer-
ing treatment: one group may have a higher rate of survival, and may argue that having different treatment rates for
these groups is a healthcare matter. Hypothetical case, but nonetheless underlines the idea of Separation (Barocas,
Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Separation: The random variables (R, A, Y ) satisfy separation if Y ⊥A | R (Barocas, Hardt, and
Narayanan, 2018).
This can be formulated as the probability of being predicted to belong to the same group(R), is equal for two indi-
viduals with different sensitive characteristics(A) given that they actually belong in the same group(Y ) In general,
under the separation criterion, it is permissible to have a correlation between the score and the sensitive attribute
to the extent that the target variable justifies it. In other words, separation allows violations of Independence if
it is justified by the desired outcome. Equivalently, disparities between groups with varying A values (male and
Page 46 of 136
==PAGE:48==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
female) should be entirely justified by the Y (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018). Under the condition of binary
classification, separation can be formally expressed as:
P{R = 1 | Y = 1, A = a} = P{R = 1 | Y = 1, A = b}
P{R = 1 | Y = 0, A = a} = P{R = 1 | Y = 0, A = b}
As can be deducted from the equation above, the formula requires that for all groups a and b, the true positive rate
or sensitivity of 3, P{R = 1 | Y = 1}, is the same, for separation to be achieved. (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan,
2018). Moreover, it requires the False Positive Rate Equation 8, the rate at which the classifier incorrectly assigns
positive outcomes to negative instances,P{R = 1 | Y = 0}, to be equal among groups as well. This notion is often
also called Equalized Odds (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018). A more relaxing version, which is often used,
is the Equal Opportunity which only requires the True Positive Rate to be the same across groups a and b, thus it
can allow for a stronger utility (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Figure 11: An illustration of a classification model for kidney transplant waiting list, which achieves the loser variant
of Separation, Equal Opportunity. The True Positive Rates are the same for the two groups. This figure is only an
example of how distribution can be, and only works for illustrative purposes. Adopted from Meredith Whittaker,
2021; Amalie Broggaard Pauli, 2021
As can be seen from Figure 11, the loser variant of Separation, Equal Opportunity is achieved, as the true positive
rates from Equation 3, are the same across the groups. As can be seen from 11, the threshold is changed, which has
a significant impact on the distributed waiting spots for the two groups, compared to Figure 10, as more patients are
added to the kidney transplant waiting list. However, there is a downside, as with more patients being added, more
False Positive Patients are being added as well, increasing the False Postive Rate significantly compared to that of
Figure 10.
As can be seen from Figure 11. Equal Opportunity has the potential to ensure equal treatment of subpopulations.
In addition, it sanctions laziness and focuses only on truly eligible patients which were one of the problems of
independence. Additionally, this formulation eliminates the issue of independence’s problem of rejecting the op-
timal classifier R = Y.. For Separation, an optimal classifier would be achievable, even with different base rates,
under the notion both false positive and false negative rates would be 0% for the entire population, and 0% for each
subpopulation, demonstrating that the optimal classifier can fulfill both Equalized Odds and Equal Opportunity
(Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Separation can either be used as a metric to determine the performance of a model or by using the formulations
of Hardt, Price, and Srebro, 2016, to achieve separation by post-processing a given score function. The post-
processing step makes use of the ROC, as mentioned in Section 3.3.1, by using the intersection of all of group-level
ROC curves. By remembering the notions in Section 3.3.1, the greater the AUC, the more accurate the model is at
differentiating between positive and negative classification. Thus within the constrained region of group-level ROC
curves, the optimal classifier yields the highest AUC:

==PAGE:49==
