Figure 12: ROC Curve or a score for each group a and b. From Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018
As demonstrated by the ROC curves in 12, the highlighted region, enclosed by the two lines a and b, is the feasible
region of trade-offs that can be achieved (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
5.3.3
Sufficiency
The third criterion formalizes that a score includes the sensitive characteristic, for the objective of predicting the
target variable. Sufficiency can be formulated as the conditional independence statement
Sufficiency: Random variables (R, A, Y ) satisfy sufficiency if Y ⊥A | R
It can be formulated as the probability of actually being in each of the groups(Y ) is equal for two individuals with
different sensitive characteristics(A) given that they were predicted to belong to the same group(R)
Under the condition of binary classification, sufficiency can be formally expressed as, given the binary case of
Y ∈0, 1.
P{Y = 1 | R = r, A = a} = P{Y = 1 | R = r, A = b}
When R distinguish between two values, this condition is recognized as requiring parity of positive/negative pre-
dictive values across all groups a, b, which leads to the terms of Positive Predictive Parity or Negative Predictive
Parity, and for Sufficiency, this is equivalent to satisfying both Positive and Negative Predictive Parity (Barocas,
Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
For Positive Predictive Parity or Negative Predictive Parity is the requirement of either Equation 5 or 6 to be equal
across group a or b, while for Suffcitncy is the requirement of both Equation 5 or 6 to be equal across a or b. Positive
Predictive Parity is achieved if, among all the recruited candidates, the same proportion of truly qualified applicants
is the same, while negative predictive parity if all the denied candidates the same proportion of truly non-qualified
applicants is the same. The notable difference between Separation and Sufficiency is then, that while Separation is
based on sensitivity, which identifies the rate at which observations from the positive class are correctly predicted,
Sufficiency is based on precision, which indicates the rate at which positive predictions are correct. Thus, predictive
parity should ensure that the algorithm selects candidates based on their actual qualifications (Barocas, Hardt, and
Narayanan, 2018).
Page 48 of 136
==PAGE:50==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Figure 13: An illustration of a classification model for kidney transplant waiting list, which achieves the loser variant
of Sufficiency, namely negative predictive parity. The negative predictive value was chosen, for conceptual reasons
for the questionnaire, as initial testing revealed, that positive predictive value and equal opportunity were hard to
distinguish. Here the term the negative predictive, has be renamed to Equalized Terms for arbitrary reasons. This
figure is only an example of how distribution can be, and only works for illustrative purposes.
As can be seen from Figure 13, the threshold of eGFR is adjusted to achieve Negative Predictive Parity, as the
probabilities is the same across the groups of patients who were not added, and who did not require a kidney
transplant either. From Equation 6, the fraction of Negative Predictive Values is the same across the two groups.
As the concept is similar to equality of opportunity, one of its practical advantages is that it validates the optimal
classifier R = Y , as the probabilities are equal to one in the event of perfect classification. Another advantage
is inclusiveness (mostly Positive Predictive Parity) as it essentially means that the odds of a recruited individual
succeeding are the same regardless of the subpopulation to which the individual belongs For Negative Predictive
Parity it can be argued that it ensures inclusions as well, as the probabilities if rejected, then not qualified are equal
across groups. (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
While it is frequently beneficial to think of sufficiency in terms of positive and negative predictive values, there is
an option that is equally as valuable. Indeed, sufficiency is intimately tied to a critical concept called calibration,
which will be explained next.
5.3.4
Calibration and Sufficiency
Calibration aims to improve our model so that the distribution and behavior of projected probabilities are comparable
to those observed in training data.
It is desirable in some applications to be able to understand the results of scoring functions as probabilities. In
formal terms, it can be stated that a score R is calibrated if we have the following for all score values r in the
support of R:
P{Y = 1 | R = r} = r
As stated above, the function implies that in the set of all instances with a predicted probability score of r the
probability of a positive instance should be equal across sensitive attributes. Thus a classifier is calibrated if both
protected and unprotected groups have an identical probability of genuinely belonging to the positive class for any
projected probability score r (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
For the case of Kidney Transplant Waiting List in Secition 3.3.1 then calibration implies that for any given risk
score, the probability of being truly eligible should be the same across the type groups. Thus for patients receiving
a risk score of 0.5, then the fraction of truly eligible patients should be equal. It does not need to be 0.5, it could be
any other. for black and white defendants (could then be higher than 0.5 (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Page 49 of 136
==PAGE:51==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Platt scaling is commonly used to achieve calibration. It uses the uncalibrated score, considers it as a single feature,
and fits it using a one-variable regression model against the target variable, based on this feature. In more technical
terms, given an uncalibrated score R, Platt scaling seeks to determine the scalar parameters a b that maximize the
sigmoid function (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
S =
1
1 + exp(aR + b)
fits the target variable Y with respect to the so-called log loss
−E[Y log S + (1 −Y ) log(1 −S)].
As is customary in supervised learning, this objective can be minimized given labeled instances selected from
(R, Y ) (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Group Calibration - Well Calibrated
A classifier is calibrated if both protected and unprotected groups have an identical probability of genuinely belong-
ing to the positive class for any projected probability score r. Group calibration goes a bit further, as subjects in
both protected and unprotected groups should have not only an equal probability of really belonging to the positive
class, but also a probability equal to R (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018):
P{Y = 1 | R = r, A = a} = r
If this requirement is met, calibration is likewise met automatically, though the converse is not true. The distinction
between calibration and Group Calibration is merely one of mapping; the scores of a calibrated predictor can be
turned to Group Calibration scores using a suitable transformation. Moreover, from Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan,
2018, they derive proof that calibration by group implies sufficiency. Sufficiency is just slightly less robust than
calibration by group in the sense that a simple renaming of score values transitions between properties. For the
case of Kidney Transplant Waiting List in Secition 3.3.1 then for the score to be well-calibration, among patients
receiving a risk score of 0.3, the probability of being truly eligible should be the same across the type groups, and
should it should be exactly that of 0.3. So 30Similarly, patients assigned the highest risk score of 0.9, and those
who are truly eligible within that category, should account for around 90% of that group. (Barocas, Hardt, and
Narayanan, 2018).
5.3.5
The shortcoming of the Fairness Metrics
The difference between Separation and Sufficiency compared to Independence is their reliability of the sensitives
attributes A and their corresponding intended outcomes Y , which is used when training the model, which is also
some of the metrics’ inherent flaws. By definition, the criterion presupposes that these desired results represent the
’ground truth’, which is often not the case.
However, there is a growing wariness that, even though fairness metrics are applied, there is a challenge in practice,
as true labels do not always accurately reflect/summarize accountability variables. They are, at best, a representation
of the current state of circumstances — which may be tainted by historical injustices and discrimination (M. Lee,
Floridi, and Singh, 2021).
Separation and Sufficiency do not take into consideration possible discrimination outside of the model. As protected
attributes as race, gender, class, etc., more or have almost always affected access to opportunities such as loans or
education, such discrimination may result in an unbalanced ratio between the privileged and unprivileged classes,
which will be replicated by a model satisfying separation or sufficiency (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021). In that
situation, by retaining the contrast, the model implementing Separation or Sufficiency may exacerbate this disparity
over time, creating a vicious spiral, and contribute to a negative feedback loop, as per Figure 8. (Landeau, 2020,
Zhong, 2018)
Moreover, it may not contribute to bridging the divide between two groups. Consider group A which is the privi-
leged group, consisting of 100 candidates and 58 of them are qualified, and group B the unprivileged group, which
likewise has 100 applicants but only two are qualified. If a university, corporation etc. accepts 30 applications and
ensures Equal Opportunity, 29 offers will be made to group A, whereas just one offer will be made to group B.
However, what if this gap, was due to systematic discrimination? (Landeau, 2020, Zhong, 2018).
Page 50 of 136
==PAGE:52==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
In many fields where concerns about algorithmic bias exist, including but not limited to healthcare, credit risk,
employment, and recidivism, there has frequently been a proven history of institutional and societal discrimination,
which may alter the underlying data through biases. (Card and Smith, 2020).
As mentioned Ledford, 2019 in Section 3.6.1, an algorithm was found to exhibit systematic discrimination against
black patients. Patients who self-identified as black were generally assigned lower risk scores than equally sick
white patients, due to algorithmic biases as the algorithm predicted health care costs, rather than illness when as-
signing risk scores. However, health care costs are not a neutral proxy for healthcare needs. This led to black
patients being less likely to be referred to programs that provide more personalized care.(Ledford, 2019. Moreover,
a recent study conducted by Stanford Center for Biomedical Informatics Research came to a similar conclusion.
"Striving for health equity requires designing policies that directly counteract the systemic factors that contribute
to health disparities, primarily structural forms of racism and economic inequality. By considering only changes to
observable properties of a model, evaluations of models using the group fairness framework ignore the inequities
in the data generating and measurement processes" (Pfohl, Foryciarz, and Shah, 2020). These examples demon-
strate that the application of machine learning to clinical decision-making has the potential to exacerbate existing
disparities in health, which applies to each group fairness metric, that relies upon Y (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh,
2021.), which is echoed by Friedler, Scheidegger, and Venkatasubramanian, 2016 that state "if the decision pipeline
contains structural bias, no [group fairness] mechanism can guarantee fairness".
5.3.6
The Impossible theorem of fairness
Each of the fairness measures has distinct advantages and disadvantages that vary according to the context in which
they are utilized. What could be done, though, was to deploy them all in order to reap the full benefits of the
metrics. The simple answer is that it is not conceivable except in trivial or degenerate cases, as indicated in the
following arguments from the literature, where (Chouldechova, 2016; Kleinberg, Mullainathan, and Raghavan,
2016; Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018), provide mathematical proof and S, 2020 derive a proof from a casual
perspective. An important notice is that the impossible theorem, is not the same as the impossibility theorem of
Arrow’s; the authors consistently choose to name it the “impossible theorem". The purpose of this subsection is
not to derive the full proof of why the fairness metrics can not hold simultaneously, as the proof can be found in the
literature mentioned. However, the section will elaborate on some of the proof, and due to the mutual exclusiveness,
it leaves open the possibility that meaningful trade-offs between these different criteria exist, which then opens up
the discussion of which to choose?
Independence (Demographic Parity) vs Sufficiency (Predictive Rate Parity)
To demonstrate how independence and sufficiency are mutually exclusive begins with a straightforward statement.
The only supposition required here is that the sensitive attributes A and the target variable Y are not independent
This is another way of saying that group membership affects the target variable’s statistics. This suggests that in the
binary scenario, one group has a higher rate of positive outcomes than the other meaning that there are different
base rates (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
Assume that A and Y are not independent. Then sufficiency and independence cannot both hold, which can be
proven by the contraction rule for conditional independence. This is derived from the basic definition to form a set
of rules governing statements of conditional independence, termed by Pearl and Paz, 1986.
A ⊥R and A ⊥Y | R
=⇒
A ⊥(Y, R)
=⇒
A ⊥Y.
A ⊥(Y, R) states that A is independent of the pair of random variables (Y, R). From the Decomposition rule
of Pearl and Paz, 1986, it allows for A ⊥(Y, R) to be decomposed to A ⊥(Y ). However dropping R does not
introduce a dependence between A and Y . By using this analogy, and using the contrapositive proof it derives the
following. Note that, when push ¬ is pushed into the brackets, then according to De Morgan’s law (S. L. Harris
and D. M. Harris, 2016), the propositions and conjunction/disjunction operators are negated, for the contrapositive
prove:
A ̸⊥Y
=⇒
A ̸⊥R
or
A ̸⊥R | Y
which means that if A and Y are not statistically independent, then sufficiency and independence cannot both
hold, as one of them has to be chosen to as proven above. Thus unless there is not an imbalance in the dataset,
Independence and Sufficiency can not hold.
Page 51 of 136
==PAGE:53==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Independence (Demographic Parity) vs Separation
In this situation, the statement is more contrived and makes the extra assumption that the target variable Y is binary.
Additionally, it requires that the score R is not unrelated to the target Y . This is a mild assumption, as any usable
score function should be correlated with the target variable, unless it a complete useless score function. Barocas,
Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018
The statement that needs to be proven is the following, that holds if A is independent of Y , and R is independent
of Y
If R ⊥A and R ⊥A | Y , then either A ⊥Y or R ⊥Y
Proposition 3 in Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018 is omitted in this section, however, it reveals that when Y
is binary, this equation can only be satisfied if A ⊥Y or R ⊥Y . Thus, the only way to achieve both separation
and independence, is if either the model is useless R ⊥Y , or A ⊥Y , implying that there are equal base rates.
However, this is only under the assumption that the target variable can assume two values.
Separation versus Sufficiency
Lastly, it is the connection between separation and sufficiency. Both require a non-trivial connection of condi-
tional independence between the three variables A, R, and Y . As the following assertion affirms, imposing both
concurrently results in a degenerate solution space (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
If it is assumed that all events from the joint distribution of A, R, and Y have a positive probability, and A is
dependent on Y , then separation and sufficiency cannot both hold.
A ⊥R | Y
and
A ⊥Y | R
=⇒
A ⊥(R, Y )
which implies,
A ⊥(R, Y )
=⇒
A ⊥R
and
A ⊥Y
Taking the contrapositive completes the proof:
A ̸⊥R and A ̸⊥Y
=⇒
A ̸⊥(R, Y )
=⇒
A ̸⊥R | Y
or
A ̸⊥Y | R
The literature is primarily concerned with the proof for Separation versus Sufficiency, in which Chouldechova, 2016
and Kleinberg, Mullainathan, and Raghavan, 2016 provide extensive proof. However, as they are each mutually
exclusive, leaves open the possibility that meaningful trade-offs between these different criteria exist, which then
opens up the discussion of which to choose? For separation and sufficiency, it leaves open the possibility that
meaningful trade-offs between these different criteria exist, which then opens up the discussion of which to choose?
A well-known relationship between true positive rate (recall) and positive predictive value (precision). This trade-
off is often called a precision-recall trade-off. Other trade-off exists as well, depending on the use case. However,
often the trade-off conflicts with accuracy.
5.4
Concluding remarks on Fairness in Machine Learning
This chapter aimed to provide insights into the second research question of what is fair and ethical algorithmic
classification, and what concepts of fairness in machine learning exist.
Fair machine learning models can be described as the absence of bias or partiality in decision-making for an in-
dividual or group based on their intrinsic or acquired attributes. Thus, an unfair algorithm is one that is biased in
favor of a particular set of people (Mehrabi et al., 2019). While fairness is a highly desirable quality in society, it
can be rather challenging to accomplish in practice. By using relevant literature, three overarching concepts of fair
machine learning were defined, described, and discussed, and how they could be used as metrics for performance
evaluation combined with the Kidney Transplant Waiting List example of Section 3.3.1, but also how they could be
computed into the model when developing.
However, defining fairness in an algorithm is difficult due to the lack of a universal law of fairness, and recent
research has revealed that many of these notions of fairness are flawed, either because they contradict the system’s
original objective - i.e. they reduce predictive accuracy - because they do not conform to legal or moral notions of
fairness, or because they contradict one another (Yang, Cissé, and Koyejo, 2020; Liu et al., 2019; V. Carey, 2020).
When applying separation- or sufficiency-based criteria, care must be taken to ensure that the target variable Y is not
susceptible to prejudice and injustice. Unfortunately, as Section 5.3.5, there is a growing wariness that, even though
Page 52 of 136
==PAGE:54==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
fairness metrics are applied, there is a challenge in practice, as true labels do not always accurately reflect/summarize
accountability variables. They are, at best, a representation of the current state of circumstances — which may be
tainted by historical injustices and discrimination (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021), as demonstrated in countless
examples.
Nonetheless, if cognizant of target variable Y , another shortcoming is imposed by the impossible theorem of Section
5.3.6, which demonstrates multiple criteria can not be imposed as hard constraints simultaneously, which however
leaves open the possibility that meaningful trade-offs between these different criteria.
When developing fair machine learning models, it is vital to recognize that fairness comes with a cost and it may
conflict with model accuracy. Often two extremes can occur: a model that is as accurate as possible may be highly
unfair to at least one group, as presented in 3.3, while a model that treats everybody equally, regardless of their
qualifications would be highly inaccurate (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018). Figure 14 demonstrates this
example. By imposing more fairness by adding more restrictions to a model, it imposes a trade-off with accuracy.
What is the optimal balance?
Figure 14: An illustration of the trade-off between accuracy and demographic parity. From Zafar et al., 2017
Between these two extremes exists a diverse range of alternative models that strike a balance between accuracy
and fairness. This collection of models defines a Pareto frontier (most efficient frontier) in the space Accuracy vs
Fairness. Practitioners, developers, and stakeholders must address the concerns about these tradeoffs: for each use
case, the costs of potential harm due to unfairness and the costs of potential harm due to diminished accuracy must
be balanced. (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018).
As there is no universally accepted definition of fairness in machine learning, and since multiple fairness metrics
can not be attained simultaneously, there is frequently a trade-off between which principle to implement. However,
how can this trade-off be determined, and does the set of fairness metrics conform to moral notions of fairness from
Chapter 4
Page 53 of 136
==PAGE:55==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
6
Quantitative and qualitative analysis of Fairness in Machine Learning
The previous chapter elaborated on the different fairness metrics for machine learning. The metrics can be used
to determine models performance, which later can be mitigated either by pre-processing, in-processing, or post-
processing, with adherence to the metrics. However, as biases and disparities can be addressed, it is dependent
on the fairness measure implemented. As the impossible theorem of Section 5.3.6 demonstrated only one of the
metrics can be achieved, and they do not hold simultaneously, which opens up the notion of which fairness measure
should then be implemented?
As humans have different sets of moral principles, and diverse cultural preferences and outlooks support varying
viewpoints on fairness, a single quantitative definition or metric that is acceptable to everyone in a given situation,
is highly complex, which underscores the difficulty of resolving this issue (Suresh and Guttag, 2021). For instance,
notions of what constitutes fairness, and what should be equal for patients can be argued to differ for healthcare.
Going back to 3.3.1, for the decision-maker, it could be that of those that have been labeled eligible for a kidney
transplant, how many did truly need it? For the patient, it could be the probability of being correctly classified? For
society, it could be if the selected set is demographically balanced? (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018)? More-
over, as demonstrated in Chapter 4, notions of fairness are heavily based on context and culture, which developers
should be cognizant of. As algorithms have a direct impact on our daily lives, it is critical to not only grasp when
and what algorithmic fairness is, but algorithms and models should also to some extent reflect what individuals
believe constitutes fairness.
However, as fairness metrics are very dependent on individuals’ ethical views of fairness, it begs to question of
how may we elicit people’s perceptions of algorithmic fairness? To address this a questionnaire is conducted in
Section 6.1 to uncover individuals’ beliefs towards what principles of fairness for machine learning from Chapter
5 algorithms should adhere to. This section will thus provide answers to the third research question.
The questionnaire is then supplemented with interviews with five domain experts which will provide detailed in-
sights on why injustice and biases occur in the machine learning life cycle, thus expanding the answers to the first
research questions. Moreover, the interviews will provide context for a discussion about which fairness indicators
are most important in specific situations, how disparities may be desirable and if not, then how they can be resolved
and mitigated. Lastly, the interviews will give insights on how to establish fair and responsible AI in healthcare,
providing preliminary answers to the fifth research question. The interviews can be found in Section 6.2.
6.1
Questionnaire
The case study used for the questionnaire is based on the Kidney Transplant example in Section 3.3.1. The full
questionnaire can be found in Appendix (10.2). .
The questionnaire was based on dilemmas between Demographic Parity (Independence), Equal Opportunity (Loser
variant of Separation), and Negative Predictive Parity (Loser variant of Sufficiency). Equal Opportunity was chosen,
as it would highlight the benefits of adhering to true positive rates. The negative predictive value was chosen,
for conceptual reasons for the questionnaire, as initial testing revealed, that positive predictive value and equal
opportunity were hard to distinguish. Here the term the negative predictive has been renamed to Equalized Terms
for arbitrary reasons.
The analysis of the questionnaire showed that respondents tend to differ their answers, for what is fair, and what they
would recommend for hospitals to implement, for all of the criteria. For the scenario between Demographic Parity
(Independence) and Equal Opportunity (Separation), there was an even split between the two, regarding which one
was fairer. However, regarding which one to implement at the hospital, this changed to 70% in favor of demographic
parity. A cause of this was for one of the the respondents due to: "Equal opportunity has better true positive rates
and negative predictive rates." (10.2).
This tendency was also reported for Equalized Terms(Sufficiency) and Equal Opportunity(Separation), where the
initial split was 33% and 66% in favor of Equal Opportunity, which changed to 60% and 40%, regarding what
criterium to implement at a hospital, with one of the respondents stating: "Maximizing the impact of the transplants
seems fairer, so the equal opportunity is the more fair choice". (10.2).
Moreover, this was also the case of Demographic Parity (Independence) and Equalized Terms (Sufficiency), which
had an initial split of 82% to and 18% in favor of Demographic Parity when determining which one was more fair,
which changed to 64% and 36% for hospitals (10.2).
Page 54 of 136
==PAGE:56==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
However, the questionnaire holds some uncertainty in terms of the answers. When compared directly, Demographic
Parity (Independence), both in general and when determining which one the implement at the hospital, was chosen
as the superior alternative when compared with Equalized Terms(Sufficiency) and Equal Opportunity(Separation).
However, these answers are inconsistent when compared to the median score, of some of the later questions. For
Demographic Parity(Independence) and Equal Opportunity(Separation) they had a median of 5.125 and 5.28, re-
spectively on the Likert scale of a total of 7. (The mean was not calculated, as we do not have a normal distribution).
There are inconsistencies in the answers, that cannot be neglected. Thus, the validness of the questionnaire is de-
batable. Moreover, a goal of the questionnaire was to confirm or deny the hypothesis, that respondents’ attitudes
to the fairness of different metrics of algorithms for fair machine learning, differed across work domains or other
features, however, as too few responses were collected this can neither be confirmed nor denied.
Due to a lack of responses, as well as the inconsistent of the answers, the questionnaire can only give preliminary
insights and a general idea of potential considerations that need to be mitigated, as the difference in answers are
most likely due to coincidence. However, the questionnaire thus give some indication, that a was is fair, and what
is best for a hospital is different, a opens up the possibility of trade-offs. The lack of responses highlights one of the
shortcomings of this project. A lot of time was used to develop the questionnaire, which only gave a few responses.
This is mostly due to the lack of pushing the questionnaire out in the network. If it had been developed earlier,
it would allow for more time for gathering answers. However, due to the lack of resources, it was deemed more
necessary to focus on the report, than pursue more answers.
As both the questionnaire and Section 5.3.6 give the indication that a trade-off may be the best solution, this notion
was analyzed to a greater extent, by interviewing domain experts regarding trade-offs for algorithms. Moreover, as
fairness is only one pillar of the framework in 4.7, the experts were also questioned regarding the considerations
needed when constructing algorithms for healthcare.
6.2
Interviews with domain experts
In the United States, I spoke with Leo A. Celi of Beth Israel Deaconess Medical Center in Boston and an AI re-
searcher at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology to give insight on the issues they encounter in terms of AI
disparities in the United States and how these may be mitigated. In Denmark, I interviewed members of the Radi-
ology Artificial Intelligence Testcenter(RAIT) at Herlev and Gentofte Hospital, including Louis Lind Plesner and
Christian Hedeager, to discuss the use of artificial intelligence in the Danish healthcare system and the issues they
confront. To provide a more nuanced picture, Kristoffer Gordon, Senior Data Scientist at 2021.AI, was also inter-
viewed, providing insight into how commercial products are screened for underlying biases before implementation,
and what processes and steps are needed for commercial products. Lastly, an interview with Sune Hannibal Holm
was conducted to get an insight into his project of Fair Treatment with AI DTU, January 2021.
6.2.1
Interview with Leo A. Celi
This interview was conducted with Leo A. Celi, a critical-care physician, at Beth Israel Deaconess Medical Center
in Boston, and a Massachusetts Institute of Technology AI (Medical Engineering and Science, 2022).
In the interview with Leo A. Celi, the different sources of biases were discussed, to which he addressed several
reasons, why this occurred. From the interview with Leo A. Celi, it became clear that two sources of biases were
the most frequently observed in healthcare, representation bias and Selection bias, which are the two most frequently
occurring sources of bias in a dataset. The interview can be found in Appendix 10.3.1.
The first point Leo A. Celi addresses is that the data gathered often do not adequately reflect those who experience the
poorest consequences or outcomes, which is especially clear in terms of sepsis. (Sepsis is one of the leading causes
of death in the critical care unit; it is caused by infection, which results in a immune response. And the mortality
rate from sepsis varies between 15% and 40%, depending on the severity of the illness. (Andre Kalil, 2020). )
What frustrates Leo A. Celi, is that the guidelines for treating sepsis are based on research conducted primarily in
the United States, the United Kingdom, Germany, and Scandinavia, even though patient who experiences sepsis
is much, much greater in Asia, and poorer nations. However, these principles are applied globally, even though
there is no guarantee that studies conducted in wealthy countries with a predominantly white population, would
be applicable to the rest of the globe. Thus, there is data bias, as the data utilized to influence treatment regimens
are not from the majority of people, as the majority of people experiencing sepsis are Asians, Africans, and South
Americans. (10.3.1, Minute: 7:40). Thus, one major source of biases is that there is an insufficient representation
of populations in the data. As a result, whatever model developed will not necessarily be accurate. Additionally,
Leo A. Celi and his teams express deep dissatisfaction with the fact that the group that develops these standards
Page 55 of 136
==PAGE:57==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
is composed mostly of members from wealthy countries, which according to Leo A. Celi is truly terrible, as the
burden of sepsis is significantly greater in developing countries. (10.3.1, Minute: 7:40).
The other form of bias, which Leo A. Celi addresses is mostly due to selection bias. For instance, for an intensive
care unit, a model can be developed that clinicians can use to assist them in making decisions in the ICU. The data
used by the data scientists will often however lack important patients, as the data scientists are unaware that some
patients never make it to the intensive care unit at all since they reside in rural locations and die before they can
reach the ICU. (10.3.1, Minute: 7:40). As a result, this inflicts a bias in who is admitted to the ICU, which is a
formula for erroneous associations. Therefore, to avoid selection bias, data scientists, according to Leo A. Celi must
be aware of which pages patients entered the database and which patients were excluded from the database due to
social factors or subjectivity in decision making because the model developed would be affected by such sampling
selection biases. Moreover, with such a model, it will deduct some strange associations in the algorithm as a result
of the sampling selection bias. (10.3.1, Minute: 7:40).
However, discovering why these biases occur, to then mitigate these issues is a more difficult topic. The lack of
representative data can be fixed, but Leo A. Celi underlines that research within this field is beginning to recognize
that this is more than squeezing features into a model. Leo A. Celi is concerned since models are frequently con-
structed to mimic how the real world is, which could eventually amply biases. Currently, often models are judged
based on their accuracy, however, the problem which this approach is often the ground truth is unfair. In the real
world, certain populations have a higher mortality rate, which is not explained by disease variables. Therefore, if
accuracy is the criterion for evaluating an algorithm, this indicates that the reality will always contain these dispar-
ities. As a result, according to Leo A. Celi researchers and stakeholders must devise a new method for evaluating
these models. Because if the goal of a model is to reflect what occurs in the actual world, then healthcare models
are doomed, as disparities will continue to exist, and certain individuals will constantly experience adverse effects
(10.3.1, Minute: 12:15). This highlights one of the primary concerns of algorithms: Algorithms will continue to
perpetuate the inequities that exist, which allows for biases to be amplified, and scaled. Leo A. Celi does not have
a clear remedy to alleviate this growing concern about algorithms, as there is no definitive resolution. However,
some study is being conducted in this field. (10.3.1, Minute: 12:15).
There is one paper that was released this year, which addresses some of the concerns raised by Leo A. Celi. The
paper examines the use of an algorithm used for a knee X-ray to help doctors decide whether their patients are
candidates for knee surgery, based on the patient’s pain experience and the degradation discovered on the knee
X-rays (Pierson et al., 2021).
From the paper, it was determined that when two individuals have comparable osteoarthritis and a similar Kellgren
and Lawrence Grading System (KLG) score, black patients and low-income populations reported more pain. These
disparities have a negative and disproportionate effect on how underserved minority and low-income individuals
are treated for pain, and underserved patients who are unable to qualify for knee replacement surgery, are instead
treated with potentially dangerous opioids. (Pierson et al., 2021). To address the disparities, Pierson et al., 2021,
constructed a new algorithm, and diversified the database, as it lacked representation. The new algorithm developed
by the team was not only more accurate than current practices, but also significantly reduced racial and socioeco-
nomic disparities in pain scores. However, even after diversifying the database, the team discovered that it did not
eradicate all disparities between black and white patients, which still existed. (Pierson et al., 2021).
What concerns Leo A. Celi is that, despite the diversity of the database, there are still significant gaps in social-
economic status and race, however, the article is useful in terms of methods of mitigation biases (10.3.1, Minute:
14:34).
Leo A. Celi is studying additional ways that can be used in conjunction with dataset diversification, including
launching adversarial attacks on the data set in an attempt to trick an algorithm into believing an individual is
male rather than female. However, computers frequently employ proxies to determine an individual’s values, such
as gender. To address this issue, Leo A. Celi discusses the expanding usage of counterfactual prediction to assess
whether algorithms would predict the same outcome if certain features were altered, for example, if a black person’s
zip code was changed to that of a white person (10.3.1, Minute: 14:34).
Following these lines, Leo A. Celi discusses the underlying biases caused by socioeconomic determinants and the
unconscious biases of nurses and physicians. While everyone would have equal access to healthcare in an ideal
world, critics claim that poor people frequently face societal stresses that impede treatment such as chemotherapy.
(10.3.1, Minute: 14:34). The main challenge here is determining how much of the variance is due to social variables
and how much is due to doctors’ and nurses’ biases, omitting them from treatment. This is the issue, which Leo
Page 56 of 136
==PAGE:58==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
A. Celi and his team are currently facing, as the underlying factors of biases are difficult to determine, and for the
moment being, there are yet no answers to all of these concerns (10.3.1, Minute: 14:34).
According to Leo A. Celi the best thing that can be done in order to avoid biases and disparities, would be to
popularize the concept of a disparities dashboard. According to Leo A. Celi, this would be "the ultimate metric".
In terms of practicalities, each organization should be examined to determine if one groups’ (gender, race, etc.)
outcomes are worse than others after adjusting for illness severity. Thus, this should be tied to the hospital funding,
such that hospitals with a high rate of inequities in treatment should receive less funding until they undertake some
quality improvement activity to address the disparities. Thus, the ultimate criterion for algorithm evaluation would
be to observe their direct effect on the disparities dashboard. If the inequalities continue to increase, it indicates
that only a small number of patients are truly benefiting from this early detection of deterioration. If this is the case,
the model should be re-calibrated, since it is not benefiting everyone. (10.3.1, Minute: 19:24).
The terms of Independence, Separation, and Sufficiency were also discussed. Leo A. Celi highlights that these
terms are almost never used as constraints for algorithms, except for some cases of transplantations. Instead, they
are often used as metrics for evaluating performance across sensitive attributes. During COVID-19 there were
guidelines put in place to determine who should receive ventilators, however, there were never used, as there were
always beds available. These guidelines were based on illness severity to score, which were chosen to exhibit racist
behavior, by determining their ROC curves, and if they were calibrated correctly: (10.3.1, Minute: 24:50).
The last, and most vital point from the interview with Leo A. Celi is the importance of auditing. To audit the
algorithms, conduct due diligence, and ensure that it performs well across populations before deploying it is vital to
ensure the fair use of algorithms, and Leo A. Celi cannot stress this enough. It is of utmost importance to monitor
the algorithms, cause the outcomes and predictions, are never known. (10.3.1, Minute: 19:24).
6.2.2
Interview with Louis Lind Plesner and Christian Hedeager Krag, RAIT (Radiological Artificial In-
telligence Testcenter) Herlev and Gentofte Hospital
Based on the interview with Leo A. Celi, the focus was directed more closely towards the Danish Healthcare sec-
tor, and the objective of the interview was to elucidate the fundamental reasons for any disparities in algorithms
employed in clinical practices. This interview was conducted with Louis Lind Plesner, Ph.D. student, and Christian
Hedeager Krag, Research Assistant both at RAIT (Radiological Artificial Intelligence Testcenter) at Herlev and
Gentofte Hospital. The interview can be found in Appendix 10.3.3.
Both Louis’ and Christian’s work are focused on testing and auditing algorithms, that are considered for clinical
operations, by analyzing whether the results from publications can be replicated for clinical practices, by investigat-
ing if there are discrepancies between reported performance and the performance that can be obtained by applying
real-world clinical data, and how that difference in performance can be due to potential biases (10.3.3, Minute:
02:52).
Both initiatives use the same strategy of sequentially enrolling a large number of patients and then testing the
model’s effectiveness. This is then compared to both the given performance and the performance observed by a
radiologist to identify any deviations. This enables them to determine whether there are differences in performance
between e.g. men and women, or other features that have been added to their data sets. (10.3.3, Minute: 06:34).
They usually use the metrics o ROC, AUC, Sensitivity, Specificity, NPV, and PPV (This is not in the interviews, was
confirmed later by mail). Moreover, picking one metric over another, is never the case, as it is always a depending
on the applied use case. (10.3.3, Minute: 24:50).
Their work has frequently demonstrated cases, in which the listed performance (AUC) from studies, is different from
the one observed by using clinical data. This is due to some of the issues revolving around many radiological AI
projects in terms of patient selection, as many studies include patients who are known to be ill and patients who are
known to be healthy, however rarely something in between. This leads to a perfect dataset, which creates significant
uncertainty regarding the possibility of generalization (10.3.3, Minute: 07:22). Both Louis and Christian underline
how infrequently the performance of developers and publications matches the performance seen in a clinical setting,
which emphasizes the critical necessity for auditing algorithms.
Louis adds that many of the datasets used the last couple of years to develop deep learning algorithms, violate
established medical research standards and raise significant concerns, as they use the same data gathering practices
as mentioned above. Although the articles claim to have high AUC curves, they are based on erroneous datasets,
as the datasets utilized are capable of precisely discriminating between sick and healthy individuals, which is never
the case in reality (10.3.3, Minute: 11:35).
Page 57 of 136
==PAGE:59==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
When asked how to mitigate this matter, they both underline that the best way to do it is by external validation using
a large representative data collection, which stresses the scrutiny of their work. If they approve an erroneous algo-
rithm, it could have severe consequences on health outcomes. This allows you to determine whether any potential
biases and if the algorithms disadvantage any subgroup, which then can be mitigated concurrently (10.3.3, Minute:
10:17).
Another significant point made during the interview was how a difference in base rates can contribute to the com-
plexity of algorithms, as training data is frequently distributed according to reality, and when it comes to general
diseases, the distribution is frequently different between men and women. This poses some ethical questions about
whether one should allow an algorithm to be more effective on males than on women, or vice versa, or to be equally
effective on both. This is very dependent on the use case. While the ethical dilemma proposed by this matter, is not
the concern of Louis and Christian, as they only test algorithms before their deployment in clinical practice, they
underline the need of considering this issue when developing algorithms. (10.3.3, Minute: 29:10).
To offer additional insight on this subject, an interview was done with 2021.AI, a company that specializes in
developing algorithms for healthcare. The interview’s objective was to give information on the ethical problems
that arise when base rates differ across features, and that a model may perform better on men than on women or
other features.
6.2.3
Interview with Kristoffer Gordon, 2021.AI
In the interview with professor Kristoffer Gordon, who is a Senior Data Scientist at 2021.AI, the topics of how
to mitigate any potential biases, and how to develop algorithms were discussed. The interview can be found in
Appendix 10.3.2 What was interesting and different from this interview, is that Kristoffer works at 2021.AI who is
a company conducting commercialized algorithms, and has an intense focus on healthcare algorithms, thus offering
different insights than the other interviews.
To determine if there exists an underlying bias, 2021.AI uses the framework of IBM’s toolbox Bellamy et al., 2019,
where they check for disparate impact, statistical parity, average odds, and equal opportunity disparities, as metrics
for determining biases or discrimination. He highlights that often, they have discovered that there do exist biases
in both healthcare data and financial data. (10.3.2, Minute: 15:47)
When developing algorithms, that first course of action is the exploratory analysis, to determine what trends they
can deduce. Their research methods start with a broad spectrum, and narrow down, as they explore the data. They
are in constant communication with their legal staff to ascertain what they should pay particular attention to when
analyzing the data for any potential biases or discrimination. Depending on the application, it may be that no
discrimination based on gender, ethnic origin, or region of origin is permitted (10.3.2, Minute: 13:42) For their
analysis, 2021.AI employs fairness gerrymandering, as discussed in Section 5.1.3, by assessing multiple subgroups
simultaneously to determine whether there is underlying bias on subgroups. They quantify numerous variables as
well as cross-reference them because, in most cases, multiple biases are present. It could be an ethnic group living
in the same territory that do not necessarily clash separately, but when grouped together they do.
If they detect any differences that violate the legal rules, established by their legal department for the given ap-
plication, they will then mitigate these disparities. He emphasizes that it is a three-step process that begins with
pre-processing, continues with in-processing, and concludes with post-processing, and is based, among other things,
on the procedures for using the IBM toolbox Bellamy et al., 2019. Thus for 2021, the metrics are used to achieve
fairness, depending on the use-case, however. Throughout the machine learning pipeline, the metrics can be used,
mitigate or tweak the algorithm to be in adherence, with the desired metrics for fairness (10.3.2, Minute: 05:28).
When asked which of the metrics from Section 5, Kristoffer would think would be the best in a given scenario,
he diminishes thus questions, stating that adhering to only one concept of fairness would be a flawed approach, as
it is always case-specific. No metrics is better than others in general, which constitutes that the fairness metrics
could be a case of situational ethics, in which the moral decision-making is contextual or dependent on a set of
circumstances.(Rosenthal, n.d.,10.3.2, Minute: 14:48).
However, there are instances where biases are desired (10.3.2, Minute: 05:28). He discussed how this is especially
applicable, within the health care system. These biases are not all unwanted, as they are utilized to provide more
accurate diagnoses. Therefore, in some cases, if a more accurate diagnosis can be made by discriminating between
features, this could be desirable. He provides examples here, such as prostate cancer, breast cancer, or heart attacks,
as potential cases where some groups of features such as gender, are more dominant than others in real-life scenarios.
(10.3.2, Minute: 07:09). This highlights the need for a possible trade-off, beyond the fairness metrics.
Page 58 of 136
==PAGE:60==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
For providing better algorithms, 2021.AI incorporates their own biases into a dataset to obtain better findings, by
upscaling or downscaling some favorable classes to reach a sample with the desired outcome. Kristoffer expands
on this point by stating that while it is sometimes beneficial to discriminate based on different characteristics in
a health context because they produce better results, he also emphasizes the importance of taking a stand on this
issue, which is always case-specific. (10.3.2, Minute: 08:07). This part of the conversation thus gets particularly
fascinating as this poses some ethical problems for the engineers. According to Kristoffer, there is no silver bullet
that identifies the optimal balance; rather, it is an outcome determined by the interaction of several stakeholders, who
collectively choose which outcome is the best. (10.3.2, Minute: 09:25). This precisely underscores the importance
of ethical interaction in the development of algorithms for the healthcare system, since they raise some difficulties.
What ethical principles should algorithms adhere to? As can be seen from the interview there is some notion
of trade-offs, that could be important when designing and building algorithms. However, is it fair, if an algorithm
discriminates on some features, to allow better care? The last point will be the topic of the discussion, as determining
how algorithms should be built always comes with a trade-off.
However, a Danish research project is investigating this matter in greater depth, to determine which fairness prin-
ciples to adhere to, in a healthcare setting that prompts dilemmas, not answered by existing fairness definitions of
machine learning
6.2.4
Interview with Sune Hannibal, University of Copenhagen
The interview was conducted as a result of Sune’s involvement in a research project investigating how artificial
intelligence may be used to combat discrimination in the health care system. (DTU, January 2021.)
Sune’s participation in the project will, among other things, define what constitutes a fair algorithm in the health
care system and so establish new standards for fairness in machine learning and artificially intelligent systems.
Forskningsfond, 2019. Sune emphasizes, however, that what constitutes "fairness" in the healthcare industry is
very certainly different from what constitutes "fairness" in other sectors. Forskningsfond, 2019.
Sune will investigate the ethical implications of employing algorithms to categorize individuals and make judgments
based on such classifications. It’s a significant issue if, for example, healthcare providers are prohibited from giving
men the best possible treatment for heart disease because an algorithm is less diagnostic accurate on women. It must
be considered during the ethical analysis of a fair health algorithm, and since it is essentially a matter of distribution,
of some sort of good, it deals with the matter of fairness. (Forskningsfond, 2019, 10.3.4, Minute: 03:57).
Sune goes on further to explain how different base rates can create complications for algorithms. This is essen-
tially a problem because as the designer of an algorithm, one is obliged to consider these disparities and make
some decisions about how this algorithm should behave in connection to these groups. Should it assure equivalent
predictive value, equal error rates, or something else different? Thus, these discrepancies in base rates complicate
matters, since achieving equal treatment leads to a sacrifice of accuracy to achieve group equality, and Sune em-
phasizes this topic is of much debate, cause is this desirable? Moreover, he highlights that his primary concern is
the discussion what is a fair distribution of the misclassification for an algorithm? (10.3.4, Minute: 03:57). This
is the same consideration as Kristoffer from 2021.AI raised, how disparate base rates prompt ethical consideration
when designing algorithms. This creates concerns since it affects how people in various categories benefit from the
algorithm, which necessitates a debate of the appropriate fairness rules to follow.
Given that algorithms cannot satisfy all criteria, as demonstrated in Section 5.3.6, Sune emphasizes that it raises
ethical questions of, what should be done instead. Because healthcare is unique in comparison to other industries,
it poses certain ethical concerns about what constitutes fairness. Given that the algorithm cannot simultaneously
meet both sufficiency and separation, or some of the other principles, what should it attempt to do? Should there be
an equal probability of possessing a given attribute, if given the same prediction? Or if possing the given attribute,
should there be an equal probability of the prediction being correct? (10.3.4, Minute: 03:57).
These considerations are the source of considerable ambiguity, according to Sune, and fundamentally, there will be
doubt and discussion over how to proceed, as there is no universal answer to determine fairness. However, if we
desired to alter an algorithm, what should it adhere to? What is the proper course of action? According to Sune,
this is the primary difficulty for those attempting to create a fair algorithm (10.3.4).
6.3
Concluding remarks on chapter, and summary of interviews
The aim of the chapter was partly to elicit people’s perceptions of algorithmic fairness, provide expert insights
to why biases arise in healthcare, and lastly to give preliminary insights to how do to ensure responsible AI and
Page 59 of 136
==PAGE:61==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Machine learning for healthcare in practice.
Due to shortcomings of the questionnaire, there cannot be concluded of what individuals’ perception of algorithmic
fairness is. However, from the questionnaire and Section 5.3.6, there is an indication, that a trade-off may be the
best solution for algorithms. This notion was analyzed to a greater extent, by interviewing domain experts regarding
trade-offs for algorithms.
Different conclusions can be derived from the various interviews, which cover all aspects of biases and fairness in
the machine learning life cycle. From data collection to model training and deployment, they highlight how biases
and discrimination occur, how they are recognized, how they can be mitigated, as well as what ethical concerns
may arise when developing algorithms.
From the interview with Radiological Artificial Intelligence Testcenter, it was stated how base rate differences
might add to algorithm complexity, as training data is typically distributed according to reality, and when it comes
to general disorders, the distribution is frequently different between men and women or other features. This raises
ethical concerns regarding whether an algorithm should be more effective on males than females, or should it be
equally effective on both, and if so fairness may come at a cost of accuracy.
This subject is discussed in further detail in the conversation with Kristoffer Gordon of 2021.AI. A particularly in-
teresting issue raised by this interview is how different base rates raise ethical issues for the engineers. As Kristoffer
points out, there are cases where bias is beneficial since it can result in more accurate diagnoses, even at the ex-
pense of differentiating between features. Examples include prostate cancer, breast cancer, and heart attacks. This
improved performance can be achieved by upscaling or downscaling some favored features to attain the desired
sample. He continues by explaining that this is extremely case-specific, as there is no silver bullet that determines
the optimal balance; rather, it is a conclusion established by the interplay of multiple stakeholders, who collec-
tively choose the desired outcome. This exemplifies the critical role of ethical interaction in the development of
algorithms for the healthcare system, as they introduce complications not encountered in the finance sector’s usage
of algorithms. Sune Hannibal echoes Kristoffer Gordon’s arguments and emphasizes the significant issues arising
if healthcare providers are restricted from providing males with the best possible treatment for heart disease if an
algorithm is less accurate on women. This must be taken into account while doing an ethical analysis of a fair
health algorithm. This is fundamentally a problem because the designer of an algorithm is obligated to take these
inequalities into account and make decisions regarding how the algorithm should behave in relation to these groups.
Leo A. Celi’s interview demonstrated why biases in algorithms arise, how pre-existing biases and discrimination
in society perpetuate into algorithms, and what frameworks for healthcare should include. Leo A. Celi addresses
that in many cases there is a lack of representatives for both algorithms, and also by the groups developing these
procedures. Moreover, developers should be cognizant of which patients are included in their data as Leo A. Celi
highlighted, that intensive care units, may lack critical patient data due to social factors or subjectivity in decision-
making, resulting from admission biases. When developing algorithms based on these data, developers must keep
this in mind and be aware of which patients may be missing from the dataset.
Selection and representational biases are minor concerns in comparison to the main problem with today’s healthcare
algorithms. Leo A. Celi’s biggest concern is that algorithms may continue replicating existing inequities, allowing
biases to be reinforced and scaled, creating a negative feedback loop. While models are usually created to replicate
the real world, this might potentially increase existing gaps and discrimination, as previously shown by Ledford,
2019, where systemic racism results in millions of black individuals being affected by racial bias in health-care
algorithms. Leo A. Celi emphasizes that while models are frequently rated on their accuracy, the ground truth is
frequently unfair and skewed, implying that accuracy is not a meaningful performance criterion. In reality, certain
groups have a greater mortality rate than others, which cannot be explained by disease variables. As a result, if
accuracy is used to evaluate an algorithm, this implies that algorithms will continue to contain these discrepancies,
fueling a negative feedback look, as depicted in Figure 8. Thus, vital practices when using algorithms would be to
implement disparity dashboards, to determine how algorithms are treating certain groups. Moreover, Leo A. Celi
stresses the importance of auditing algorithms, as to audit the algorithms, conduct due diligence, and ensure that it
performs well across populations before deploying it is vital to ensure the fair use of algorithms.
Fortunately, in Danish healthcare, this is precisely what the department Radiology Artificial Intelligence Testcen-
ter is doing at Herlev and Gentofte hospitals. Both Louis and Christians’ research focuses on testing and auditing
algorithms that are considered for clinical operations. They are investigating whether results from publications can
be generalized to clinical practices look, by looking at discrepancies between reported performance and the perfor-
mance that can be obtained using real-world clinical data, and how those discrepancies manifest as potential biases.
Their work regularly demonstrates instances in which the performance (AUC) reported from studies differs from
Page 60 of 136
==PAGE:62==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
the performance found utilizing clinical data. This is due to some of the issues revolving around many radiological
AI projects in terms of patient selection, as many studies include patients who are known to be ill and patients who
are known to be healthy, however rarely something in between, which violate established medical research stan-
dards and raise significant concern. This leads to a perfect dataset, which creates significant uncertainty regarding
the possibility of generalizations. When asked how to mitigate this matter, they both underline that the best way
to do it is by external validation using a large representative data collection, which stresses the scrutiny of their
work. If they approve an erroneous algorithm, it could have severe consequences on health outcomes. This allows
you to determine whether any potential biases and if the algorithms disadvantage any subgroup, which then can be
mitigated concurrently
Moreover from both Kristoffer of 2021, and from Radiological Artificial Intelligence Testcenter, it was clear that
no metrics is better than others in general, which constitutes that the fairness metrics could be a case of situational
ethics, in which the moral decision making is contextual or dependent on a set of circumstances.
From the interviews, several findings were discovered as well as providing more questions that need addressing. As
fairness is only one pillar of the framework in 4.7, the experts gave insights to the considerations when constructing
algorithms for healthcare, which are needed to avoid algorithms continuing replicating existing inequities, allowing
biases to be reinforced and scaled, creating a negative feedback loop. No single comprehensive metric of fairness
is available, and often different base rates can allow for a trade-off between fairness and accuracy. This if often
depends on the use case, and is a case of situational ethics.
As there is no universally accepted definition of fairness in machine learning, and since multiple fairness metrics
can not be attained simultaneously, there is frequently a trade-off between which principle to implement. However,
how can this trade-off be determined, what principles should algorithms adhere to, and does the set of fairness
metrics conform with moral notions of fairness from Chapter 4?
