==PAGE:63==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
7
Analysis of Findings
As demonstrated from the earlier chapters fair machine learning faces several conceptual ethical difficulties: accept-
ing that no single comprehensive metric of fairness is available, how may we rank the metrics in terms of importance
or acceptability; or how may we decide which metric to apply in a given context? What ethical principles and norms
can be used to guide the design of fairness criteria for algorithmic classification?
Moreover, both the questionnaire and Section 5.3.6 indicate that a trade-off may be the best solution. This notion was
also raised by the interviews of Kristoffer Gordon of 2021 in 6.2.3, and Sune Hannibal in 6.2.4, which highlighted
that there are moments where it can beneficial to discriminate between groups, which opens up the notions which
trade-offs, which then again poses new ethical questions. Can misclassifications and trade-offs between fairness
and accuracy be justified?
The purpose of the following chapter is to analyze how fairness metrics for machine learning from Chapter 5 as
well as how trade-offs can be justified and conform to moral theories of distributive justice. By doing so the section
will demonstrate how the fairness metrics discussed in Chapter 5 can be used as guiding principles for developing
fairness criteria for algorithmic classification.
Moreover, concerns regarding inequalities outside of a model, raised by Leo A. Celi (6.2.1), RAIK (6.2.2),Ledford,
2019, and in Section 3.6.2, point to that even if adhering to fairness metrics, it might not result in a fair outcome,
due to historical biases, within the data gathered. This will also be analyzed to a greater extend in Section 7.1.
Even under the assumption that fairness metrics can be used to mitigate biases, it is also vital to determine who
should receive treatment. Is it treating people equally, favoring the worst-off, maximizing total benefits, or pro-
moting and rewarding social usefulness? For instance, is it fair that two patients who are otherwise comparable
are treated differently by the health care system if one is younger than the other? Who should receive the one
available organ between two otherwise comparable patients, or a ventilator? (Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel,
2009). These are considerations, as also raised by Kristoffer Gordon and Sune Hannibal, which incorporated into
an algorithm, and are thus vital to consider, when determining which ethical principles and norms, can be used to
guide the design of fairness criteria for algorithmic classification, which will be the focal point of Section 7.2.
However, as it is particularly difficult to anticipate the consequences of policies in the distant future, any policy
that imposes a clear burden on the present in exchange for a hypothetical future advantage should be held to a high
standard, which does not only include fairness (Card and Smith, 2020), By focusing exclusively on fairness measure-
ments that quantify the objective outcome redistribution, a decision-maker may neglect critical factors regarding the
ethical principles for the adoption of socially beneficial AI in Section 4.7 (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021). Con-
sequently, 7.3 will examine the consequences of imposing fairness constraints on beneficence and non-maleficence,
as well as the necessary considerations of fairness when imposing a possible framework, thus addressing the fifth
research question.
Thus the analysis will analyze how the sources described and reviewed in previous chapters (literature and inter-
views), as well as additional literature address the arising challenges in the distribution of benefits and burdens,
relating results to principles and metrics of fairness for algorithmic classification.
7.1
Justifying Fair Machine Learning Metrics from Distributive Justice
According to Libertarianism, equitable distribution of tangible goods occurs as a result of consensual exchanges
between persons, and procedures take precedence over any assessment of substantive outcomes (Iserson, 1999.
Thus Libertarianism pay only limited attention to issues of public utility or to adequately addressing the health
needs of the population (Iserson, 1999, Section (4.2.1)). Thus for libertarianism distributions should be based on the
resources that each person contributed towards production, or at least the distribution of goods to individuals should
be at least as equitable as their initial contribution, thus leaving no individual worse of (Leben, 2020; Andersen,
2020b).
An illustrative example is the case discussed in Section 3.3.1, where the proportion of truly ill lower SES patients
in their group is 1.4 times that of truly ill higher SES patients (86%:63%). Thus for Independence, Separation, and
Sufficiency to be justified, the differences in the PPV, TPR, etc. cannot exceed the gap of 1.4 as it would allow
for patient groups to be worse off, than they initially were, thus violating requirements of Libertarianism, as per
Section 4.2.1. For example, if the disparity gap in FP or FN rates between lower SES and higher SES surpasses the
amount of the initial disparity gap in the target attribute, then the Libertarians may call that classifier unfair, as they
are worse off than they initially were (Leben, 2020).
Page 62 of 136
==PAGE:64==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Even though an initial gap could exist due to historical discrimination or social determinants, it is out of the scope
in Libertarianism, and in classic Libertarianism, any variance from the proportions of original contributions is
judged to be unfair. (4.2.1). Thus, Libertarianism is simply concerned with ensuring that inequality across groups
does not exceed pre-existing inequalities in the target variable Y from a fairness standpoint, as long as the initial
distribution is the result of initial fair distribution and based on voluntary transactions. The Libertarian’s objective is
not to increase equality, but to avoid exacerbating society’s current inequities, which can be deemed as unfortunate,
but not unfair. (Leben, 2020). Thus inequalities outside of a model, as raised by both Leo A. Celi 6.2.1 and the
interview with Radiology Artificial Intelligence Testcenter (6.2.2) and demonstrated by Ledford, 2019, and the case
of Aalborg in Section 3.6.2, are not within the scope of libertarianism if the distribution of tangible goods occur as
a result of consensual exchanges.
By remembering the notions of section 4.4.1, and by translating Rawlsian EOP into the supervised learning setting,
Heidari et al., 2018 derive proof that group fairness metrics of Independence (Demographic Parity) and Separation
(Equal Opportunity) can be justified from Rawls’ definition of EOP. The findings of Heidari et al., 2018, are shared
by M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021, who proposes the same findings.
For Separation, if the target variable Y reflects an individual’s effort-based utility D, then Rawlsian EOP translates
to Separation across protected groups, as individuals receive exactly what they work for. That is:
• d = Y
• a = ˆY
• u = (Y −ˆY )
The argument of Separation or Equal Opportunity as an instance of Rawlsian EOP is critical, is highly dependent on
the underlying assumption: For Separation to be justified as Rawlsian EOP it is implicitly said that all individuals
with the same true label have the same effort-based utility d. As mentioned earlier, true labels present a challenge
in practice, as true labels do not always accurately reflect/summarize accountability variables. Moreover, the as-
sumption that these skills are distributed equally frequently does often not hold in practice, as demonstrated by the
interviews Leo A. Celi (6.2.1) and Radiology Artificial Intelligence Testcenter (6.2.2), and Section 5.3.5. They
are, at best, a representation of the current state of circumstances — which may be tainted by historical injustices.
M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021 share similar concerns as they highlight that Rawlsian EOP does not address
discrimination that may already exist in the data. Discrimination may be embedded throughout the machine learn-
ing life cycle, (see Figure 8, as a result of skewed data collecting, skewed data labeling (e.g., humans rating male
candidates as more capable), or skewed human decisions feeding the system (e.g. if courts are more likely to find
black defendants as guilty). For these reasons, Heidari et al., 2018 argue that Equal Opportunity can be justified as a
valid measure of algorithmic fairness (with an EOP rationale) only after the above moral equivalency assumption’s
validity has been thoroughly investigated and its implications thoroughly understood in the context in which it is
used.
For Independence it can be argued, that is in the notion of strict egalitarianism or more collectivist egalitarianism
for distributive justice, and does not fall under the notions of Rawls EOP, as proposed by M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh,
2021. The argument is based on by interpreting Rawls’s EOP, which states fairness is achieved by " those who are
at the same level of talent and ability, are equally willing to use them must have the same prospect of obtaining
desirable social positions, regardless of arbitrary factors such as the socio-economic background" M. Lee, Floridi,
and Singh, 2021". M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021 highlights that these principles are not applied when enforcing
Independence, as Independence only ensures that individuals have the same positive rates, and does not take into
account talent, ability, and the willingness to use them when distributing goods when ensuring fairness.
However, Heidari et al., 2018 argue that Independence actually can satisfy Rawls’s fair EOP, under the moral as-
sumption, that metrics explained by Independence are suitable for scenarios in which all the individuals have the
same effort-based utility, and there exists no advantage in distribution among subgroups. Consequently, Indepen-
dence can be justified from Rawlsian EOP, with the effort-based utility defined as a constant of d = 1 and the actual
utility defined as the consequence of the decision a = ˆY . This indicates that each individual possesses an inherent
constant utility that is unaffected by effort or circumstances, which could be argued to be the case if examining
the allocation of a good that all people deserve equally (e.g. an intrinsic right), like ventilators, or the will to live.
(Heidari et al., 2018.).
Thus, Heidari et al., 2018 argue that these assumptions may hold through an example: Assume that society views all
patients as possessing the same effort-based utility—which can be realized through access to appropriate clinical
tests. Assume that undertaking an invasive clinical examination has a utility of 1 if one has a suspected disease
Page 63 of 136
==PAGE:65==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
and -1 if one does not, but avoiding the same clinical inquiry has a utility of 1 if one does not have the suspected
disease and -1 if one does. Thus the effort-based utility is identical for all patients in both situations. In other words,
everyone who has a condition deserves invasive clinical evaluation, whereas everyone who does not have a disease
deserves to avoid it. For a classifier thus distributing utility, it should achieve an equal distribution of effort-based
utility (d) and distribute no advantage u, to any subgroups e.g., gender or race. For an imperfect accuracy predictor,
a fair predictor (with R-EOP rationale) would require the distribution of utility to ensure that each group with or
without the disease had an equal likelihood of receiving (avoiding) an intrusive clinical exam, which would satisfy
demographic parity. (Heidari et al., 2018)
As Rawls EOP takes an absolutist view on effort and circumstances, as described in Section 4.4.1 which assumes
that an individual’s effort-based utility and comparable and is independent of circumstance when distributing goods,
then Sufficiency or predictive value parity cannot be justified from Rawls EOP. Sufficiency necessitates conditioning
on the classifier’s conclusions; it necessitates equal frequencies of true outcomes for equivalent predictions. (Heidari
et al., 2018).
However, by using the formulation of Luck Egalitarianism EOP Heidari et al., 2018 mathematically prove that
Sufficiency can be justified from luck egalitarian EOP, by equating effort-based utility with the classifier’s decision,
and actual utility with the true outcome, which builds on two assumptions:
• A = Y : the true label reflects the individual’s actual utility.
• D = ˆY . The predicted label/risk reflects the individual’s effort-based utility.
The underlying premise is that the predicted label/risk ˆY reflects the individual’s effort-based utility u. Thus indi-
viduals are morally responsible for their chances of success because the predictions are calculated based on their
actions. Secondly the true label Y reflects the individual’s actual utility. (Heidari et al., 2018).
According to notions Heidari et al., 2018 it can be argued that of Sufficiency and assumes that the classifier’s
assessment is assumed to capture effort, while differences between effort in the actual outcome Y are primarily
due to circumstance, such as brute luck. Consequently, if the two assumptions are held, a fair predictor (with EOP
rationale) should satisfy of Sufficiency. (Heidari et al., 2018).
Moreover, the assumptions for Sufficiency as an instance of Luck Egalitarianism EOP, echo the impossible theorem
in Section 5.3.6, as Sufficiency and Separation cannot hold simultaneously due to differences in moral assumptions
encoded in effort-based utility. (Heidari et al., 2018). D = ˆY presupposes that for Sufficiency all individuals with
the same ’risk’ (predicted label) are held to the same standard of accountability for their forecasts. Two individuals
are considered to have the same prospects if they are at the same quantile or rank of their corresponding type.
Conversely, separation assumes persons with similar true labels are equally accountable for their labels. (Heidari
et al., 2018).
Consequentialists, as utilitarianism would respond with some skepticism to disputes over various fairness metrics,
as social justice is not contingent on equality or inequality between groups, but rather on the impact of inequality on
the utility of individuals within those groups. Consequently, a statistic such as demographic parity is irrelevant to a
utilitarian, as sheer inequality of representation in the categories ˆy = 1 or ˆy = 0 reveals nothing about the relationship
between classifications and total utility. (Leben, 2020)
To illustrate, take once more the instance of attempting to govern algorithms that banks will employ to create loans.
Requiring compliance with any of the above-mentioned fairness constraints will modify the pool of loan applicants
authorized (and denied). While some of these criteria may result in broadly beneficial changes (e.g., demographic
parity may improve access to credit for historically marginalized groups), consequentialists argue that evaluating
the outcome solely in terms of the probabilities or labels assigned to each group is insufficient. It is vital to analyze
the ramifications for people and society as a whole. (Card and Smith, 2020).
In some instances, a loan can favorably improve an individual’s or community’s life through mechanisms such
as education and entrepreneurship. In other circumstances, increased credit availability may result in speculative
borrowing and financial collapse. While not directly related to concerns about fairness, the potentially catastrophic
effects of lending policies that disregard long-term and systemic consequences are readily apparent in the aftermath
of the subprime mortgage crisis, which was exacerbated in part by perverse incentives and risky lending. (Card
and Smith, 2020). Thus for a fairness metric to be justified by Consequentialists, it requires that relative weights
of each outcome based on the total social cost can be predicted, which will enable the construction of a model to
maximize social cost. However, the utilities created for each category of TP, FP, FN, TN will vary according to
the classifier type. For loan eligibility algorithms, the negative utility generated by FPs is significantly worse, than
Page 64 of 136
==PAGE:66==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
what is generated by FNs, because the former represents a total loss of investment. For risk-assessment algorithms
in criminal justice, the negative utility of FNs may be far greater than that of FPs, simply due to the quantity of
violence avoided. For decision-making algorithms in healthcare, the negative utility of FNs is often greater than
that of FPs due to the sheer impact of not being allocated the needed care, which is in worst-case scenarios could
have fatal consequences. (Leben, 2020)
Thus fairness in consequentialist approaches is demonstrated by the way weights are assigned to each group outcome
according to its proportionate social cost. For the case of the Kidney Transplant waiting list of Section 3.3.1, the
proportion of eligible higher SES patients to their group is 1.4 times larger than the proportion of lower SES patients
to their group. In that case, if the negative social cost of continued false positives for lower SES are weighted to
be 1.4 times worse, and the positive social benefit of true positives for lower SES to is 1.4 times better, equality
can be achieved through a consequentialist approach (Leben, 2020). This weighting element is the fundamental
means through which Consequentialist systems achieve greater parity. Consequentialism, whether utilitarianism or
prioritarianism, has the benefit of incorporating fairness directly into the data, and optimizing for overall benefit.
Optimizing for utility will allow for increased parity between groups, which arguably can also be said for the case
of the Kidney Transplant waiting list. However, it requires an understanding of the inherent data, and it necessitates
a full range of the consequences to individuals and society, impacted by said algorithms, to be justified. Thus, it
is critical to recognize that fairness standards defined solely in terms of a limited set of short-term measurements
may not ensure beneficial outcomes beyond what they measure, and may in some situations result in increased total
harm, or a negative feedback loop. (Leben, 2020) In summary, while a consequentialist approach can be justified,
adopting a consequentialist perspective uncovers significant flaws in existing suggestions for considering fairness
in machine learning. (Card and Smith, 2020)
By using Rawls Difference Principle opens up for the notions of trade-offs between the accuracy and fairness met-
rics, as long as the lexical order of Rawls EOP is upheld. By allowing a trade-off, by pursuing a Rawlsian EOP
can help justifying some of the concerns highlighted by the interviews of Kristoffer Gordon of 2021 6.2.3, and
Sune Hannibal of KU, 6.2.4. Their interviews both demonstrated, that to provide the best care, something it should
be allowed to distinguish between groups, as this could allow for better care, where Kristoffer Gordon of 2021.AI
highlights that "[...] in some cases, if a more accurate diagnosis can be made by discriminating between features,
this could be desirable".
Even though this distribution allows for inequities unrelated to accountability, i.e. an individual’s qualifications,
it is regarded as a morally superior alternative to equality if it increases the utility of the least fortunate. (Heidari
et al., 2018) Thus from a Rawlism EOP standpoint, an algorithm can be deemed fair, even though it might be more
accurate in its prediction for one group rather than for the other, if accuracy is the good being distributed (Hashimoto
et al., 2018). However, Franke, 2021; M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021; Rawls, 1971 have address that defining the
least advantaged is difficult, and Rawls admits that "the serious difficulty of how to define the least fortunate group
Rawls, 1971", which raises concern about its use. Nonetheless, researchers have managed to operationalize one of
Rawls’ distributive principles to alleviate language representation discrepancies in a text auto-complete mode in a
new fair machine learning article given at the 35th International Conference on Machine Learning (ICML). The
researchers operationalize "predictive text accuracy" as the "resource" for allocation using one of Rawls’ distributive
norms, dubbed the Difference Principle. Their research entailed optimizing a model to allocate a “fairer” quantity
of accuracy (as accuracy was the currency of distribution), by allocating less accuracy to one group rather than
another. (Hashimoto et al., 2018). Thus adhering to a Rawlsian EOP, a trade-off would be justifiable, however only
to the extent, that every individual would have equal access to the care, and it provides the least advantaged with
better care.
Distributive justice, according to luck egalitarians, compels those who have had the good fortune to recompense
those who have had a terrible fortune. In this redistribution, “the lucky should transfer some or all of their gains due
to luck to the unlucky” Persky, 2013. It would thus allow for a trade-off in treatment and accuracy for reasons that
are due to personal choices. Thus an algorithm under the notions of Luck egalitarianism would allow for trade-offs
benefiting subgroups and allow historical suppressed groups to be compensated (Heidari et al., 2018). Following
the case of Hashimoto et al., 2018, who operationalize one of Rawls’s distributive principles to mitigate language
representation disparities, similar principles could be argued to be used for a luck egalitarian algorithm. In the case
of breast cancer, it could be argued the difference in who gets breast cancer is not due to personal choice, however
it is more a question of circumstances over which individuals have no control over i.e., being born a woman. As
getting breast is a matter of circumstances luck egalitarianism would allow the lucky(men) should transfer some or
all of their gains due to luck to the unlucky(women). In this case, it would allow for women to have a greater claim
at treatment, or greater prediction accuracy, as they are more likely to get cancer than men. Hashimoto et al., 2018.
