However, by claiming that it is the individual’s responsibility to pay for their inaccuracies, Luck egalitarians turn a
blind eye to all inequalities that reflect a choice in some way, irrespective of the degree of individual responsibility.
This results in the exploitation and abandonment of the wretched. (Anderson, 1999)
Consequentialism would however allow for the possibility of treating some patients better than others, thus allowing
for a trade-off. This however is depending on how the utility function is constructed, and the associated weights.
However, this opens up a discussion on how can it be determined which patients are more beneficial to give treatment
than others, which will be analyzed to a greater extent in 7.2.
Thus, consequentialism emphasizes the importance of considering outcomes widely; technological systems are
rooted in social contexts, and policies can have a wide range of consequences on communities, not just those
classified. Consequently, the political nature of value involves the search and consideration of a varied range of
opinions on what is desirable, not to arrive at a reductive utilitarian calculation, but to educate oneself about the
plurality of viewpoints.
From the interview with Leo A. Celi 6.2.1, and from the Danish case with Aalborg 3.6.2 it was highlighted that in
the real world, certain populations have a higher mortality rate. Thus, certain social determinants as residency could
potentially affect the outcome of health. However, this would not be necessary be considered under consequential-
ism, which allow individuals who are already well off, to be better off (Stein, 2002). Social determinants of health,
that are not the result of individuals’ choices, such as being part of a family, social circle, or neighborhood with
higher rates of mortality.This could be objectionable to the extent that they are not the result of personal choices,
but they influence the health outcome, as demonstrated in Section 3.6.2 and 3.6.1. The question then becomes,
whether healthcare distribution should compensate nonpersonal choices. If patients have been systematically de-
nied healthcare, it could be argued, that they might have a greater claim. How should then be prioritized?
According to Rawls EOP, individuals who possess the same amount of skill or ability and are equally motivated to
use them must have the same prospect of achieving desirable social positions independent of arbitrary circumstances
such as socioeconomic background, and circumstances, which individuals can not be held morally accountable for,
cannot be deemed as legitimate sources of inequality. As independence and separation can be cast of instances of
Rawls EOP, then under these assumptions they can be justified. This would require the modification of separation
to reflect the distinction between relevant" and "irrelevant" features and inequalities from Table 5.
By ensuring notions of Luck Egalitarianism, patients systematically or indirectly denied access to the healthcare
system (e.g. by being born into poverty) may have a greater claim to e.g., ventilator (despite being in poorer health
and thus a poorer chance of benefit) so that the expected benefit equals those with easy access to healthcare (and
thus more likely to be healthy, arrive at the hospital less sick, etc). (Heidari et al., 2018). However, even though
Sufficiency was demonstrated to be a case of luck egalitarian, Sufficiency can potentially increase injustice, as the
metric suffers due to its requirement to access to the target’s true value, which is sometimes difficult to define (true
qualification for a surgery, job, loan, etc.), as per Section 5.3.3. Thus, even ensuring Sufficiency, may still have
potential flaws.
Depending on one’s moral preferences towards resource distribution, each metric for fairness can be justified, within
the frames of their inherent assumptions. This does not, however, provide a clear answer as to which metrics to
implement, however, it does give indication what ethical principles and considerations can guide an algorithm.
However, as fairness in machine learning often is concerned with ensuring equal treatment across groups, it is also
vital to determine who should receive treatment. As the fairness metrics build on notions of equal treatment across
true labels, to determine, which patients should be prioritized, an analysis of what constitutes true labels is vital.
Is it treating people equally, favoring the worst-off, maximizing total benefits, and promoting and rewarding social
usefulness? (Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009).
7.2
What ethical principles should fairness algorithms adhere to?
During the peak of the COVID-19 pandemic, there was a growing danger, that the hospitals would be overcrowded,
and would be faced with a ventilator shortage Savulescu, Cameron, and Wilkinson, 2020). Triage system and ma-
chine learning allocation programs have been used during COVID-19 to allocate care. Triage, ethically speaking,
entails identifying clinically significant information about individual patients and their likelihood of benefit. Ad-
ditionally, as discussed in the paper by in Section 3.6.1, poorly designed triage systems can expose individuals to
discriminatory actions. However, what should the triage system adhere to, when allocating a scarce supply of ven-
tilators to a too-large number of patients in need? Should ventilators be reserved for people with the best chance of
recovery (BMA, 2020)?
Page 66 of 136
==PAGE:68==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
This option seeks to maximize the available (scarce) supply of ventilators and hence falls within a utilitarian cat-
egory. There are, however, a couple of caveats to this: What if ventilators are lying idle in anticipation of future
patients with a better prognosis? How is the "probability of recovery" determined? Those with a better likelihood of
recovery may be so only as a result of past access to high-quality health care. Should that be accounted for? Other
options might imply that patients have an equal entitlement to necessary ventilators: Should patients admitted to
the hospital be treated on a ’first-come, first-served basis for ventilators? Should anybody in need of a ventilator,
regardless of their abilities, have an equal chance of receiving one? Who should be treated (Persad, Wertheimer,
and Emanuel, 2009; BMA, 2020; Savulescu, Cameron, and Wilkinson, 2020)?
Even when we use fairness metrics built on notions of equal treatment there is still missing an important ethical
consideration. Who should receive treatment? Fairness is not just about ensuring equal treatment, it is also about
who should be treated. Is it treating people equally, favoring the worst-off, maximizing total benefits, or promoting
and rewarding social usefulness? (Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009). There are different solutions depend-
ing on which principles for allocation of scarce medical interventions are chosen. By using the notions of Persad,
Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009; Rogowski et al., 2014; BMA, 2020; Savulescu, Cameron, and Wilkinson, 2020,
the following chapter will analyze which ethical principles and norms can be used to guide the design of fairness
criteria for algorithmic classification.
There are two basic ethical perspectives on this issue: egalitarianism and utilitarianism. (Savulescu, Cameron,
and Wilkinson, 2020). Egalitarianism holds that each person should be treated equally based on their need: equal
treatment for equal need. It rejects consideration of probability, length, or quality of life, and a patient born with a
disability should have an equal claim to a ventilator as someone without a disability, even if that disability affects
or complicates potential recovery (Savulescu, Cameron, and Wilkinson, 2020). For Luck Egalitarians a patient
who has been denied access to the healthcare system on a systematic basis (for example, due to birth into poverty)
may have a larger claim to a ventilator (although being in worse condition), so that the predicted benefit equals that
of individuals who have easy access to healthcare (and thus more likely to be healthy, arrive at the hospital less
sick, etc). However, there are issues with equal treatment for equal needs. . To ensure equal treatment we may be
required to disregard considerations about the likelihood of how much a patient may benefit from treatment.
According to scholars, such as Stein, 2002, Egalitarianism requires us to either (1) provide an equal amount of some
benefit to all individuals or (2) give preference to those who are less fortunate in some way. The equitable alloca-
tion criteria described here are age-based allocation, impending death-based allocation, waiting time, or random
allocation, as lottery. The latter notions necessitate an epidemiological assessment of at-risk groups, and predicting
survival rates will need to be established. The considerations of probability, length, or quality of life, can be argued
to contradict notions of Egalitarians, and reflect more notions of utilitarianism. However, Stein, 2002 argues that
all major contemporary distribution models are egalitarian in the broadest sense; they all treat people with equal
respect. Maximizing theories, like utilitarianism, value benefits equally regardless of who receives them. However,
there is a significant distinction between equally valuing benefits and equalizing benefits. The important differ-
ence between maximizing and equalizing theories is that maximizing theories will allocate resources to those who
can benefit the most, even if those individuals are already better off (Stein, 2002). Thus, adhering to notions of
egalitarianism is the notion of benefiting everybody equally, which involves giving equal opportunities for scarce
intervention. This entails adherence to either one of two criteria outlined in the Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel,
2009, namely first-come, first-served, or lottery distribution.
First come, first served is an egalitarian principle. This means that persons who get critically ill early in the pandemic
are more likely to require intensive care or mechanical breathing than those who become critically ill later in the
epidemic. However, as first-come, first-served ignores significant individual differences, it fails to treat everyone
equitably. Thus, it is under critique from Luck Egalitarians and Rawls, as it benefits the wealthy, who become
informed, and the method is likely to give priority to those who are mobile, who have access to transport, or who
live close to hospitals and other sites of health provision. There is no equal access to care. (BMA, 2020). Another
method is lotteries. Lotteries have several benefits. Even when candidates are almost identical, lotteries eliminate
the possibility of slight differences having a large impact on the outcome, such as prior ease of access to healthcare.
Additionally, some individuals favor lottery distribution on the grounds that each individual’s desire to exist should
be handled equally and with the same respect as everyone else’s. (Rogowski et al., 2014). Thus, Independence
could be considered a lottery distribution, given its independence from a target variable and its resource allocation
based on luck. Independence, on the other hand, goes beyond lottery, it is a multi-principle system that accounts
for sensitive characteristics such as ethnic origin or gender when allocating resources and equalizes positive rates
among groups.
The major flaw of lotteries is their failure to account for a vast number of seemingly significant factors. It is
Page 67 of 136
==PAGE:69==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
imprudent to make random comparisons between someone who can gain 40 years and someone who can gain only
four months, or between someone who has already lived 80 years and someone who has lived only 20 years. Thus
by adhering to lotteries, an algorithm treating individuals equally frequently falls short of treating them as equals.
(Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009; Rogowski et al., 2014) Thus, while employing a lottery-based algorithm
may appear to be fair, it suffers from the same drawbacks as Independence or Demographic Parity. Nonetheless,
as determining which patients are equal, in terms of the true label has been shown to be frequently tainted with
biases as a result of historical discrimination, advocating for a lottery could potentially enact a "positive feedback
loop," as it could potentially change previous biased practices, making everything equal for everyone. This makes
this method particularly well-suited for applications involving difficult-to-qualify targets (predicting survival rates),
such as those involving healthcare distribution, such as organ transplantation or ventilators. However, given the
principles’ inadequacies, it could be suggested that lotteries should be incorporated into a multi-principal system.
(Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009; Rogowski et al., 2014).
Another principle equalizing benefits is prioritization. Finding the appropriate level of medical utility for equalizing
benefits is contingent on prioritization concepts. A method for giving respect to those who are less fortunate is
Prioritarianism (Rogowski et al., 2014). Some describe being worst-off as a current lack of valuable commodities,
while others view it as a lifetime absence of valuable items. These two perspectives are embodied in two principles:
sickest first and youngest first. Imposing either sickest first, or youngest first has its limitations, as the principles do
not involve consideration of the likelihood of success, duration, or quality of life beyond the given therapy. Often,
the notion of treating the sickest patients first prioritizes those with the poorest prognosis if left untreated. The so-
called rule of rescue embodies this principle since it asserts that "our moral reaction to the imminence of death is
that we save the doomed." Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009). For organ transplant such as the one described
in Section 3.3.1 a combination of the sickest first approach is integrated, favoring those with the lowest values of
eGFR, into a multi-principle system. However, as a sickest first allocation ignores post-treatment prognosis on its
own, as it applies even when only minor gains at high cost can be achieved (Rogowski et al., 2014).
Moreover, prioritizing those who are currently sickest appears to imply that resource constraint is temporary: that
we can save the person who is currently sickest and then save the one who is becoming increasingly unwell (Persad,
Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009). When we are unable to save everyone, saving the sickest first is intrinsically
flawed and contradicts the fundamental concept of priority to the worst-of-the-worst. For the kidney transplant,
Section 3.3.1, preferential allocation of a scarce liver to an acutely unwell individual could be argued to be unjust
as it disregards a currently healthy patient with increasing liver disease, who may be worse off later in life, if he or
she suffers from kidney failure (Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009).
Prioritizing the youngest helps the poorest—those who would otherwise die with the fewest life years—and is thus
fundamentally different from favoritism toward older adults or the wealthy. Priority has been given to younger users
of dialysis devices and scarce organs, and allocation recommendations for pandemic influenza prioritize infants and
children (BMA, 2020; Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009; Savulescu, Cameron, and Wilkinson, 2020). The
idea of youngest first is also incorporated into the example of kidney transplant waiting list of Section 3.3.1, as the
patients’ age is incorporated. However, the youngest-first allocation ignores prognosis and excludes patients based
on their age. During pandemic demand peaks, clinicians may be compelled to determine a patient’s eligibility for
treatment on a ’capacity to benefit fast’ basis. As a result, some of the most critically ill patients may be denied
access to critical care or artificial ventilation. Similarly, a straightforward ’cut-off’ policy based on age or handicap
would be unethical and illegal, as it would involve outright discrimination. A healthy 70-year-old cannot be legally
denied treatment on the grounds of his or her age (BMA, 2020). However, elderly patients with severe respiratory
failure caused by COVID-19 may have a very high risk of death despite intensive care and so have a lower priority
for critical care admission (BMA, 2020). Thus, while the youngest-first allocation appears insufficient on its own, it
can be paired with prediction and lottery principles to create a multi principal allocation system, which will suggest
the implicit rule of pick on merit/need and then lottery (Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009).
Another principle is utilitarianism, which holds that the optimal path of action is one that maximizes utility and
benefits, or the good produced for all humans. Utilitarianism unlike the previously stated principles demands
consideration of the likelihood of success, duration, and quality of life (Savulescu, Persson, and Wilkinson, 2020;
Rogowski et al., 2014). One maximizing technique is to save the most individual lives possible, which has driven
policies on influenza vaccine allocation, or ventilators. A model or system built on these notions could addressee
some of the consideration of trade-offs raised by Kristoffer Gordon of 2021.ai and Sune Hannibal. The benefit of
saving the most lives is that it avoids comparing individual lives. Other things being equal, it should always see
to save five lives rather than one. Other factors, on the other hand, are rarely equal. Some lives can be prolonged
for a longer period of time than others. It is uncertain how to balance these additional significant factors against
Page 68 of 136
==PAGE:70==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
the goal of saving more lives—whether to save one 25-year-old who would live another 50 years if saved or three
65-year-olds who might survive only ten years (Rogowski et al., 2014; Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009;
Savulescu, Persson, and Wilkinson, 2020)?
Rather than preserving the greatest number of lives, prognostic allocation seeks to save the greatest number of
life-years (Savulescu, Persson, and Wilkinson, 2020). This technique has been utilized in disaster triage, and it
encourages the exclusion of persons with poor prognoses from waiting lists for organ transplantation or ventilators.
Optimizing life years makes natural sense. If living a longer life is beneficial, saving a longer life appears to be
equally valuable. Even supporters of prognosis-based allocation acknowledge, however, that it is incapable of
considering both distribution and quantity (Savulescu, Persson, and Wilkinson, 2020). Making a wealthy person
somewhat better off than a poor person would be unjust, which is the critical distinction between maximizing
utilitarian theories and equalizing egalitarian theories. (Savulescu, Persson, and Wilkinson, 2020; Stein, 2002)
Finally, a triage algorithm might incorporate the premise of promoting and rewarding social usefulness. Instrumen-
tal value allocation prioritizes particular individuals in order to facilitate or promote future utility (Rogowski et al.,
2014). Prioritizing critical healthcare personnel does not imply that they are more valuable in and of themselves,
but rather that they benefit others. Thus, instrumental value allocation presumably acknowledges the moral signif-
icance of each individual, even those who are not instrumentally valuable, however, fails to treat people fairly by
unequally valuing benefits.(Rogowski et al., 2014).
Reciprocity allocation is retroactive, recognizing past utility or self-sacrifice. As such, many refer to this allocative
notion as egalitarian desert/luck justice or rectification justice rather than reciprocity allocation (Rogowski et al.,
2014). For significant health-related objectives, reciprocity may entail preferred allocation to previous organ donors,
to vaccination study participants who took on risk for the benefit of others, or to those who made healthy lifestyle
choices that reduced their need for resources. Reciprocity allocation, like instrumental value allocation, may en-
tail time-consuming, intrusive, and humiliating inquiries, such as determining whether an individual maintained a
healthy lifestyle (Rogowski et al., 2014; Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009).
Which principles best exemplify the morally significant values? Lotteries are insufficient, however, they secure
equal treatment whereas first-come, first-served is flawed as it permits irrelevant factors such as location to hos-
pitals influence allocation decisions. Similarly, the sickest-first principle is intrinsically flawed. The youngest-
first principle, while insufficient, recognizes the critical value of priority to the poorest. Both utilitarian princi-
ples—maximizing lives saved and prognosis—are noteworthy but insufficient, whereas social usefulness and reci-
procity are important in situations where irreplaceable persons make significant sacrifices, such as those during
public health emergencies.
However, no principle can account for all ethically significant concerns on its own. Combining principles into
systems adds complexity and potential for conflict, yet it is important if allocations are to reflect the complexity
of moral standards. (Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009) Often, however, guidelines are neither utilitarian
nor egalitarian in nature, as the NHS, who have incorporated a multi-principle system that distinguishes between
individuals based on their likelihood of surviving (as predicted, allegedly, by frailty), but not on their duration or
quality of life. This maximizes the number of lives saved but does not ensure equal opportunity for everyone, nor
does it maximize the good of the outcome in terms of years of life saved adjusted for their quality. (BMA, 2020).
This is just one example of a multi principal system, which an algorithm can adhere to
The term "quality-adjusted life year" (QALY) is frequently used to quantify the utility of medical therapies. (Savulescu,
Cameron, and Wilkinson, 2020; Savulescu, Persson, and Wilkinson, 2020). It builds on a utilitarian notion as the
allocation system considers the quality of life-years (Savulescu, Cameron, and Wilkinson, 2020; Savulescu, Pers-
son, and Wilkinson, 2020). It seeks to maximize future benefits, by taking to into account the quality of life. The
use of QALY is incorporated into a large number of existing, mathematically advanced frameworks. However, there
are objections to QALY, as disabled persons are disadvantaged by outcome measures, and even though a treatment
produces equal numbers of QALYs, it fails to incorporate equality. Finally, QALY allocation systems do not ade-
quately account for a number of ethically significant values—such as treating individuals equally, prioritizing the
poor, and saving the most lives—and hence are insufficient for just allocation. (Gold, Stevenson, and Fryback,
2002; Savulescu, Persson, and Wilkinson, 2020).
Another principle for determining utility is the Disability-adjusted life-years (DALY) allocation, which maximizes
future benefits and incorporates instrumental value by rescuing individuals whose productivity is critical to a flour-
ishing society. However, it suffers the same flaw as QALY, as a dependency of age-weighting, would equally warrant
counting the years of the life of economically active individuals and those who care for others. (Salomon, 2014).
Page 69 of 136
==PAGE:71==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Another principle is UNOS points systems for organ allocation in the USA. The principle makes use of the first-
come, first-served; sickest-first and prognosis, and is flexible in its usage, depending on the use case. However,
the use of UNOS is flawed, as it is based on flawed principles: first-come, first-served, and sickest-first (Persad,
Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009). The first-come, first-served as stated before would see the benefit of patients
that are well off, and is also susceptible to exploitation, as wealthy patients could enroll on various waiting lists.
Moreover, the sickest first approach can also be flawed, as some transplant centers have fabricated or falsified their
patients’ health status to get scarce organs, rendering sickest-first both practically and inherently flawed (Persad,
Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009).
Due to the fact that none of the already utilized methods meets all ethical standards for equitable allocation, Persad,
Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009 offer an alternative: the complete lives system. This system is based on five
principles: youngest-first, prognosis, saving the greatest number of lives, lottery, and instrumental value, and aims
to enable more people to live complete lives. The principles must be prioritized lexically: lesser-valued principles
should be invoked only when more-valued principles are fulfilled, which is also one of the critiques of the system, as
Rawls recognized that lexical priority was inappropriate for allocating specific resources in society (Rawls, 1971).
As such, the system gives priority to younger individuals who have not yet lived a complete life and are unlikely
to do so without assistance. Many thinkers have accepted complete lives as the appropriate focus of distributive
justice, as "individual human lives, rather than individual experiences, [are] the units over which any distributive
principle should operate.", advocating for the system to be appropriate (Bou-Habib, 2011).
Building of notions of the legal scholar Ronald Dworkin, some authors argue that the youngest-first concept should
be modified to prioritize teenagers and young adults over newborns, as, following Ronald Dworkin, despite the
awfulness of the death of an infant, it is even worse still when an adolescent dies. (Ross, 1994). Notably, prioritizing
adolescents and young adults takes into account the social and personal investment that people are morally entitled to
receive at a given age, rather than just accepting the consequences of an unjust status quo. As a result, disadvantaged
adolescents are treated equally to affluent adolescents, even if they have received less investment as a result of social
injustice (Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009). Prognosis is also a vital part of the complete lives system,
and thus when the worst-off patients benefit only modestly while the better-off benefit significantly, allocating to
the better-off is frequently justified possible. Following along these lines is the importance of saving the greatest
number of lives, which is also a goal of this system, as enabling more people to live full lives is preferable to enabling
fewer. Following the lexical order is then instrumental value, which is also a part of the system, and is important,
as it will enable more people to live complete lives (Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009). Lastly are lotteries,
which could be used to choose amongst equal receivers and also to assure that no individual—regardless of age or
prognosis—is deemed unsalvageable.
Based on the initial analysis, Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009 argue that adhering to the complete lives
system, would create a system that encompasses every relevant moral concept that is of importance, and which can
be morally justified.
However, the complete lives system is anti-aging, and allocation based on age is ageism. A policy based on age
or disability would be unethical and illegal, as it would constitute open discrimination. A 70-year-old who is in
good health cannot be lawfully denied treatment on the basis of his or her age, which is one of the drawbacks of
the system (Kerstein and Bognar, 2010). Kerstein and Bognar, 2010 argue that the system lack moral justification;
Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009 notions of giving priority to people between the ages of 15 and 40 leaves the
system vulnerable to charges of child discrimination. Second, Kerstein and Bognar, 2010 argue that the complete
lives system fails to provide significant practical guidance in critical instances as it lacks a mechanism for reconciling
conflicting principles.
Above is demonstrated the principles for allocation of scarce medical interventions. However, what should an
algorithm adhere to? Egalitarianism and utilitarianism offer different principles on how to allocate care, but as
demonstrated before there exists no universal law for fairness, thus determining which principles to incorporate for
triage algorithms in COVID-19 allocation or organ donation, is difficult, and requires a thorough examination of
the current case and algorithm, involving several stakeholders, from legal to developers, as Kristoffer Gordon of
2021.AI also highlighted 6.2.3. Moreover, no principle is sufficient on its own to recognize all morally relevant
considerations. Thus, adhering to multi-principle systems could encompass every relevant moral concept that is of
importance, and which can be morally justified, and be argued to be the best-case scenario for healthcare algorithms.
At least according to Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009.
However, how can the multi-principle system be related to metrics of fairness as Independence, Separation, and
Sufficiency? Independence can be linked to a multi-principle system building on a lottery-based principle, as
Page 70 of 136
==PAGE:72==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Independence requires the positive rate to be equal across protected groups. Independence, on the other hand,
goes beyond lottery, it is a multi-principle system that accounts for sensitive characteristics such as ethnic origin or
gender when allocating resources and equalizes positive rates among groups.
Returning to the fairness metrics of Separation and Sufficiency. A multi-principle system as the ones mentioned
above could be combined with the notions of Separation and Sufficiency. This would require a modification of
what constitutes true labels for Separation and Sufficiency, so it would reflect the multi-principle chosen, either
QALY, DALY, or the complete lives system. By modifying the true labels, to reflect the principles, the fairness
metrics can be utilized when disturbing healthcare such as ventilators or kidney transplants, ensuring fairness across
sensitive attributes, to counter any lurking biases. This however necessitates an epidemiological assessment of at-
risk groups and predicting survival rates will need to be established. Both Dr. Celi 6.2.1 and the interview with
RAIK (6.2.2) highlighted that in the real world, certain populations have a higher mortality rate, which is not
explained by disease variables, as also demonstrated by Ledford, 2019, and the case of Aalborg in Section 3.6.2.
Thus algorithms constructed to replicate the ’real’ world might end of replicating preexisting biases. True labels do
not always accurately reflect/summarize accountability variables. They are, at best, a representation of the current
state of circumstances — which may be tainted by historical injustices and discrimination (Heidari et al., 2018). As
protected attributes as race, gender, class, etc., more or have almost always affected access to opportunities such as
loans or education, such discrimination may result in an unbalanced ratio between the privileged and unprivileged
classes, these are a risk of being replicated by a model satisfying separation or sufficiency. (M. Lee, Floridi, and
Singh, 2021) Often, however, guidelines are neither utilitarian nor egalitarian in nature, as the NHS, who have
incorporated a multi-principle system that distinguishes between individuals based on their likelihood of surviving
(as predicted, allegedly, by frailty), but not on their duration or quality of life. This maximizes the number of lives
saved but does not ensure equal opportunity for everyone, nor does it maximize the good of the outcome in terms
of years of life saved adjusted for their quality. (BMA, 2020).
However, according to Persad, Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009, to avoid retreating to the status quo, society must
accept the task of adopting a coherent multi-principle framework, to ensure equitable distribution of scarce medical
interventions. Nonetheless, because it is particularly difficult to anticipate the repercussions of policies in the distant
future, any policy that imposes a clear burden on the present in exchange for a hypothetical future advantage should
be held to a high standard (Card and Smith, 2020).
7.3
Analysis of Framework Conditions and Ethical Principles for the Adoption of Socially
Beneficial AI from Section 4.7
By focusing exclusively on fairness measurements that quantify the objective outcome redistribution, a decision-
maker may neglect critical factors regarding the ethical principles for the adoption of socially beneficial AI in
Section 4.7. M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021 derive lessons from the literature on welfare economics to demonstrate
the interconnectedness of fairness beneficence and non-maleficence. Autonomy and explainability are also vital
components, that need additional addressing, however, they are left out this analysis, as the discussion often revolves
about notions of liberty (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021).
Beyond the egalitarian perspective on the relative distribution of resources between individuals and groups, it is
important to consider the aggregate impact of an algorithm on society, when applied in a social context. Due
to the difficulty of measuring significant biases and disentangling them from the desired outcome, adjusting for
biases run the risk of increasing prediction inaccuracy, which may have a costly impact on beneficence and non-
maleficence. However, there are numerous trade-offs between beneficence and non-maleficence. For instance,
the public may tolerate a given death rate for autonomous vehicles if it is lower than the fatality rate associated
with humans driving more traditional vehicle, thus striking a balance between beneficence and non-maleficence.
However, while accuracy is frequently sacrificed for fairness, from an ethical standpoint accuracy also represents
a critical principle between beneficence and non-maleficence. For instance going back to the case of the kidney
transplant list, as presented in Section 3.3.1, an algorithm satisfying beneficence would lower the demand needed,
allowing for more patients to be eligible for being added to the kidney transplant waiting list, which can indirectly
upward socioeconomic mobility. However, doing so would also increase the waiting list, putting patients at risk,
inflicting non-maleficence.
Thus it comes to a trade-off between accuracy, beneficence and non-maleficence, as lowering the criteria too much,
could potentially create more harm than good and may unwittingly hurt the subgroup the model is aiming to assist.
Thus, striking a balance between beneficence and non-maleficence is the goal, as it could be argued that the public
would tolerate some harm by the algorithm i.e., some patients are not allowed to be added to the list, as long as it
Page 71 of 136
==PAGE:73==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
is better than the existing harms caused by the current protocol.
Moreover, the ethical principle of non-maleficence may be in direct conflict with fairness in some circumstances.
Adding fairness constraints may end up harming the groups they intended to protect in the long term, by not con-
sidering possible discrimination outside of the model. In the case of extreme inequality between privileged and
unprivileged groups, by retaining the contrast, though Separation or Sufficiency, may exacerbate this disparity over
time, exacerbating representative harms, and inflicting non-maleficence, contributing to a negative feedback loop,
Figure 8 (Landeau, 2020). Adhering to Demographic Parity may also inflict non-maleficence. By providing loans
to a non-eligible borrower, the consequences, if the borrower defaults, results in his/her credit score will decline,
potentially precluding the borrower from future loans, which could potentially lead to the promotion of historical
biases and representative harms. These considerations are vital to take into account, when establishing a frame-
work, and in the presence of a feedback loop, it is necessary not only to consider the consequences of providing a
resource to an applicant in a disadvantaged group, but also what happens as a result of that resource being allocated.
The interview with Kristoffer Gordon of 2021 in Section 6.2.3, showed how developers, when constructing models,
they used the, among others, toolkits such as the one from Bellamy et al., 2019. However M. Lee, Floridi, and
Singh, 2021, 2021 argue, that relying solely on toolkits for algorithmic fairness like the one presented by Bellamy
et al., 2019, could fail to capture nuanced ethical trade-offs. Moreover, a recent survey by M. S. A. Lee and Singh,
2021, also highlights the gaps and limitations, especially in the user-friendliness and interpretability of said toolkits
and their guidance, suggesting a steep learning curve, for both experts and average practitioners with more limited
exposure to fairness metrics. Thus, according to M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021, these Fairness toolkits need
improvement from both open source communities, technology companies, and other practitioners to capture full
nuanced ethical trade-offs. while emphasizing the importance of viewing fairness, not in isolation at a moment in
time, but rather, in the context of long-term objectives, as being an important principle in the context of a framework
for AI.
As emphasized numerous times throughout this chapter and also during the interviews, particular ethical principles
and morals are frequently extremely case-specific, and are a matter of situational ethics. And they are not only case-
specific; they can also be somewhat cultural or religious. As demonstrated from Awad et al., 2018 in Section 4,
ethical disagreements are frequently the consequence of cultural, political, or religious differences, and there is no
universal truth. While public ethical preferences should not be the sole determinant of ethical policy in health care
algorithms, it does imply that politicians, programmers, and designers should be aware of the patterns of similarity
and difference in nations’ moral preferences when designing artificially intelligent systems. Additionally, a recent
investigation Ikkatai et al., 2022 by underlined the importance of context when applying AI. Their findings indicated
that public opinion on the use of AI changed based on the circumstance. They compared common themes based on
various concerns that are frequently discussed in AI standards and discovered that each of the eight themes varied
according to the application for which they were used. Moreover, the importance of fairness and nondiscrimination
differed depending on the application. Thus, to construct a proper framework, the surrounding cultural, political,
and religious circumstances, as well as the application of the algorithm must be considered closely when both using
the algorithm, but also for the surrounding framework.
7.4
Summary of Findings
The aim of the analysis was to analyze how fairness metrics for machine learning can be justified and conforms with
theories of distributive justice, and what principles for allocation of scarce medical interventions can be to guide
and justify the design of fairness criteria for algorithmic classification.
From the first part of the analysis, it is evident that depending on one’s moral preferences towards resource distribu-
tion, each metric for fairness can be justified, within the frames of their inherent assumptions. However, from the
findings of Section 5.3.5, and from Leo A. Celi 6.2.1 and the interview with Radiology Artificial Intelligence Test-
center (6.2.2), the examples of Ledford, 2019, and the case of Aalborg in Section 3.6.2, it is evident that even though
fairness metrics and tradeoffs can be justified from political philosophy, it is clear that the metrics do not guarantee
fairness. Each fairness metric that relies upon the target variable Y , has the potential to exacerbate existing dis-
parities in health, including Sufficiency and Separation. As true labels do not always accurately reflect/summarize
accountability variables and may be tainted by historical injustices, or biases, there is no guarantee that metrics
guarantee fairness, and from M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021: "if there is structural bias in the decision pipeline,
no [group fairness] mechanism can guarantee fairness” M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021. Even if the true positive
rates are equalized according to the mathematical definition of equal opportunity (Separation), a model that results
in harm to an already-disadvantaged group would not meet the Rawlsian EOP criterion. Algorithms constructed
Page 72 of 136
==PAGE:74==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
to replicate the ’real’ world might end up replicating preexisting biases, and in many fields where concerns about
algorithmic bias exist, including but not limited to healthcare, credit risk, employment, and recidivism, there has
frequently been a proven history of institutional and societal discrimination, which may alter the underlying data
(Card and Smith, 2020).
Without taking into account the long-term influence on welfare, fairness measurements fall short of capturing the
entire scope of ethical dilemmas inherent in model selection M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021. Thus, it is critical
to recognize that fairness standards defined solely in terms of a limited set of short-term measurements may not
ensure beneficial outcomes beyond what they measure, and may in some situations result in increased total harm, or
a negative feedback loop demonstrated in Figure 8. This is reinforced by a critique of existing parity measures for
categorization, in which Simoiu, Corbett-Davies, and Goel, 2017 conclude: “to the extent that error metrics differ
across groups, that tells us more about the shapes of the risk distributions than about the quality of decisions".
Without an awareness of how metrics may affect other ethical objectives, fairness measures should not be taken at
face value. (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021)
Coming back to the notion in Section 3.4.1, and the talk given by Crawford, 2017, ensuring fairness would require
both the absence of allocative harms, and representative harms. However, as demonstrated by the numerous exam-
ple, allocative harms are easily mitigated through redistribution, whereas representative harms, may be reinforced
due to implicit bias in the machine learning pipeline. If the data was drawn from a biased data sample, reflecting
the ’real world’ the algorithms might end up replicating preexisting biases, thus reinforcing or even worse amplify-
ing representative harms, even when adhering to metrics. Thus to avoid representative harms, the fairness metrics
can only be justified and used as a valid measure of algorithmic fairness, if and only if the true labels, have been
thoroughly investigated and their implications thoroughly understood in the context in which it is used. Thus it is
important to view fairness, not in isolation at a moment in time, but rather, in the context of long-term objectives.
The second part of the analysis was concerned with who should receive treatment. Is it treating people equally,
favoring the worst-off, maximizing total benefits, or promoting and rewarding social usefulness? A multi princi-
ples system could be argued to be the best-case scenario for healthcare algorithms, and by the findings of Persad,
Wertheimer, and Emanuel, 2009, adhering to the complete lives system, could create a system that encompasses
every relevant moral concept that is of importance, and which can be morally justified. However adhering to such
principles necessitate an epidemiological assessment of at-risk groups, and predictive survival rates will need to be
established, which again raises similar concerns as before. True labels do not always accurately reflect/summarize
accountability variables. As protected attributes as race, gender, class, etc., more or have almost always affected
access to opportunities such as loans or education, such discrimination may result in an unbalanced ratio between
the privileged and unprivileged classes, these are a risk of being replicated by a model satisfying these principles.
(M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021).
Lastly, the ethical principle of beneficence and non-maleficence from Section 4.7 may be in direct conflict with
fairness in some circumstances. Consequently, in the presence of a feedback loop, it is necessary to consider not
only providing a resource to an applicant in a disadvantaged group but also what happens as a result of that resource
being allocated. Thus a potential framework, cannot view fairness in isolation, as the exchange of goods to mitigate
potential allocative harms, instead, it should be analyzed in the context of long-term implications.
From the analysis, sources described and reviewed in previous chapters as well as additional literature were com-
bined to address challenges arising in the distribution of benefits and burdens, which was related to the principles
and metrics of fairness for algorithmic classification. However, models that are created to replicate the real world,
might potentially increase existing gaps and discrimination. To avoid reverting to the status quo with biased self-
perpetuating algorithms fueling negative feedback loops, society must face the challenge and risks associated with
developing a cohesive multi-principal framework for responsible AI. To do so, the project concludes with a discus-
sion, which will answer the rising concerns within the subject of fair machine learning while keeping a focal point
on the research question of how do to ensure fair and responsible AI and Machine learning for healthcare.
Page 73 of 136
==PAGE:75==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
8
Discussion on how do to ensure fair and responsible AI and Machine
learning for healthcare in practice
Most of the past chapter have dealt with how to ensure fairness in algorithms, however even when enforcing fairness
metrics, they may be not reaching their desired goal of fairness. The question then becomes, how can this be
mitigated, in order to ensure fair and responsible AI and machine learning for healthcare, that does not replicate
existing disparities.
As demonstrated in Section 4.7 adhering only to justice when constructing fair algorithms, would be insufficient:
by focusing exclusively on fairness measurements that quantify the objective outcome’s redistribution, a decision-
maker may neglect critical factors regarding the ethical principles for the adoption of socially beneficial AI, as per
Section 4.7. However, as the framework Section 4.7 only provides principles of what a framework should include,
it does not provide concrete solutions on how to attain them. Thus, the discussion will combine the work of Hond
et al., 2022 and M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021, as well findings from interviews and the analysis of previous
chapter, to formalize a framework for the responsible use of AI and machine learning in healthcare, that integrates
the notions of socially beneficial AI of beneficence, non-maleficence, autonomy, justice, and explainability.
Several researchers have tried to stipulate an adequate framework for the use of Machine Learning and AI.
As mentioned in Section 4.7, Floridi and Cowls, 2019 proposes a framework for socially beneficial AI. Luciano
Floridi who is the co-author of the paper used in Section 4.7 is also co-author of the paper M. Lee, Floridi, and
Singh, 2021 where Floridi’s initial findings are used to construct a new method that moves away from attempts
to define fairness mathematically, and instead, provides a more holistic view of the ethical considerations of a
model. M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021 framework builds on the notion that due to the subjectivity of fairness
metrics, choosing one metric another may be difficult, and instead, decision-makers should develop a customized
definition of what "fairness" is for each model, again highlighting that fairness metrics is cast of situational ethics, as
emphasized by Kristoffer Gordon in 10.3.2 and the Radiological Artificial Intelligence Testcenter in Section 6.2.2.
Moreover, M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021 stresses the importance of adhering to the concepts of 4.7, in order to
develop socially beneficial AI, and emphasizes the critical need to develop customized success metrics tailored to
the context of each model, which, entails taking into account welfare (beneficence and non-maleficence), autonomy,
fairness, and explainability, in the ethical analysis.
As Heidari et al., 2018 mentioned in the earlier chapters when debating the fairness metrics through political phi-
losophy, determining accountability features and effort-based utility, should be resolved through the appropriate
process with input from stakeholders and domain experts, and not include the expertise of computer scientists, as it
is outside their domain. M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021 disagree, and argue the engineers’ roles and responsibili-
ties are indistinguishably linked to those of the expert or business stakeholder, as the ethical and practical definitions
of what constitutes "success" in the model directly influence the algorithm’s design, construction, and testing. The
developer and domain expert must collaborate actively to determine which inequities should influence the outcome
and how to resolve those that should not, which requires collaboration from all relevant stakeholders including the
business owner and the technical owner, with potential input from regulators, customers, and legal experts (M. Lee,
Floridi, and Singh, 2021). Others have joined in on the discussion on the development of a framework ensuring
the safe and responsible application of AI in healthcare. Hond et al., 2022 have performed a scoping review of
the relevant literature, that provides guidance or quality criteria regarding the development, evaluation, and imple-
mentation of AI within healthcare, to identify meaningful guidance for people involved in the creation, evaluation,
and deployment of AI-based prediction models (AIPMs). This includes software engineers, data scientists, and
healthcare professionals for healthcare use, and entails six predefined phases.
Due to the wide recognition and industry-proven use to guide data-mining efforts, the CRISP-DM model will be
adopted in the framework to leverage the benefits of the CRISP-DM model. As evidenced by both M. Lee, Floridi,
and Singh, 2021 and Hond et al., 2022, there is a high degree of consistency between their stages and the steps
described by the CRISP-DM model in Figure 5. Thus instead of developers and stakeholders being faced with
difficulties or information overload, the aim is, that it could build on the already established knowledge of the
CRISP-DM model.
Each step, of the following established framework, is not necessarily consecutive, but shares dependencies with the
preceding phases and is subject to change, as the CRISP-DM. The order in which the phases occur is determined
by the outcome of each step. When a project is ready for deployment, the model is evaluated with all stakeholders
to identify any missing components. A model review can generate fresh insights and sharpen the model’s focus,
Page 74 of 136
==PAGE:76==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
resulting in a more complete picture of ethical criteria for "success", which underlines the iterative process of
the framework. It is important to notice that not all the findings from Hond et al., 2022 are highlighted within
the discussion, as some of them are of more relevant than others when discussing the topic of responsible AI in
healthcare. Notions of critical network infrastructure and hospital security systems, are not examined to a great
extension.
Phase 1: Define ethical success
The first step along the process is to define what constitutes a successful algorithm in the given setting. Moreover,
how can fairness be defined in the applied setting? Both papers highlight this as being the first course of action and
share similar principles of how to define it. Following the lines of M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021, the first step is to
define the ethical "success" of a model, in terms of what benefit does a more precise algorithm provides to patients,
society, and systems? What are the possible consequences of false positives and negatives? Are any fundamental
rights in jeopardy? Each use case has unique concerns for what constitutes a "successful" model, which is unlikely
to be covered by a single mathematical formula. Fairness, as underlined before, cannot be viewed in isolation, and
it is critical to specify all relevant objectives so that trade-offs may be easily identified, allowing for a more holistic
understanding of algorithmic ethics. Hond et al., 2022 findings share similar principles, as to prevent algorithmic
bias, there needs to be a definition of fairness that corresponds with the models’ intended use, and developers and
stakeholders must address, what constitutes fairness, and how is defined in the observed setting. What constitutes
fairness is of course difficult, but 7.1, offers some idea of fairness can be determined, and what much be considered
when defining fairness. However, fairness cannot be viewed in isolation. As the first step is to define what constitutes
ethical success, it is not only limited to fairness but also the other pillars of 4.7, of beneficence, non-maleficence,
autonomy, and explicability, which should be both considered and included, to provide a holistic view of a model.
Thus the consideration of the beginning of this chapter should be included as well. What is the trade-off between
beneficence and non-maleficence, and how can autonomy be ensured explicability, while adhering to a principle
of fairness. The interview from Kristoffer Gordon in Section 6.2.3, also demonstrates this point, when developing
algorithms, as there needs to be a declared definition, between relevant stakeholders, where this algorithm is headed,
and what the intended purpose is. Depending on the application, it may be that no discrimination based on gender,
ethnic origin, or region of origin is permitted. There is no silver bullet, that identifies the optimal balance; rather,
it is an outcome determined by the interaction of several stakeholders, who collectively choose which outcome is
the best. In other words, what constitutes ’success’.
Moreover, fundamental questions must be determined before constructing a healthcare model. What characteristics
of a medical or healthcare problem or setting increase the likelihood that the adoption of a model will result in
improved patient care, and when are the conditions necessary to commence a model development? There is a need
to precisely characterize the medical condition and context that the model will address, as well as the healthcare
setting(s) in which the model would be deployed, as well as a clear rationale for why the current approach falls
short. What would be the success of a model is implemented in healthcare, and what medical condition will the
models successfully address? Hond et al., 2022
Thus the first step is to define what constitutes ethical success. Notions from the previous chapters and sections can
be used in the determination of what ethical principles and norms should be used to guide the design of fairness
criteria for algorithmic classification. Both from Section 7.1, but also the multi-principle systems of Section 7.2.
However, this is not only limited to fairness but also the other pillar of EAI, which should be both considered
and included, to provide a holistic view of a model. The ’success’ of a model should be integrated with model
development as part of the models’ evaluation metrics, and always be adhered to.
Phase 2: Detecting of representativeness, potential biases, inequalities or discrimination
As definitions of ethical success and fairness are defined, these should be adhered to when determining if there are
any potential biases, inequality, or discrimination there might exist within the data, thus ensuring the notions of
beneficence, non-maleficence. Are the model fulfilling the desired aspirations for fairness and does any potential
bias tamper with the desired ethical goal of the algorithm?
The next step, involves model selection, training, and validation of the model, to determine if any discrimination
occurs or inequalities exist within the data, to determine, what kind of potential bias could influence the model. The
mapped definition of fairness from Phase 1, can be utilized to determine whether there is any algorithmic bias in
adherence to fairness metrics such as Independence, Separation, and Sufficiency, that require potential mitigation.
These metrics together with other performance evaluations metrics as AROC in Figure 7, and the quantitative met-
rics for Classification Models in Section 3.3, are frequently used to compute various individual and group fairness
metrics to check for bias in datasets and models (Hond et al., 2022; M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021). The team at
Page 75 of 136
==PAGE:77==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
Radiological Artificial Intelligence Testcenter at Herlev Hospital is using the same steps when investigating biases
in the dataset and stresses the importance of this practice. Moreover, as emphasized by Hond et al., 2022 there is a
need to ensure a reasonable sample size for the intended purpose, and proper representativeness of the target pop-
ulation and intended healthcare setting, to sufficiently cover the relevant real-world heterogeneity and diversity. If
the data is not representative, it defeats the whole purpose. The interviews, from both Kristoffer Gordon in Section
6.2.3, Leo A. Celi in Section 6.2.1 and the Radiological Artificial Intelligence Testcenter at 6.2.2 also emphasized
the importance of having a representative dataset, which underlines its critical significance. When the bias is caused
by unrepresentative training data, the main recommendation is to redo the data collection to rectify this. Unrep-
resentative training data may also be addressed by undersampling the overrepresented group or oversampling the
underrepresented group. However, this may cause miscalibration of the model predictions and should be used with
caution (Hond et al., 2022). In addition, it is also important to test a model’s ability to generalize its performance
to a new setting. To guarantee that the AIPM is generalizable to the target healthcare setting, developers should
evaluate the model extensively using representative data from that setting. Consequently, the recommendation is
to evaluate generalizability using data from another time period, location, or healthcare setting (Hond et al., 2022).
Additionally, it is also of critical importance to explicitly report any detected sources of algorithmic bias, so that
end-users are aware of instances where the model’s predictive performance falls short of expectations. Numerous
systems will exhibit some degree of inequity in their output, and so a baseline comparison to the algorithmic bias
of current systems may be explored (Hond et al., 2022). Along with the inequalities outlined above, there may be
biases throughout the machine learning lifecycle that worsen existing inequities between groups. The difficulty is
that the patterns linked with the desired goal are frequently also related to an individual’s identity, including race
and gender. By identifying the type of bias present in adherence to Section 3.4, the developer may pinpoint the
phase in which the bias was introduced, enabling the development of a focused mitigation approach for each bias
type. This importance was also highlighted by the interview Leo A. Celi in Section 6.2.1, in which he explains
that additional ways that can be used in conjunction with dataset diversification, including launching adversarial
attacks, are important in determining any implicit biases. This stems from models frequently employing proxies to
determine an individual’s values, such as gender. To address this issue, Leo A. Celi discusses the expanding usage
of counterfactual prediction to assess whether algorithms would predict the same outcome if certain features were
altered, for example, if a black person’s zip code was changed to that of a white person
However, due to the complex and entangled sources of inequalities and bias affecting an algorithm, there is no simple
mathematical solution to unfairness. It is critical to understand which sorts of inequality are acceptable in each use
case and which are not, in adhering with Table 5, which presented different layers of inequality (M. Lee, Floridi, and
Singh, 2021). Considering health evaluation, or screening for breast cancer, natural inequality may be considered
relevant: if a woman has a higher risk of getting cancer than a man, she may be more entitled to receive treatment,
given her born disability. By compelling the decisionmaker to look beyond legally protected features to distinguish
between acceptable and relevant inequalities and those that are not, it can more accurately identify subgroups at
risk of discrimination ( M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021). Kristoffer Gordon and Sune Hannibal also underline this
point in the interviews in Section 6.2.3 and Section is 6.2.4, as there are moments where discrimination could be
desirable. However, as Kristoffer Gordon highlights this should always be done in close accordance with both legal
staff and other relevant stakeholders to ascertain what they should pay particular attention to and what is desired
output.
After biases are identified, they should be addressed through the use of an appropriate mitigation strategy which
may vary depending on the application and domain.
Phase 3: Mitigation strategy
The next step is to mitigate any potential biases within the data. Biases can be mitigated through the use of several
techniques, e.g. Bellamy et al., 2019 toolkit, a practice shared by Kristoffer Gordon of 2021. A viewpoint Hond
et al., 2022 and M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021 converge on.
This includes methods for pre-processing, which involves removing bias from data before the algorithm’s construc-
tion, in-processing, which involves building an algorithm with bias-related constraints, and post-processing, which
involves adjusting an algorithm’s output predictions. However, this is depending on whether the decision-maker
has access to the model, as demonstrated in Section 5.2, as the technique are depending on the access level of the
decision-maker.
These methods, however, presuppose that the inequities in Table 5 and the biases in Section 3.4 are quantifiable and
can be surgically addressed. While mitigation methods are necessary, they are unlikely to be sufficient to address the
issue of algorithmic bias and fairness. That is because, unlike the assumptions underlying fairness formalizations,
Page 76 of 136
==PAGE:78==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
it is frequently not possible to analytically quantify and surgically eliminate unfair prejudice from a model that is
affected by systemic inequalities and biases. (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021). Hond et al., 2022 raise similar
concerns, and additionally underline that it is worth noting that designers’ prejudices and biases can be reproduced
in their modeling choices. As a result, it is deemed critical to assemble a diverse development team, raise awareness,
and involve stakeholders in design decisions, and developers should constantly evaluate algorithmic bias throughout
the development process.
Phase 4: Development of the software application
This phase delves deeper into risk management than the previous phases and covers a broader range of themes related
to AI integration, as it relates to the development of the software application, which includes the programming,
design, implementation, and support of a model’s digital packaging. Four steps are deemed of critical importance
when developing the software application, as also highlighted by Hond et al., 2022: Interoperability, Human–AI
interaction, Facilitating software updating and monitoring, and software testing.
Interoperability between models and various current digital infrastructures in hospitals and clinical care centers
is critical for their successful integration into clinical practice. Additionally, numerous studies propose the usage
of open source or publicly available libraries in the model’s software implementation to boost the model’s overall
accessibility, thus incorporating notions of explainability from Section 4.7. (Hond et al., 2022).
For models uptake and successful and safe use in daily clinical practice, a well-designed user interface is a critical
need. It has been advocated to incorporate professionals in user interface design during the software development
stage, as creating an effective user interface and interaction takes careful consideration of the end users’ cognitive
load Hond et al., 2022. It is recommended that end users receive a model facts label that contains the system’s
technical specifications, statistical operation, restrictions, fairness standards, validation, implementation disclaimer,
and links to process logs, which was also addressed in the interview with Leo A. Celi in Section 6.2.1, who urged
the implementation of the concept of disparity dashboards.
Following along these lines is the importance of facilitating software updating and monitoring. From the perspective
of user interface design, it has been advocated that decisions are deterministic (consistently producing the same
output for a given input) and that updates and adaptations to the model are made cautiously. End users should be
notified clearly and concisely of any modifications to the model. Finally, because monitoring and auditing models
in practice are widely suggested (and discussed in greater detail in the following phase), the developed software
should make this possible (Hond et al., 2022).
Phase 5: Impact assessment and Key Ethics Indicators
The adherence to the "success" of a model as described in phase 1, should be a constant subject to compliance. An
impact assessment is conducted to ascertain a model’s clinical use in clinical practice. This is critical to remember,
as a model’s success in terms of discrimination and calibration (phases 2 and 3) does not always equate to clinical
value. (Hond et al., 2022). A prospective comparative study approach is recommended for impact studies, where the
effects on clinical outcomes and decision-making are examined between a group exposed to the AI’s predictions and
a non-exposed control group receiving standard care in comparative research. This sort of study entails multiple
live clinical tests in which variance is critical for understanding the technology’s and workflow’s functionality.
Additionally, it is recommended to describe any variations in healthcare settings between the present and prior
(validation) studies and to specify the inclusion and exclusion criteria at the participant and input data level. The
latter is also highlighted by Leo A. Celi in Section 6.2.1, as certain demographic groups may not be in the data
gathered, as they are excluded, which developers might be unaware of, again highlighting the need for a diverse
team. The team at Radiological Artificial Intelligence Testcenter at Herlev Hospital is using the same steps when
investigating the clinical impact of their models and stresses the importance of this practice, as many as many
clinical algorithms, with high ARUC performance, often fail with real clinical data (See Section 6.2.2).
Due to the importance of thorough assessment of the clinical impact, then similar to how a business may define a
set of quantifiable values to measure its performance through Key Performance Indicators (KPIs), there could be
developed outcome-based, quantifiable statements from an ethical standpoint: Key Ethics Indicators (KEI). The
KEIs would enable developers to manage and track the extent to which each model meets stated objectives from
phase 1, and what the impact of the model is (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021).
Context-specific KEIs can be constructed for each use case, and by explicitly stating the ethical aims for each
use case, decision-makers can justify the usage of any algorithm, which could result in the formation of industry
standards and guidance on best practices (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021). This will ensure that model is not
only just, but also provide explainability as to why. Leo A. Celi also highlighted the importance of an impact study,
Page 77 of 136
==PAGE:79==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
and by using the KEI, they can be used to provide a basis for a feasibility-and impact study, as these are of vital
importance when determining if the model adheres to the ethical principles of phase 1 Hond et al., 2022. The KEI
can be displayed, by using the disparity dashboards, as proposed in phase 4, which in turn would determine any
inequalities. If the model is not performing in the way it was intended, the earlier phases need to be revisited.
Phase 6: Model roll-out and use in daily healthcare practice
The last step is the model roll-out or model deployment, which both entails the justification of using a model for
clinical use, as well as the ensuring that a model not only works now but also consistently adheres to the established
ethical "success" to prevent a negative feedback loop.
By using the KEI provides from phase 5, the concrete stakes for the decision-makers are quantified, allowing him or
her to select the model that is most suited to their priorities, customized for each use case, which can then be used and
implemented with needed justification through the KEIs (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021). One of the primary
benefits of outcome-driven KEI trade-off analysis is that can change the status quo. They provide interpretable
and actionable insights into the decision-maker’s value which is especially crucial for complex machine learning
algorithms whose actual mechanism may be complex or incomprehensible, thus providing explainability, to why
a model may be beneficent and non-maleficent for some patients. Moreover, they offer the regulator valuable
justification for why a certain model was deemed preferable to all other plausible options. Lastly, they may assist in
soothing the decision-makers’ reservations about the deployment of machine learning models due to their hidden
risks, if the study demonstrated that they outperform traditional rules-based models in meeting all of the KEIs (M.
Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021).
To ensure the benefits of a model throughout its lifetime it is necessary to explicitly disclose what part of deci-
sion making might be affected by model predictions, thus adhering to the principle of explainability, together with
integrated mechanisms for real-time monitoring before model deployment. From the interview with Leo A. Celi
in Section 6.2.1, the importance of auditing algorithms, conducting due diligence, and ensuring that a model per-
forms desirable, is a critical need. This is vital to ensure the fair use of algorithms, and Leo A. Celi cannot stress
this enough. It is of utmost importance to monitor the algorithms, cause the outcomes and predictions, are never
known. Moreover, institutions and implementers should disclose their innovation pathways, including the routes to
commercialization. The risks, investments, roles, and responsibilities of the different parties may inform the allo-
cation of benefits in a commercial arrangement (Hond et al., 2022). After deployment developers need to update
their AIPMs frequently to increase prediction performance as new advancements become available and to prevent
dataset shift. A dataset shift is a change in the composition of the input data over time as a result of changes in
clinical or operational practices, which might result in a decline in AIPM performance (Hond et al., 2022).
It is also recommended to enable automatic rollback for production models to address real-time operational con-
cerns, thus enforcing the concept of autonomy from 4.7. Additionally, a technique for securely terminating an
operation is strongly recommended if the system should be ceased from usage owing due to safety issues. (Hond
et al., 2022)
Moreover, models should always be subject to auditing, where a full audit trail should be implemented, in which the
model predictions, model version, input data, and usage practices are methodologically logged and made available
to interested third parties. Additionally, certain parts of monitoring require special investigation. The monitoring
of model fairness during its lifecycle is of utmost importance, for example, by measuring the rate of false-positive
and false-negative prediction across different subgroups, but also the monitoring of dataset shifts (Hond et al.,
2022). Lastly, comprehensive monitoring would also include the monitoring of feedback loops, which arise, as a
consequence of flawed models. Additionally, end-users should receive general education as well as application-
specific training. The end-user should be educated about a model’s underlying assumptions, legal framework,
benefits, risks, and (technical) restrictions, for example. By providing samples of improperly classified cases, the
end-user can gain a better understanding of a model’s strengths and limits.
The six phases as demonstrated above are used to formalize the framework for responsible AI for healthcare, inspired
by the work of the CRISP-DM model, which is depicted below:

Figure 15: Framework for the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare. The iterative
process is symbolized by the outer arrows. Adopted by Chapman et al., 1999.
8.1
Conclusion on Discussion
The aim of the discussion was to answer the question of how to ensure algorithmic fairness in practice, which was
addressed through the construction of the framework.
As mentioned, the construction of responsible AI in healthcare is an iterative process, which is why is each phase
is not required to be sequential, but is dependent on the preceding phases and is subject to change. One of the most
important phases of the framework is the establishment of the ethical goal. Notions from the previous chapters and
sections, can be used in the determination of what ethical principles and norms should be used to guide the design
of fairness criteria for algorithmic classification, and to what constitutes "success". The order in which the phases
occur is determined by the outcome of each step. When a project is ready for deployment, the model is evaluated
with all stakeholders to identify any gaps. A revision of the model may elicit fresh findings and sharpen the model’s
emphasis, resulting in a more accurate definition of ethical success. The iterative process is symbolized by the outer
arrows in Figure 15.
The framework can assist in establishing the necessary structure for developing a responsible AI and machine learn-
ing that integrates all the critical components, established from relevant literature and Section 4.7, throughout the
model’s phases, from the initial phase through deployment. It will offer a shared reference point for all parties, facil-
itating communication and documentation of outcomes and increasing awareness of the critical nature of modeling
concerns. The model will not only benefit the organizational mindset and structure; it will also ensure a thorough
ethical understanding in which project objectives and requirements are highlighted and made explicit, allowing for
the objective and goals to be determined and translated into a complete ethical model.
Additionally, given the lack of user-friendliness of algorithmic Fairness metrics tools, as demonstrated in M. S. A.
Lee and Singh, 2021, one could argue that given the widespread recognition and industry-proven application that an
adaptation of the CRISP-DM as an overarching framework for developing Responsible AI can be used for purposes
Page 79 of 136
==PAGE:81==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
outside of healthcare.
However, one of the limitations of this framework is its lack of testing. Moreover, the framework is overly broad.
Because this model, has been cherry-picking from existing guidelines from literature, it may lack important steps,
that are neglected, in the process. Moreover, the framework is only theoretical, however, builds on other tested
framework applications as Hond et al., 2022, and methods used 2021.ai and the Radiology Artificial Intelligence
Testcenter, for developing and testing algorithms. Thus, the framework shows some potential in terms of feasibility;
nonetheless, future research in the topic of framework feasibility may be worthwhile, examining whether supplied
rules are applicable and lasting, as well as accomplishing their stated objectives.
Due to the limitations discussed above, satisfying the framework should not be regarded as conclusive evidence of
how to ensure fair and responsible AI and machine learning for healthcare. Likewise, violations of the framework
do not inflict unfairness. Rather than that, the framework can be seen a reasonable method for identifying and
quantifying potential areas of concern that deserve additional study.

==PAGE:82==
