February 13, 2022
9
Conclusion
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare are not easy concepts to define.
The project aimed to answer the rising questions of what the types of biases that appear in ML-based algorithms
are, how fairness in Machine Learning classification can be defined, what principles should guide machine learning
systems, and how we can ensure fair and responsible AI and Machine learning for healthcare in practice.
The first research question, the project aimed at answering was, what are the types of biases that appear in machine
learning-based algorithms in healthcare, what are their sources, and what are their consequences to patients? As
demonstrated throughout 3, biases can have devastating consequences, which can result in the loss of life, as both
demonstrated in Section 3.4.3, and further elaborated in Section 3.6. Moreover existing discriminatory practices
can perpetuate an algorithm, copying existing societal biases constructing a negative feedback loop, that maintains
existing discriminatory practices, or even worse, amplify them, as illustrated in Figure 8.
From there, the project started to explore the second, fourth and fifth research questions. As machine learning is
the distribution of goods, either treatment, loans, etc., it is relevant to induce a fairness discussion from the view
of distributive justice. In Section 4.1 notions of distributive fairness were listed, in which concepts of distributive
justice were elaborated, such as Rawls EOP, Luck Egalitarian, Libertarianism and Utilitarianism, and how they
can be used in machine learning systems, thus providing preliminary insights to the fourth research question. Each
principle has its advantages and disadvantages, and the different notions demonstrate that there is no clear definition
of what constitutes fairness, however there is a link between said principles and machine learning. However, as
demonstrated in Section 4.7, adhering to fairness is just one of the needed critical principles for the adoption of
socially beneficial AI. The ensuring of beneficence, non-maleficence autonomy, and explainability is also of vital
importance. The framework holds enormous promise for how AI should be applied, furthermore it gives preliminary
insights to the fifth research question and was thus used in later sections. Nonetheless, the rules remain normative;
they do not provide precise answers to the question of what ethical criteria and principles should be used to guide
algorithms.
Fair machine learning models can be described as the absence of bias or partiality in decision-making for an in-
dividual or group based on their intrinsic or acquired attributes. Thus, an unfair algorithm is one that is biased
in favor of a set of people (Mehrabi et al., 2019). While fairness is a highly desirable quality in society, it can be
rather challenging to accomplish in practice. By using relevant literature, three overarching concepts of fair ma-
chine learning were defined, described, and discussed. The metrics can be used for performance evaluation, but
also be computed into the model both when developing, during pre-processing, in-processing, or post-processing.
The extensive exploration of fairness in machine learning provided answers to the second question.
However defining fairness in an algorithm is difficult due to the lack of a universal law of fairness, and recent
research revealed that many of these notions of fairness are flawed. Either because they contradict the system’s
original objective - i.e. they reduce predictive accuracy - because they do not conform or moral notions of fairness,
or they contradict each other (Yang, Cissé, and Koyejo, 2020; Liu et al., 2019; V. Carey, 2020).
When applying separation- or sufficiency-based criteria, care must be taken to ensure that the target variable Y is
not susceptible to prejudice and injustice. Unfortunately, as Section 5.3.5 demonstrated, there is a growing wariness
that, even though fairness metrics are applied, there is a challenge in practice, as true labels do not always accurately
reflect/summarize accountability variables. They are, at best, a representation of the current state of circumstances
— which may be tainted by historical injustices and discrimination (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021). Even if
cognizant of the target variable Y , another shortcoming is imposed by the Impossible Theorem of Section 5.3.6,
which demonstrated that multiple criteria can not be imposed as hard constraints simultaneously.
Fair machine learning faces several conceptual ethical difficulties: accepting that no single comprehensive metric
of fairness is available, the project tried to answer how may we decide which metric to apply in a given context?
However, as humans have different sets of moral principles, and diverse cultural preferences and outlooks support
varying viewpoints on fairness, a single quantitative definition or metric that is acceptable to everyone in a given
situation, is highly complex, which underscores the difficulty of resolving this issue (Suresh and Guttag, 2021).
For instance, notions of what constitutes fairness, and what should be equal for patients can be argued to differ
for healthcare. Going back to the example of the kidney transplant waiting list of Section 3.3.1, for the decision-
maker, it could be that of those that have been labeled eligible for a kidney transplant, how many did truly need
it? For the patient, it could be the probability of being correctly classified? For society, it could be if the selected
set is demographically balanced? (Barocas, Hardt, and Narayanan, 2018)? As algorithms have a direct impact on
our daily lives, it is critical to not only grasp when and what algorithmic fairness is, but algorithms and models
Page 81 of 136
==PAGE:83==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
should also to some extent reflect what individuals believe constitutes fairness. To address this a questionnaire
was conducted in Section 6.1 to uncover individuals’ beliefs towards fairness in healthcare algorithms and what
principles of fairness for machine learning from Chapter 5 algorithms should adhere to. The questionnaire aimed at
answering the third research question of how we may elicit people’s perceptions of algorithmic fairness. Due to the
shortcomings of the questionnaire, it cannot be concluded what individuals’ perception of algorithmic fairness is,
which is one of the limitations of this project. There is still unclarity to the third research question, which requires
further examination. However, from the questionnaire and Section 5.3.6, there was an indication, that a trade-off
may be the best solution for algorithms. This notion was analyzed to a greater extent, by interviewing domain
experts. Moreover, as fairness is only one pillar of the framework in Section4.7, the experts were also questioned
regarding the considerations needed when constructing algorithms for healthcare.
The interview with Leo A. Celi addressed essential insights on how to construct a framework for responsible AI,
which was resonated through the interviews of Kristoffer Gordon of 2021.ai, and the Radiological Artificial In-
telligence Testcenter at Herlev Hospital. Moreover from both Kristoffer of 2021, and from Radiological Artificial
Intelligence Testcenter, it was clear that in general no metrics is better than others, which constitutes that the fairness
metrics could be a case of situational ethics, in which the moral decision making is contextual or dependent on a
set of circumstances (6.2.3,6.2.2).
Leo A. Celi’s biggest concern was that algorithms may continue replicating existing inequities, allowing biases to
be reinforced and scaled, creating a negative feedback loop, which in turn might potentially increase existing gaps
and discrimination, as previously shown by Ledford, 2019, which was the primary finding from the analysis. From
the findings of Chapter 5, and the interviews in Chapter 6 it was evident that even though fairness metrics and
trade-offs can be justified and conform to moral notions of fairness, it does not guarantee fairness. Each fairness
metric that relies upon Y , has the potential to exacerbate existing disparities in health, including Sufficiency and
Separation. In reality, certain groups have a greater mortality rate than others, which cannot be explained by disease
variables. As previously stated, real labels may not always adequately reflect/summarize accountability elements
and may be tainted by historical injustices or biases; fairness metrics do not ensure fairness, and from M. Lee,
Floridi, and Singh, 2021: "if there is structural bias in the decision pipeline, no [group fairness] mechanism can
guarantee fairness” M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021. As a result, this implies that algorithms, even if adhering
to fairness metrics, will continue to contain these discrepancies, fueling a negative feedback look, as depicted in
Figure 8. Thus, it is critical to recognize that fairness standards defined solely in terms of a limited set of short-term
measurements may not ensure beneficial outcomes beyond what they measure, and may in some situations result
in increased total harm, or a negative feedback loop.
While addressing fairness in machine learning, it was also vital to determine who should receive treatment. In Sec-
tion 7.2, it was determined that a multi-principle system, could be argued to be the best-case scenario for healthcare
algorithm, as such systems have the ability to encompass relevant moral concept that is of importance, and which
can be morally justified. However, adhering to such principles often necessitate an epidemiological assessment
of at risk groups, and predicting survival rates which raise similar concerns to before. Thus enforcing them into
notions of fairness for algorithmic classification, should be treated with caution, as demonstrated from the analysis
of Chapter 7.
Furthermore, the ethical principle of beneficence and non-maleficence from Section 4.7 may be in direct conflict
with fairness in some circumstances. Consequently, in the presence of a feedback loop, it is necessary to consider not
only providing a resource to an applicant in a disadvantaged group, but also what happens as a result of that resource
being allocated. Thus a potential framework, cannot view fairness in isolation, instead, it should be analyzed in the
context of long-term implications. (M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021).
The analysis in Chapter 7, combining of Section 7.1 7.3 and 7.3 provide answers insights to the fourth research
question.
However, substantial body of research indicates that data-driven risk assessment instruments are generally more
accurate than professional human judgments (Chouldechova, 2016). So, to avoid reverting to the status quo with
biased self-perpetuating algorithms fueling negative feedback loops, society must face the challenge and risks as-
sociated with developing a cohesive multi-principal framework for fair and responsible AI and Machine learning
for healthcare. To do so, the project concluded with a discussion, which combined the work of Hond et al., 2022
and M. Lee, Floridi, and Singh, 2021, as well findings from interviews and analysis, to formalize a framework for
fair and responsible AI and Machine learning for healthcare, that integrated the notions of socially beneficial AI of
beneficence, non-maleficence, autonomy, justice, and explainability.
The resulting framework is visualized in Figure 15 and consists of the six phases: Define ethical success, detection
Page 82 of 136
==PAGE:84==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
of potential bias and discrimination, mitigation Strategy, development of software application, Impact assessment
and KEIs, and model rollout. The framework can assist in establishing the necessary structure for developing a
multi-principal framework for fair and responsible AI and machine learning that integrates all the critical compo-
nents, established from relevant literature in Section 4.7, and findings from the analysis, throughout the model’s
phases, from the initial phase through deployment. It can offer a shared reference point for all parties, facilitating
communication and documentation of outcomes and increasing awareness of the critical nature of modeling con-
cerns. Moreover, it can ensure a thorough ethical understanding in which project objectives and requirements are
highlighted and made explicit, allowing for the objective and goals to be determined and translated into a complete
ethical model.
Due to the framework’s limitations, compliance with the framework should not be viewed as conclusive evidence of
how to provide ethical and responsible AI and machine learning in healthcare. Similarly, infractions of the frame-
work do not result in unfairness. Rather than that, the framework can be viewed as a rational tool for identifying
and measuring possible areas of concern that merit further investigation.
9.1
Limitations and self-reflections
This project may be criticized for at too great dependence on grey literature used from arXiv and articles in con-
ference proceedings. These papers have been presented at conferences and are working papers, however, some
of them are not peer-reviewed journal articles. The lack of peer-reviewed papers, hinders the scientific impact of
these projects, as conclusions that are drawn from these papers could be lacking essential review. While the use
of non-peer reviewed sources raises concerns, it is necessary to acknowledge that in a highly dynamic field such
as algorithmic fairness several important contributions are found in early non-peer-reviewed publications. Along
these lines are the limitations due to the selected material. As the PubMed and Google Scholar search yielded
several important articles, all of them could not be included. While the exclusion of articles raises concerns, it is
necessary to acknowledge that in a highly dynamic field such as algorithmic fairness, articles, that are not included
in this master’s thesis, can be just as significant as those that are.
Moreover, in computer science, the majority of work on proposing new fairness constraints for algorithms comes
from western literature. Thus, there is an insufficient representation in the literature. (Mehrabi et al., 2019). More-
over, as Awad et al., 2018 demonstrated, ethical disagreements are frequently the consequence of conflicting in-
dividual interpretations of the issue at hand, which might be cultural, political, or religious. Thus the lack of
representation in literature poses additional limitations, as agreeable concepts may not be the most appropriate for
particular problems (Mehrabi et al., 2019).
Another limitation is the selection of experts, who were not chosen systematically and rigorously due to time pres-
sure and pandemic restrictions, and can thus be characterized as a convenience sample, which was one of the
foreseen constraints of this project. Expert knowledge that was excluded, could have provided additional insights
and contributed to new findings.
The questionnaire aimed to provide answers to the third research question. Due to shortcomings of the questionnaire
in terms of responses, it cannot be concluded how individuals’ perception of algorithmic fairness is, which is one
of the limitations of this project. There is still unclarity to the of this matter, which requires further examination.
However, the significant work on developing the questionnaire, in terms of literature review, was beneficial, since
the knowledge obtained might be applied in future work when developing new questionnaires.
Moreover, one of the limitations of this framework is its lack of testing, as well as the fact that the framework is
overly broad. Because the framework was developed by cherry-picking from existing guidelines from literature,
it may lack important steps, that are neglected, in the process. However, the framework does show some poten-
tial in terms of feasibility, due to sources from which it was inspired; nonetheless, future research in the topic of
framework feasibility may be worthwhile, examining whether supplied rules are applicable and lasting, as well as
accomplishing their stated objectives. Due to the limitations discussed above, satisfying the framework should not
be regarded as conclusive evidence of how to ensure fair and responsible AI and machine learning for healthcare.
Likewise, violations of the framework do not inflict unfairness. Rather than that, the framework can be seen as a
reasonable method for identifying and quantifying potential areas of concern that deserve additional study.
As algorithmic fairness in healthcare lack the contextual context and causal awareness required to reason about
the mechanisms underlying health disparities outside of a model, as well as the potential for algorithmic fairness
methods to mitigate those mechanisms, this needs further addressing. Future work should require developers and
stakeholders involved in the development of predictive models for clinical application to think critically about the
Page 83 of 136
==PAGE:85==
Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
broader sociotechnical context surrounding the use of machine learning in healthcare. However, instead of revert-
ing to the status quo with self-perpetuating biased algorithms, society must face the challenge and risks associated
with developing a cohesive multi-principal framework for responsible AI, and research should not abandon its data-
driven approach, as a substantial body of research indicates that data-driven risk assessment instruments are gen-
erally more accurate than professional human judgments (Chouldechova, 2016). Rather than that, research should
focus on ensuring that the instruments utilized do not contain quantifiable biases that could result in inconsistent
impact in the situations in which they are to be used.

Please elaborate on your choices (optional)
Equal opportunity has better true positive rates and negative predictive rates.  Though I’d want to better understand the
implications of a higher false positive rate and a longer wait list time period
 
Please elaborate on your choices (optional)
Maximizing the impact of the transplants seems more fair, so the equal opportunity is the more fair choice.  But the hospital
may get more negative PR for not giving a transplant to someone who needs it, so they want to maximize negative predictive
rate
 
Please elaborate on your choices (optional)
Demographic has a similar threshold between the two groups, so it’s hard to argue to change that threshold unless the rates are
better for every group

Because it biased? Is that Yeah, bias? Um, so several, the first is that it doesn't represent the 
people who have the worst outcomes or who are marginalized. So one of the things that I would 
I would complain about, is sepsis, sepsis, as you know, it's one of the most common cause of death 
in the intensive care unit, it's infection that leads to a dysregulated response. And the death rate 
from sepsis is anywhere from 15% to 50%, no matter depending on how sick you are. And the 
guidelines for treating sepsis come from research that are that's performed primarily in the US 
and in the UK, and Germany and Scandinavia, when the burden from sepsis is much, much 
bigger in Asia, in the poorer countries. So now, these guidelines are used around the world, even 
though there's no guarantee that the research that is performed in a few rich countries, and that 
would primarily enroll white individuals will also be applicable to the rest of the world. So there, 
there is bias, because the data that is being used to inform the the treatment protocols is 
coming from not even the majority is the majority of people are actually Asians and Africans and 
South Americans. So that's one of the biases that there's not there's not a good representation of 
populations in the data. So that whatever model that you come up with will not work will not 
necessarily be accurate. So whatever treatment recommendations that we provide to the rest of 
the world. And then the other thing, too, that we complain about is the committee that forms this 
guidelines are also primarily from rich countries. And that is a. I mean, that's really unfortunate, 
because as they said, the burden of sepsis is so much more in poor countries. The other source of 
bias is primarily from sampling selection. For example, if we go to the intensive care unit, so that 
we and we build a model that you can use to help clinicians make decisions in the ICU. And the 
data scientists, they all start looking at the data and doing exploratory data analysis, they're not 
even aware that some patients actually don't even make it to the intensive care unit, because they 
live in rural areas, and they die before they could even come to the ICU. So now there's a bias of 
who gets into the ICU. And that's a recipe for spurious associations. So knowing exactly which page 
how patients got into the database, and where there are patients that were excluded from the 
database for because of social determinants, because of subject subjectivity in decision making, 
you have to be aware of that because the model that you build is going to be affected by those 
sampling selection bias. And you're going to find some weird associations that will be in the 
algorithm, because of the sampling selection bias. So those are perhaps the two most common 
sources of bias in a dataset. 
 
Jonathan Patscheider: 11:55

Exactly, exactly. So we don't know. So no one, no one has answers. And I think this is what makes 
this field exciting is we're realizing that this is not just throwing a bunch of features into an 
autoencoder transformer. Because right now, the way we are evaluating these models is from is 
based on accuracy. So you have real world data, is this patient going to die? And what and what's 
the real number of people who die? The problem with that is the ground truth is not fair. In the 
real world, some populations are more likely to die, and it's not explained by disease factors. So if 
accuracy is your metric, to evaluate an algorithm, that means that forever our world is going to 
have all these disparities. So we need to think of another way of evaluating this models. Because if

what your essay saying is that it should reflect what's happening in the real world, which is how

this is the biggest concern is that the algorithms that we build are just going to perpetuate 
whatever inequities that we see now. And that's the last thing we want to do, because now you 
can scale the biases. Before it's just two or three healthcare providers, we have some unconscious 
biases, and now it becomes part of the treatment algorithms. And to your question of how do we 
mitigate this? We don't know. There's one paper that got published this within this year, which I 
think is heading the right direction. And this is looking at the use of an algorithm applied on a knee 
X ray

Leo A. Celi: 14:34   
to make to decide whether they're candidates for knee surgery, so it's trying to approximate the

when they look at the algorithm, it was under estimating the pain experience of Black and 
Hispanic patients. And it turns out that the original algorithm was developed using primarily knee 
X rays of white patients. And there were some differences in the knee X rays of black patients that 
were not picked up by the algorithm, so that it wasn't giving points, for example. And for that 
reason, the estimation of what the symptoms are, is not accurate. So all they did was they 
improve the dataset, they included more knee X rays from black and Hispanics. But when they did 
that, what they found was they did not completely remove. So there were still residual disparities, 
even after diversifying the data set. So diversifying the data set is not enough. And we're doing a 
lot of experiments now. We're sort of doing adversarial attacks in the data set, like can we fool the 
algorithm to think that this person is a man rather than a woman, for example, in algorithms used 
to screen CVS, for job interviews, they tried to hide the gender. But it turns out that the computers 
can figure out just from the content, whether it's a man or a woman, and then it starts using that 
to make decisions rather than what's important. So can we change, for example, some features in 
a bank loan algorithm? What if we change the zip code of this black person to a zip code of a white 
person? And does that change the prediction of whether this person will be able to repay that 
loan? So there's active research in that area. How do you fix this? How can you provide a 
counterfactual prediction? So what will be the prediction for response to cancer treatment? If the 
world were fair, and everyone has equal access to health care? So some people would question

chemotherapy is not going to work like, and we don't know the answer to that, because we have 
not really determine what fraction of the outcome is due to social determinants. What fraction of 
the outcome is due to the biases of the doctors and the nurses.  
So there are studies showing that women who have heart attacks have better outcomes when 
their cardiologist, or heart specialists are women than men, because it turns out that men will

two types of outcomes. This is the outcome if the if the cardiologist the man is the outcome, is the

So the ultimate method, the ultimate metric, is looking at disparity. So we are, we've been trying

outcomes of which group of people are worse than the others after adjusting for illness severity. 
So what we want is that this is tied to funding of hospitals, like hospitals where they you see a lot 
of disparities in care, maybe should receive less Funding unless they do some quality improvement 
initiative to reduce that. So the ultimate way of evaluating algorithms would be to see their impact 
on the disparities dashboard. If the disparities is widening, it means that only a few patients are 
really benefiting from this early detection of deterioration, then you should probably recalibrate 
your model, because it's not helping everyone. So to us, that is the like, there's no way for you to 
preempt or predict or anticipate what would happen, it's hard to, like you could imagine the 
unintended consequences. But to me, the only way is to do due diligence and making sure that it's 
performing well across populations, you deploy it, and then you continue monitoring it. And we 
think that regulation of AI should perhaps be shared between a central organizations such as the 
FDA and the healthcare organization, because it's going to be very difficult for the FDA to oversee 
all the algorithms. Yeah, deployed.

Christian: [00:10:17] Jeg er sammen metode, vi læner os op af, så vi kopiere lidt metoden for. 
Jeg tror faktisk den vigtigste måde at komme noget af det, der er potentielle biases til livs, er at 
få lavet ordentlig ekstern validering, fordi meget det biases det opstår jo i den måde, firmaerne 
træner deres model på. Et helt konkret eksempel, det er faktisk nogen du har kørt for dine

kæmpeproblem. Altså, det er jo helt naturligt. Så ja. Så jeg tror fortsat at det jo egentlig også 
firmaernes ansvar at teste og træne deres modeller for at minimere dette potentielle biases, 
der kan være i dem. Men det er også vores ansvar at finde ud af, om der er noget bias, og det 
undersøger vi bedst ved at have et stort repræsentativt datasæt. Vi tester algoritmerne på. Ja, 
så kan vi også kigge på om der er forskel på, hvordan vi performer på mænd og kvinder, og vi 
kan også kigge på nogle andre ting.  Og som om de performerer bedre på yngre patienter end

Jonathan Patscheider: [00:15:12] Så vi kan jo leve med det. Hvad synes du så? Målet for en er,

Christian: [00:15:26] Altså jeg tænker helt klart målet, at det skal være bedre patient  
outcomes, fordi det er så meget af det her AI forskning og træning på data. Det er jo også 
noget, hvor man risikerer at altså at kompromittere noget patientsikkerheden, fordi det netop 
kræver, at man tager meget data ud. Og hvis vi laver en masse fancy i modeller og radioer ikke 
rigtigt brugte man, og det ikke gør nogen forskel for patienterne, så så synes jeg ikke, det giver 
mening. Men det er jo sådan på længere sigt den helt hårde endemål. Der synes jeg helt klart, 
det skal være bedre at afkommets for patienterne, og det kan jo for eksempel være morbidiltet 
eller mobilitet. Det kan være alle mulige konkrete ting, men det er vi ikke noget dertil i 
forskning endnu, hvor vi kan begynde at lavede store randomiserede studier, hvor vi ser på, at 
vi giver en radiolog et AI værktøj, og anden radiologi giver vi ikke et værktøj. Og så ser vi på, 
hvordan det går med patienterne afhængig af, om de er blevet beskrevet med, eller uden AI. 
Det kunne være en måde, man kunne kigge på. De der er mere hårde endepunkter. Så det er 
sådan lidt min drøm i forhold til det. Men jeg tror det der med rette, altså i modeller, uden at 
der er nogen radiologer ind over. Der er vi slet ikke endnu i forhold til, hvordan vi performer. 
Men det kan da godt være nogle år, at der kommer nogle mere eller mindre trivielle opgaver 
op, hvor man godt kan sætte en model til at køre selvstændigt. Men det er sådan lidt. Det 
kommer også an på, hvor høj risiko er, altså det udenom.

Den rigtige beslutning igen. Altså fordi at. At den ting ikke kun billeder af sted, er bare også

historien for patienten. Jeg tror, at det har en vigtig ting at sige, at man er ligesom at man er lidt 
tilbageholdende som læge ved bare at stole på noget, som man ikke har fulde i information. Og 
så tror jeg godt, at den kan være bedre til netop det der med kun at kigge på billedet og så

Som også er en relativt ideel case for piger for sådan noget med machine learning, og med der 
er slet ikke denne her patient historie. Du går bare ind som kvinde og lægger brysterne op på 
den dér mammografi, og så blev der taget et billede, og så er det det. Så det er jo egentlig bare 
det, der står der. Og så er der nogle radiologer, der sidder, siger suspekt eller ikke suspekt. Og 
de så testet faktisk en kommercielt tilgængelig mammografiscreening, algoritme på 100.000. 
Og det var alle, der havde været til screening, så det var netop konsekutivt. Virkelig flot lavet, 
og den havde præcist samme performance som radilogerne. Det er det der skal til for at man 
siger okay, den alligevel lige så god som de danske radiologer. Det kan man sige uden rigtig at 
være i tvivl, fordi det er de fuldstændig samme billeder, som de har læst, og der er ikke blevet 
taget nogen ud. Så det der med bare at kigge på billedet, det er de jo langt. Altså. Men lige så 
snart der kommer noget subjektivt ind i det, og det sådan bliver lidt mere, så tror jeg, at så er

Christian: [00:21:42] Ja, lige præcis det, der er resultater, har jo ført til, at man nu på

de har snakket om at brugen en AI model til at beskrive de, der er mammografi, betyder for de 
patienter, der laver risiko. Så det er jo ret interessant. 
 
Louis: [00:21:59] Men støttet af en læge, så var der også en læge, der kigger efter, hvad 
 
Christian: [00:22:02] Det egentlig den vej. Jeg tror, vi kommer til at se AI de næste år i 
sundhedsvæsnet, at det begynder at komme mere og mere ind. Men det skal suppleres en 
person 
 
Louis: [00:22:11] Hvordan der fandt de ud af. I det studie, er jo der sagde han jo, at der var

Fairness and the use of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning in Healthcare
February 13, 2022
10.3.4
Interview with Sune Hannibal Holm, University of Copenhagen
Page 133 of 136
==PAGE:135==
Interview with Sune Hannibal 
October 6th 
Interview Sune Hannibal, from University of Copenhage. 
Interview was conducted October 6th, at the Technical University of Denmark.  
00:00:01 
Jonathan: Jeg har skrevet nogen spørgsmål ned, men der er nok nogle af dem som overlapper 
hinanden, men så kan vi tage den derfra. Det første er om du vil fortælle lidt mere om projektet 
fordi jeg har kun kunnet læse det, som står inde på DTU, som er 1 1,5 side.  
00:00:13 
Sune: Så det her projekt, om bias and fairness i medicin, der er der to grundspekter.  Det ene 
handler om at finde nogle metoder til at finde bias i data, som man gerne vil bruge til at træne 
algoritmen. Så der er nogle bias, man måske forventer kunne være til stede, køn eller race, som er 
de sædvanlige. Men der kan jo også godt findes bias, som man ikke lige forestiller sig. Det er i 
hvert fald en hypotese. Men det er mere teknisk. Der mener jeg, at en af deres tanker er, at de vil 
forsøge at finde nogle metoder til at finde biases i data. Og udgangspunkt er data fra om 
depression fra Danmarks Statistik. Jeg tror, der er 200.000 diagnoser eller noget i den stil, de har. 
Man kan få adgang til dem, men det har været meget besværlig. Men i hvert fald så det ene 
handler om det. Men det er også en del af projektet, der ikke er kommet helt så langt. Det er fordi 
de har netop skulle bruge en masse tid på at få disse data plus, den daværende postdoc, hendes 
far blev syg. Hun var fra Indien. Hun tog tilbage. Nu er der kommet nye ind, så det er egentlig ikke 
fordi, vi er noget så voldsomt langt i forhold til planen. Men den andel af det, som jeg står for, som 
er helt uafhængig af andre. Det handler om mere de etiske aspekter ved at anvende algoritmer til 
at klassificere individer, og bruge de klassifikationer som grundlag for beslutninger. Og der kan 
man sige, at et af de centrale områder er selvsagt inden for medicin. Men det er jo noget, der går 
igen i andre områder og de her udfordringer Og hvad kan man sige det, det der har været mit 
udgangspunkt, det jeg kigget klart mest på indtil videre, det er, hvad man stiller op med det her 
forhold, at hvis du har to grupper sorte og hvide. Du har sikkert læst Compass casen. Compass er 
sådan en case og den problematik, den har især givet anledning til det her med, at forskellige mål 
for hvor godt en algoritme klarer sig på performancemæssigt, altså falsk, positiv og negativ alt det 
her. Der kan man ikke rigtig på lige performance for forskellige grupper, hvis de har forskellige 
base rates af den egenskab, man gerne vil forudsige. Det er grundlæggende en udfordring, fordi at 
i det øjeblik du som designer er en algoritme, er nødsaget til at enten uden at tænke over det eller 
eksplicit og tænke over det, at tage nogle beslutninger om hvad er det denne algoritme skal gøre i 
forhold til de her grupper? Skal den sikre, at der er lige god prædikativ value? Eller skal den sikre 
lige error rates eller noget i den stil.  Du kan ikke få det hele. Så er der problemstillinger involveret, 
fordi det har en impact på, hvordan folk i, hvordan de her grupper faktisk får udbytte af den her 
algoritme.  
 
00:03:44 
Jonathan: Ja, og i forlængelse af det, hvad kan man stille op?  
 
00:03:57 
Sune: Ja, så det er de forskellige måder der er. Det er sådan set mere en anden måde at sige det 
samme på, at man kan. Denne terminologi på dette område er fuldstændig i øst og vest, fordi alle 
mulige bruger alle mulige forskellige begreber. Independe, sufficency og separation. De tre, der så 
bliver sat i spil tit. Og det er sådan set jo forsøg på at sige, jamen altså, hvis vi forsøger at opnå så 
==PAGE:136==
Interview with Sune Hannibal 
October 6th 
suffiency, så sørger vi for at algoritmen… Lad os sige den spytter en risiko scorer ud for hvorvidt du 
har en eller anden sygdom eller ej. Eller hvad du nu vil forudsige og når en sort person for et 5 tal 
og et en hvid person for et 5 tal af denne risiko scorer. Så skal det helst være således at de der 
risikoscorer er lige nøjagtige. Det vil sige uanset om du er sort eller hvid. Det vil sige at 5 ud af 10 
sorte der får et 5 tal, de har egenskaben og 5 ud af ti hvide der får når, de har egenskaben. Således 
at når du giver en person et 5 tal, så er 5 tallets betydning ikke relateret til om de tilhører den ene 
eller den anden.  
Hvis ikke du har det, så er alt algoritmen bare ikke lige god til at forudsige, fordi folk er forskellige 
køn eller grupper i det hele taget. Og det er klart, hvis du sidder som som læge og får spyttet den 
risiko score ud for et individ. Lad os sige, du kommer til lægen og blive bekymret for, at du har en 
eller anden sygdom, og så kører de dig igennem sådan en algoritme. Det kan være hudkræft for 
eksempel. Og så siger den, du falder i kategori otte, så det i højere risiko. Den vil sige, den 
klassificerer dig som én, der har hudkræft. Men du er mand, og du er hvid. Hvis det var en sort 
mand på samme alder og det hele, der kom en, der fik et ottetal, så ville lægen skulle tænkte: ”Det 
svarer jo egentlig til et 6 tal for en hvid eller noget i den stil.”. Det er jo noget rod, fordi hvis du 
sætter en bestemt grænse, for dit threshold, for hvornår den siger højere risiko eller lavere risiko, 
så vil det threshold jo ikke have samme betydning for de to grupper. Fordi nogen, der i bund og 
grund er lige individer. For invidivider, hvor det lige risikabelt, at de har en sygdom eller ej. De vil 
falde på hver sin side af thresholden eller kan gøre det alt efter hvilken gruppe de tilhører. Så det 
er dér, hvor man får de forskelle i predictive value. Det var den der Compas sag var jo et eksempel 
på, at dem der havde lavet, compass, sagde, at den er kalibreret som de siger, den sørger for…. 
Hvis vi er betinget af, at en sort får en positiv, prediktion, der siger, at vedkommende vil fortage ny 
kriminalitet. Så er der den samme sandsynlighed for, at det faktisk er rigtigt, som hvis en hvid får 
en positiv prediktion, for at det er rigtigt. Og de der publica folk, deres anke… Det er den der 
suffiency. Det er dem, der hævder, at der skal være lige stor sandsynlighed for at givet at du får en 
positiv reaktion, at du så også har. Det er den korte version af det. Og tilsvarende med negative 
reaktioner e det vigtigt. Det er begge dele. Den anden er separation. Det er så at sige det er ikke 
betinget af at du får en positiv prædiktion, at der skal være lige stor sandsynlighed for, at det 
faktisk er rigtigt, uanset hvilken gruppe du tilhører. Det skal være således at gide du har, den 
egenskab vi forsøger at predicte. Så skal der være lige stor sandsynlighed for, at prædiktionen er 
korrekt. Så man vender sådan set dem, der konditionelle sandsynlighed, bliver byttet om. Hvor jeg 
den ene part siger, at det er betinget af, at I har samme prædiktion, at der skal være lige stor 
sandsynlighed for, at I har egenskaben. Så siger de andre nej, det betinget, at I har den samme 
egenskab, at der skal være lige stor sandsynlighed for, at prædiktionen er rigtig. Og det er der så 
en masse diskussioner om.  Og Independence det svarer lidt mere til, at der bare er samme 
sandsynlighed for at den rigtige. Altså, jeg tror det mere statistiske parity. Altså at den samme 
proportion af sorte eller hvide der får, der har den rigtige prædiktionen af. Men jeg, dem har jeg 
ikke kigget så meget på, der er kommet diskussionen der, men den er selvfølgelig også en spiller i 
det her. Men det, der er ved den anden der, det er også er interessant, også fordi det, der så 
bliver. Det etiske spørgsmål er så, givet at vi ikke kan få det hele, hvad gør vi så, hvis vi mener, at vi 
egentlig burde have det hele? At allgoritmen både burde tilfredsstille suffiency og separation, og 
det kan den ikke. Hvad skal vi så gøre? Og det tror jeg, at der er en ret stor uklarhed om i bund og 
grund, og der vil være en uklarhed om, hvordan skal vi finde ud af, hvad vi skal gøre?  
Vi kan jo ikke lave det her, uden at vi gør et eller andet, fordi det tallene falder jo falder ud på en 
given måde. Sådan er det bare. Men hvis vi ville lave om på den, hvilken vej skal vi så lave om? 
==PAGE:137==
Interview with Sune Hannibal 
October 6th 
Hvad er det rigtige at gøre? Det er det, der helt grundlæggende er en stor udfordring for folk, der 
vil designe en fair algoritme. Når man sidder i dette dilemma, at hvis vi skaber deres suffiency,  så 
er der ikke separation. Altså hvad så? Det jeg har forsøgt at gøre det videre, det er sådan set 
måske at forsøge at få sat den dér diskussion ind i en moral, teoretisk ramme. For mig handler 
denne diskussion i høj grad om, hvad er en fair distribution af mis klassifikationen af sådan en 
algoritme?  
Og en anden ting i det der er vigtigt, synes jeg, at tage med, det jo ikke mit… Det er der også folk, 
der er lidt uenige om, men som udgangspunkt dér, hvor det bliver etisk relevant, det er jo, at den 
dér klassifikation, den har konsekvenser for distributionen af et gode. Om det er tilbud om 
behandlingen eller tilbud om at undgå behandling for de behandlinger, kan jo også være ret 
anstrengende. Især hvis der ikke er grund til det. Men i bund og grund er der jo tale om 
distribution, af en eller form for gode. Derfor begynder vi at tale om fairness. Der er en forskel på, 
hvem der får hvad og hvilke grupper der er, hvilke grupper der får mest, og hvilke grupper der får 
mindst. Der begynder at lave de der, og det er jo ikke et nyt problem. Så inden for politisk filosofi 
har man jo altid diskuteret, hvordan skal goderne fordeles retfærdigt. Så der er jo noget teori, og 
en ramme man kan forsøge at diskutere det inden for. Det har jeg forsøgt, og forsøgt indtil videre 
at analyserer de dér to separation og så suffiency, og kigge lidt på, hvad ville være så den 
filosofiske ræsonnement eller rationale for at acceptere en af dem. Så kan det jo være man kan, 
om ikke andet forholde dem som applikationer af en moralsk teori. Og så har vi ligesom, hvis vi 
kan gøre det, så har vi også en lang historie, hvor man har diskuteret fordele og ulemper ved disse 
moralske teorier. Og det jeg har kigget meget på, er sådan set, at de jo begge to går meget op i, at 
der er lighed mellem grupper i forhold til et eller andet. Så helt grundlæggende for der fairness 
problematik er der jo en bekymring om ulighed mellem grupper, i forhold til hvad de zfår ud af 
det. Og det er der sådan nogle teorier filosofiske altså ikke er lige til. Rigstyske teorier raser. 
Diskussionen af fiskevande accepterer igen teoretiske teorier eller ej. Og hvad er der?
